BEFORE THE MIRROR
By “JEANNETTE.'’
These are, undoubtedly, days of exceptional nervous strain for one and
all of us. Anxiety, with its beauty-destroying influence, is casting its shadow
over the lives of many of us women, and while perhaps a few —the more
stoical among us —can avoid worrying over past or prospective events, we
may all, with a little care and forethought, succeed in preventing the result
of worry from being noticeable in our appearance. To these fortunate
few this little chat will, however, be as interesting and instructive as to
those who stand in real need of help in toilet mattersj and may .be the
means of bringing to their notice some hitherto unknown method of retain-
ing or regaining that beauty of face and iigure which is the birthright of
every! woman. Some of the ingredients mentioned below are, at present, not
generally knownsto the public, but any good chemist will usually be found to
have a small quantity in stock.
Freckles and How to Cure Them.—lt is always
those with the finest and fairiest skins who
freckle most easily, but this knowledge is poor
comfort when the face is disfigured with these
ugly little blemishes. To prevent the forma-
tion of freckles a wash should be applied to
the face which will form a shield from the
rays of the sun. A simple and inexpensive one
can be made up at home by dissolving an
ounce of cleminite in four tablespoonfuls of
hot water. Shake the bottle, and when cold
apply to the face and allow to dry on 'the
skin. If this is 'done every day. renewing the
application whenever the face is washed, and
a little mercolised wax rubbed into the skin
every night, freckles will not make their
appearance, no matter how hot the sun. To
remove freckles which have already formed,
apply the wax every night, rub it well into
the skin of the face and neck and leave it on
till the morning. Then wash off, using Pilenta
soap and warm water.
To Reduce a Double Chin.—To reduce a double
chin, give hard, firm massage every night with
mercolised wax, stroking the flesh firmly back-
wards from the chin towards the lower por-
tion of the neck. Do this every night, and in
the morning bathe with cold water and rub
into the skin a little parsidium jelly. This
treatment will strengthen the flabby tissues
and restore the contour of the face. Occasion-
ally a double chin Is due to stooping over
books or work, but generally It Is the result
of putting on weight. When the latter is the
case a-few clynol berries, eaten regularly every
day, will greatly assist in reducing the super-
fluons flesh. A glass of hot water should he*
sipped after each meal.
The Hair—To Increase the Growth.—To arrest
an undue falling of the hair and increase the
growth, scalp-massage and the application of
a good tonic is absolutely necessary. Part the
hair in the centre, and. starting at the fore-
head. massage for at least ten minutes. Then
apply a tonic. A simple and inexpensive one
can be made up at home by mixing one ounce
of boraniuni (which can he bought at the
chemist’s! with a quarter of a pint; of bay
rum. Add the boranlum to the bay rum. allow
to stand for half an hour, then strain aud add
sufficient water to make half a pint. Dal)
amongst the roots of the hair with a soft
sponge.
Superfluous Hair.—Dark-complexioned women
are far more frequently troubled with a
growth of superfluous hair than those with
fair skins, and at the first sign of these objec-
tionable hairs steps should he taken to remove
them, or they will develop into a strong growth
which will take a very long time to destroy.
A little powdered pbeminoi applied to this
growth will remove the unsightly blemish,
destroying it permanently If the hairs be but
few. Two or more applications may be neces-
sary, if the growth bo unusually strong, but
about one ounce of pbeminoi should he suffici-
ent for tiie most stubborn case. Pbeminoi can
be obtained at the chemist’s, and a little
powdered alum Rhould he obtained at. the
same time for dusting the skin before applying
the pbeminoi.
ANSWERS TO CORRESPONDENTS.
TO DARKEN THE EYEBROWS (L.M.). —
There is no dye which I could recommend as
“safe,” but if you rub a little mennaline into'
the eyebrows and at the roots of the eyelashes
regularly every night it will darken them
naturally, and will also increase the growth
of the eyelashes.
TO REDUCE WEIGHT (SADIE).—Yes, your
weight and measurements are certainly more
than they should be for your height, and I
quite agree that being so Stout makes you look
old. I am pleased to be able to tell you of a
quick, easy and pleasant way, to reduce both.
Get a small quantity of clynol berries from
(he chemist’s and take one after each meal,
three times a day. Weigh yourself after one
month’s treatment, and you will be delighted
with the result.
TO KEEP THE' HAIR PAIR (DORIS).—If
you shampoo your hair regularly wdth stallax
granules it will prevent it from turning dark.
One teaspoon of stallax is sufficient for a
shampoo, and an original packet will make
from 25 to 30 shampoos. Stallax keeps
indefinitely. [4
TO IMPROVE A DUEL, MUDDY COM
PLEXION (D.B.J.— Your complexion is dull
and muddy-looking because it needs thorough
cleansing and renewing of the outer cuticle.
To do this get a tin of inercolised wax, and
with the tips of the fingers rub a little gently
into the skin, going over the entire face and
neck. Leave the wax on all night and in the
morning wash it off with a good soap (you
will find Pilenta excellent). In a month’s time
you will notice a most surprising improve-
ment. (2) Liquid pergol will prevent the
excessive perspiration of winch you complain.
Nothing can be done.
TO ARREST GRLYNESS (FLO).— You are
indeed far too young to go grey yet. Get
about two ounces of concentrate of tamma-
lite and mix it with three ounces of hay rum.
Dab this on the hair and amongst the roots.
It will soon make matters all right,
TO WHITEN A BROWN NECK (W.K.).—To
whiten .your neck use jettaline. Get a tube of
this from the chemist’s and rub a little well
into the skin of the neck; leave it on till next
morning. Do this regularly every night for
three or four weeks and you will find that the
skin will become beautifully clear and white.

never scratches. It is a model of simplicity. IT The Onoto is

The Red Page
War Pabulum for Writers
Well, the war’s over ; and now let us see
what use it has been to literature. Because
if it hasn't done anything for the poets and
the storytellers we’ll have to abolish wars
as mere haphazard murderers of embryo
Lawsons and Bellerives. Among the millipns
of young men wiped out there must have
been thousands of Kiplings and several Leon
Gellerts. There remains the compensation
in the thought of the battalions of future
Hall Caines and Charles Garbages who were
destroyed before they had a chance to become
virulent; and among them there must have
been •hundreds of potential fathers of Ella
Wheeler Wilcoxes. And it is probable that
the number of future Australian poets
destroved is equalled by the number pf news-
papers editors who, if they had survived,
would have had to read their verses. So on
the destructive side the war holds the balance
even. Nature knows what she is about.
On the war’s credit side must be placed,
first, the restoration of romance. Early in
1914 it was recognised that mankind suf-
fered from a world-shortage of story plots
and movie-scenarios. The huge increase in
the output of paper, pens, ink, erasers, car-
bon-ribbons and blotting-pads had outrun the
supply of stories and poems. The world
was rapidly running out of plots. All plots
based on wars had been used up, and then
used over again. The American Civil War
had been so pawed over by novelists and
photo-playwriters that it was beginning to
fray at the edges. The Franco-Prussian War
was full of holes, out of which plots had been
stolen. The Balkan Wars were too confus-
ing for anybody to write or read about. And
so novelists were forced to invent wars of
their own in Ruritania and Xcnda ; and 11.
jpr. Wells had to fight all his campaigns in
the future. The Boer War proved merely a
bore. Kipling, finding no new wars to write
about, smashed his'fountain-pen.
To this plotless world the war came as a
welcome gift. This war was different to all
other wars ; and it affofded chances of plots
that had never been conceived of before. It
just wanted a few changes in background
and uniforms, and all the American Civil
Wars could be used over again. At first the
novelists feared that scientific warfare would
upset all the old sentimentalities on which
fiction is built; but they needn’t have
worried. The war killed one sort of
romance; but it invented another. At first
sight there wasn’t much chance of individual
heroes in that war of masses, and the stag-
nation of trench-life seemed fatal to heroics.
Yet even trench-warfare brought forth the
old pirate-chief and Red-Indian motif that
will never die. The trench raid revived the
Red Indian on the warpath. It caused more
exciting moments and reached a more crash-
ing climax than the best yarn ever written
about baseball.
If only for the spy yarn and the detective
story the war was worth while. The Ger-
man secret service and the German female
spy, such as Mata-llari, will yet make a
dozen best-sellers. Rasputin’s heirs would
draw fabulous royalties from playwrights
and film-carpenters if there was a copyright
law in Bolshevik-land. And the war con-
ferred its greatest gift upon future genera-
tions of English novelists by inventing' the
Anzac. And for Australian writers there is
the supreme discovery of Billjim. He ought
to be good for a thousand years yet as hero
and bloke.
Though there were thousands of individual
“stunts,” worth at least a one-reel scenario
or a short-story, there were several environ-
ments that seemed to breed sensational plots
and romances. When Russia broke up into
chaos there were romances and heroisms
that exceed any novelist’s invention. One
such needs to be documented some day. Its
plain facts, as told to me by a casual Fmzed,
out-Zenda Zenda. He was with a British
force in Russia when the big smash came.
His C.O. summoned him one day, introduced
him to an American girl who was stranded
in Russia, and ordered him to get her out
of Russia. That was all. He managed, after
adventures that Thomas Griffiths would pay
huge royalties for, to get that girl to Vladi-
vostoc-k across a seething continent. And he
didn’t fall in love with her, nor she with
him ; though their track across Siberia was
marked with dead men shot by the Maori-
lander to save her life.
Escape by English and Australian prison-
ers of war from German prisons to Holland
forms another fertile depot of sensational
plots. A series of these incredible adven-
tures is running in Blackwood: and no
best-seller or American drama could touch
them for thrills and climaxes. And there are
more thrills to come; for the authors could
not give details of the way they managed to
get out of German gaols without implicating
others or giving the show away to the Huns.
Now these details, the most interesting
parts of these true stories, will be printed,
and the Hun gaolers will kick themselves in
the same places once again.
Another department for the provision of
thrills for the tired business man is in the
new methods of scientific war. Into the
sensational novelist’s lap falls aeroplanes,
submarines, tanks. Nothing that any novel-
ist from Job to Oliver Onions ever invented
could be more exciting than the play-outs of
these “mystery V.C.’s.” And for pure ber-
sG'kism there are points about the Zeebrugge
show.
But all these themes for novels will need,
digesting. The important fact is that they
are there, ready to be dug up out of official
documents and worked over when they are
needed for their real purposes, to enable a
poet or novelist of the future to earn a crust.
Already, however, the war shows some
results; it is beginning to pay dividends to
literature. The most important credit item
is Henri Barbusse’s TJnder Fire. That
masterpiece alone justifies the war. It is a
terrible thought that if the war hadn’t hap-
pened along, Under Fire would never have
existed. Let us give thanks that the ways
of mysterious Providence are so wise. It
sent the war to inspire Henri Barbusse.
Other important items to set off against
the war-debt of the world are Phillip Gibb’s
(Continued on page 26.)

Adamax Roofing: “The Best
Roofing Money Can Buy.”
J udged by users all over Australia it has proved itself the
best roofing for Australia’s climatic conditions, and the
best quality roofing of its type. On Factories, Bungalows,
Garages, Mine Buildings, Farm Buildings, Wool and
Grain Sheds—everywhere it has made a name for its
unfailing quality.
j ASPHALTUM ROOFING
is most economical in every way. First cost is only one-tliird tliUt of
galvanised-iron with a similar saving in freightthe cost of laying it is very
small, as any handy man can do the job. Adamax upkeep cost is practically nil.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Song of the Dial.
“Horas non numero nisi serenas.”
The Dial faced the summer sun,
The garden blossomed all around ;
If happiness could bless a scene
I felt that here was holy ground ;
Afar I heard the chime of bells
And caught a glimpse of gleaming towers
And all the while the Dial sang
Until the dell with echoes rang:
“I only count the shining hours.”
The. roses nodding gaily nigh
Would wave and wave and call to me;
The jasmine blooms above my head
Would nid and nod in careless glee;
Was not a speck in all the sky
Save one white cloud above the bowers,
And all the while the Dial sang
Till all my soul with music rang:
“I only count the shining hours!”
And flashing out and flashing in
Amid wisteria blossoms gay,
Would glance at times the golden locks
Of one sweet girl at joyous play ;
And when one whispered “Time the swift
Is Lord of Youth and all the Powers,”
A burst of silv’ry laughter rang:
“I sing the song the Dial sang—
‘l only count the shining hours.’ ”
And as the years go fleeting by
And locks of brown are flecked with gray,
And shadows loom across the rim
Of what was once a Perfect Day,
There swings a cadence through my brain,
A cadence born of sun and flowers
When all the dell enchanted rang
With that dear song the Dial sang:
“I only count the shining hours.”
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
Rendezvous.
This is the hour of fulfilment; the time and
place of our meeting,
Roses red in the garden, sunset red in the
west;
Sit by my side, old friend, and give me your
hand in greeting,
Here where the journey is ending, old ways
and old friends are best.
This was the pact we made when the bells
of youth were ringing
Down in the fragrant meadows a-shine with
the cape-flower’s gold ;
You said: “I will wander away and wake
the world with my singing!”
I said: “I will make a garden to rest in
when we grow old!”
You have won to the far-off heights, your
songs are a living story,
Wafted o’er shining waters, blown round the
world as a flame;
They have wakened men to love, or led
them to deathless glory,
And the little children lisp them, and earth
is glad at your name.
And I—l have made the garden and decked it
for your returning;
Year after year the roses have blazoned and
died away,
And the tall gums gazed to seaward where the
sunset fires were burning,
Waving their arms in greeting to you who
would come one day.
Babes have grown with the blossoms, and
played in the scented clover;
Age has found calm and solace and rest as
the long years pass;
And there where the jasmine clusters, many
a lass and lover
Have marked the bright hours fleeting by the
sundial set in the grass.
But all was fashioned for you, for the time
and place of our meeting
(See, how the gold grows silver from the
moonrise over the hill!)
So sit by my side, old friend, and give me
your hand in greeting,
For here is the peace of evening and the
tides of the world are still.
London.
A. M. BOWYER-ROSMAN.
Mater Dolorosa.
In the field of death I sought him. Oh, the
curse on him was sore!
Sweat of blood and tears my portion, but
his?....to die....to die!
O bruised reed waiting for the wind to reap
as it blew by;
O smoking flax awaiting to be quenched for
evermore!
In the field of death I sought him, I all sad
and sorrow-shod;
Before my eyes a vision, and within my heart
a flame;
And the smoking flax was quickened, and the
broken reed became
The Miracle—the mystery of Aaron’s bud-
ding rod.
In the field of life I found him! Oh, very
joy of breath!
On my eternal wheel the potter’s clay of his
desire
I have graved with cunning purpose, I have
moulded as with fire.
I have shapen to Divinity, and conquest over
Death !
VICTOBIA.
MARIE E. J. PITT.

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 2G, 1918.
CHRISTMAS, 1918.
A couple of thousand years ago a section
of the human race—the palest section, which
was also the most restless and most ambi-
tious —wanted many things, but three in
particular. The three were a new means
of preserving and diffusing knowledge (the
printing-press) ; a new force (steam and its
brother, electricity) to multiply power a
thousandfold; and a new and more precise
faith to fill in the blank at the end of life.
It didn’t know that it had wanted these
things till it got them; then it knew. The
faith came first, for the manufacture of
creeds was an old industry; and it held its
own for various reasons, one being that it
was fairly definite, while previous religions
were hazy. It promised, though not so posi-
tively as one might wish, a brilliant and
eternal future life which all men could
attain to, while its rivals either promised
nothing or promised very little. And it
wasn’t required to prove its theory of life
after death by results, as the steam-engine
and the printing-press had to do. The pro-
mise that existed at Christmas 33 exists at
Christmas, 1918, and in all that time no
dead person has come back to report whether
the assurance rests on any basis. If any-
body said he was the returned traveller
with the report he wouldn’t be believed ex-
cept by the very foolish, just as happened
to Dowie, who said he was Elijah. Chris-
tianity threw a vast amount of deck cargo
overboard as premature and unsuited to the
times before it had fairly got the anchor up.
Its doctrine of non-resistance went early,
and it became (he first real fighting faith,
spreading itself by the sword; offering the
heathen the choice of sincere belief or death
with Sheol to follow after ; and smiting with
(he strong arm and the high hand all people
who didn’t part the hair of their orthodoxy
exactly in the middle. In fact, it invented
heresy—the mild Greek and Rofuan fMgtms
knew neither' the nit me hor tne* idea : and it
invented the thing as early as eight years
after the Crucifixion. Thus it provided a
fresh excuse for war. as if there weren't
excuses enough before. The idea of human
equality was exchanged for complete sub-
servience to kings and prelates, who
wouldn't lend their power for the spread of
the gospel of equality on any other terms.
The eternal life which was to be attained
by righteousness was replaced by one that
could be acquired by formulas and cash, and
by subservience to the Church and its part-
ner the monarch. Hence arose almost every-
thing, including the first Pope and the last
Hohenzollern. also the Council of Trent,
the Solemn League and Covenant, Brigham
Young and the Augsburg Confession. The
ship that was launched was a humble un-
armed craft painted white, but the one that
actually got out of the harbor was a dread-
nought with a heavily-armed crew, and a
grudge against everybody save itself.
The new faith had a driving force of its
own. The happy-go-lucky myths of the
nations with whom it first came in contact
had no ambition to spread over the earth.
Many of them hadn't even an ambition to
spread over their own country. Jupiter
was the official god of Rome, but he was
content to be ollicial until his existence was
threatened, and clods and insults were
aimed at him. The Hebrews, who might
have been in the race, almost regarded the
conversion of outsiders as the sharing of a
rich spiritual property with undeserving
strangers. So when a small creed came
along with a fixed resolve to save everybody
even if it lost itself in the process—a creed
with the new big gun of eternal damnation
to shoot with —it was like Cortez with his
ironclad army of two men and a horse fall-
ing on nude Mexico. The unorganised reli-
gionists who didn’t care went down before
the organised ones that cared a great deal,
especially when monarchy began to realise
that the organised church was a good stand-
ing army and began to use it as such. Later
the Mohammedan took a leaf out of the
Christian’s book, so there were two mission-
ary creeds, missioning in similar style. The
printing-press and steam power helped to
decide the issue. The Christian races hap-
pened also to ho the mechanical races.
There is no Buddhist, Mohammedan or Con-
fncian press that can turn out religious and
other literature at a fraction of the pace
which in leading Christian countries is a
matter of course, and the blank idolaters
have no press. Also, thanks to steam and
electricity, the Christian can get about
faster than the Mohammedan, and can do
ten miles to the pagan’s one—and speed
counts.
* * * ft
The faith passed its infant days amid a
maze of difficulties. It tried, in a vague
sort of way, to be a continuation or develop-
ment of Hebrewism, but the Hebrew re-
fused to be continued or developed save by
his own consent. It endeavored to annex
the Old Testament, which was the history,
poetry, legend, moral code and water and
sewerage regulation of a small particularist
people, written in a difficult local language,
the idea being to use it as a preface to a
book in a different language, which taught a
widely-different code, had a cosmopolitan out-
look, and had no vestige of the old intense
local patriotism. With few exceptions, the
owners of the old book furiously declined to
be a preface. They said many things—
among others, that they were the men on the
spot and could swear that the miracles
never happened. They refused to lend the
line of brief little kings of which they were
so inordinately proud to make a lineage for
any unorthodox prophet. They denied the
Saviour’s descent from David, the Hebrew
emperor: and He, being a pure democrat with
a soul above any craving for relationship
with the bloodstained emperor, made no
claim to royal descent. He might have been
more popular if He had acted otherwise;
blank indifference to the important David
was unpardonable. Even the patchwork
chroniclers who tried after His passing to
tie together two stories that wouldn’t assimi-
late —a tuft-hunting folk who couldn’t get
away from monarchical traditions —weak-
ened on this business, and ended the alleged
pedigree with Joseph of Nazareth, a mere
stepfather of the Redeemer. It remains so
to this day, so that Ruth the Moabitess,
David, Solomon and all the other persons
enumerated have no more to do with the case
than if they were a list of Druids or ruins.
The New Testament creed was so utterly
rejected in the place where all its evidences
were said to lie that when a bustling, feverish
theologian, who had apparently never even
met the Founder of the Faith, became the
biggest contributor to the New Testament he
wrote in a European language and mostly to
European peoples. Letters were addressed
by him to Rome and Corinth and Salonika
and to Greek cities in Asia Minor, but not
even a postcard to Jerusalem, though there
were a few words to Hebrews in general.
Rome began to be the . centre of the Church
even in Paul’s day. But any fight to a
finish between the case for the Old Testa-
ment and that for the New was prevented by
outside interference. An army which wor-
shipped Jupiter and sundries levelled the
Old Testament fortress-city and dispersed
the synagogue into far countries. Thereby
Jupiter destroyed a possible ally of value,
and, it may be, committed suicide.
* ft ft *
The breakage of the Jews left no strong
organised spiritual force to argue with the
Church in the Mediterranean countries—
only hulking, good-matured myths, which?
objected to it on account of its language,;'
its politics and its table manners. JbaflQicffc
in any serious spirit. This smoothed' Tspi&F
difficulties, but there were plenty left.' The
Church wasn’t really founded on the New
Testament. as many people suppose. IF
founded itself on personal recollections,
hearsay, rumor and common or uncommon
report—possibly to some extent on
lime's persons ~WhT> ; ' didn't use
shorthand. It wrote its own book of guid-
ance as it went along, and it was none too
well served by its writers. The four Evan-
gelists didn’t even profess to have been pre-
sent at any of the events they described.
They didn’t speak with conviction in the
first: person, and they gave neither authori-
.MM r H
Acts is a scrappy record of visits to
churches already founded. The Epistles are
mostly letters to churches already in exist-
ence. They are sermons of varying degrees
of merit selected presumably out of multi-
tudes of sermons, and based on truths which
the Founder of the Faith set forth much
better. Still they are fairly coherent, while
the Evangelists lack order and conviction
and, above all. corroboration.
Thus in their word-picture we see Pilate
as a small bureaucrat, a cog in a vast Gov-
ernment machine, the emperor’s plodding
deputy (at least he plods in the mind’s eye),
dominating a little province, presumably
taking note of things and reporting, like
any other bureaucrat, to headquarters. Also
we see Herod, known as a man of brains
and character, higher in the scale than
Pilate the plodder, and a definite figure in
history where Pontius is rather doubtful.
In their narrow domain, under their very
eyes, a Prophet is said to have ranged for
six years, preaching a perfectly new faith,
raising the dead (a quite unusual thing),
giving sight to the blind, healing innumer-
able sick folk and cripples, distributing an
infallible cure for leprosy, allaying storms
and feeding multitudes with improvised vic-
tuals. Yet. when lie was brought before
them they give the impression of having
never heard of Him, and Pilate enters on
the case as if it were part of the ordinary
day's work. The amazing reports which
the office-holder should have sent to Rome
about the splitting open of graves on the
first Good Friday, and the rising of dead
men, who walked past the very windows of
Government House, haven’t left a spot of ink
on the records of the time. When Paul
went to Athens —a restless, refined, in-
quisitive afternoon-tea and la’te-hour city
of art and letters and dandies and fashions
and gossip-hunters—it was much the
same. Apparently no Greek in all Greek-
speaking Lesser Asia had written to his
brother PYGMALION at home about the rais-
ing of the dead, so that he might read the
letter to Cynisca and Galatea over the
breakfast egg and coffee. Paul, in fact,
had to carry the belated information himself.
The wonderful and very upsetting device of
Augustus, by which everybody, even a
woman on the verge of maternity, plodded
by slow locomotion to his or her native
town, however remote, to pay his or her
income-tax can’t, be traced in the records
of the mean, suspicious potentate with the
home-made clothes and the perpetual
snuffles. Yet it should have been almost as
disturbing as a Crusade, and it should have
brought about the prompt assassination of a
potentate with an even worse cold in his
head than Augustus. The special and won-
derful darkness which is said to have fallen
on all the world for three forenoon hours
on Crucifixion day hasn’t been found in any
secular writings, though it should have
attracted the eye of Petronius Arbiter
and his friends. It has been explained even
by clerics as a common or garden eclipse.
And the story of the days after the Resur-
rection is a tale of a few sporadic appear-
ances by a Figure which no one recognised
at sight, and which even a devoted friend
took for that of a gardener.
All this, and much more, have failed to
kill the idea at the root of Christianity. It
has survived even its worst friends and its
worst chroniclers. Its basis is the teaching
of the truest gentleman and most unselfish
reformer of whom the world has record—an
older and greater Bayard, without fear and
without reproach. It is not the creed that is
amiss, but the ballast and encumbrances it
has to carry. The Old Testament should,
perhaps, be sent adrift by the cutting of that
connecting link or towline—the pedigree
which has Adam of the Euphrates at the
beginning, and David of Jerusalem in the
middle, and which professes to end. but
doesn’t, with Jesus of Nazareth. The Old
Testament is quite capable of floating on its
own merits as the remarkable Sacred Book
of the most virile and progressive of all non-
Christian races. Christianity needn’t lose
the previous volume, but it needn't tow it
behind any more than it need shove the sub-
sequent Koran in front. A large section of
the New Testament should be marked as
open to criticism, especially the four quasi-
historical books written by men who don’t
even profess to be first-hand witnesses. They
tried to cut the garment of Christianity out
of the old _ cloth of Hebrewism, and caused
bitter recrimination between the large new
wine that couldn’t fit into the tight old bottle
and the proud old bottle which said the new
wine wrns an upstart and “no class.” They
decked their narrative with strange fancies
such as might be expected of an imaginative
community which, because of paper shortage
and other things, was accustomed to rely on
village gossip, old wives’ tales, and the recol-
lections of the venerable inhabitant. But
the words of the Teacher himself are so
lofty, impressive and imperishable that even
this literary ballast doesn’t sink them. They
may be impracticable so far as the world has
got—it has been said that any State which
based its Government upon the Sermon on
the Mount wouldn't last an hour after the
adjacent Powers had assured themselves that
it wasn’t really trying to beguile them within
reach of a new explosive. But that doesn’t
make the Sermon any less!'a priceless ifleal.
And there is always a hope that it may some
day be more than an idea!.-.\The foundation

are reviving. The Hun, even with the

Arrangements ai - e being made—not by tin

which regard every enterprise as a device foi
buying the goodwill of electors with easy anc
well-paid employment, but by private effort—
for inaugurating an air service between Aus-
tralia and England. The object in this cast
is to fly, and that alone—not to fly as a fifth
consideration, while the first, second, third
and fourth considerations are to organise the
Human Birds’ T’nion with a special Court t(

Australia to attempt Something out of its
ordinary rut—not the purchase of mone
billiard-tables for postal employees, oi
another huge consignment of flower-pots for
the gardens at. Canberra, or another public
wheat farm at Tottenham, where loaf-
material was produced at the rate of about
Is. Gd. per loaf, but some plain business.
A point to be considered is that if Aus-
tralia is railroaded in the same degree as is
considered necessary in the United States it
will have about 250,000 miles of line. As
the U.S. has 26,410 miles of river navigable
for large vessels, while Australia certainly
hasn’t, this country is even more dependent
than Uncle Sam’s land on dry means of
transit. Australia has 22,000 miles of rail-
way built, so if affairs proceed in the old
way it has 228,000 to build. Therefore it
has 228,000 possible mistakes to avoid by
finding out early whether the iron way is the
carrier of the future, or is spoil to be a
superannuated encumbrance. (This is taking
no account of the fact that 17,000 miles of
line require to he reconstructed on account of
the break-of-gauge blunder.) Australia has
spent some £220,000,000 which may require
to be written oil' as lost, but there is time to
save the other £2,280,000,000 which might
be lost (saying nothing of all the millions
for reconstruction) but which isn’t gone yet.
Countries like England, France, Holland,
Denmark and tin' United States have no
special need for haste or wakefulness over
this matter. If the old locomotive device is
going to be a failure after a bare century
of superiority they have by this time made
about all the failure and all the bad debts
that they can make. But Australia is little
more than beginning a career which is
already a very doubtful kind of career. It
has a chance to think provided it thinks
promptly and scientifically and doesn’t begin
by appointing its seven Premiers’ rag-tag
and bobtail acquaintances as a committee.
* * * *
Air service means having a movable or
portable civilisation instead of one that is
chained down like a convict during a life-
sentence. Therefore a country which can
practically begin under such a system—and
Australia is only beginning—will have a
new outlook. Hitherto man has made a
settlement for good or inferior reasons, and
the making of the caravan track, and still
more the solid road, and most of all the
railway, has clamped it down and clamped
him down also. To change his mind is
ruinous. A city like Melbourne is anchored
by many million pounds’-worth of railway.
No rival can get a fair start even if it has
greater natural advantages unless it also gets
many million pounds’-worth of railway, and
that is a great deal to gamble on a new ven-
ture. If the new rival succeeds, its competi-
tion may depreciate the old congestion of
railway, and in so doing depreciate the ridi-
lous land values which are caused by the
congestion of people brought about by the
congestion of railway. Land of very little
agricultural value was recently sold in Mel-
bourne for £IOOO per foot, which is a case
in point. That is to say, the location of the
railways brings the people; the people create
the £IOOO per foot, which does them no good;
they are afraid to move lest they snouid
spoil the £IOOO per foot; they couldn’t move
in any great numbers unless similar railway
facilities w r ere provided somewhere else; and
people are afflicted with a stodgy terror lest
the provision, at great cost, of a similar con-
gestion of railways somewhere else should
diminish the value of the old congestion. Sup-
posing the Black Hole of Calcutta had been
a permanent affair, people would also have
reckoned they couldn’t afford to leave it. If
population wasn’t directed to one place by
roads and railways while it was absolutely
prohibited at another by the want of these
things, there would be a wonderful change in
congested and vested rights and values. Pro-
bably a sale price of £IOOO per foot repre-
sents £lO for the value of the foot and £OOO
for the difficulty of getting away to any other
foot owing to the way things are nailed down.
*....* * *

PLAIN ENGLISH.
Temperance in Tassie.
According to figures presented to the
Hobart Licensing Court the other day, there
were 147 grog-shops in that village in 1870,
118 in 1889, 107 in 1902. while this year
the number of places where a man may con-
venient] v see another man about the same
old dog' has dwindled down to a miserable
90 or less. Roughly, in half a century the
number of pubs has decreased by 40 per
emit., while the population has increased
by 100 per cent. In 1870 there was one
licensed house to every 180 of the popula-
tion; told ay it takes many a pub all its
time to dodge the bailiff, though authorised
to quench the thirst of an average of over
400 people. The Speck is not furiously
given to booze nowadays. The consumption
of alcoholic beverages per head and con-
victions for drunkenness compare more than
favorably with most of the mainland States.
Of course it is a cool climate, which partly
accounts for the fact that the rush for loug-
sleevers in Hobart is not nearly so prevalent
as in, say, Bourke, or Hay, or Cairns, or
Coolgardie, or Broken Hill, or even Pyrmont
and Footscray. And it does not appear from
the figures that the local abstemiousness
from summer drinking is counterbalanced,
as against the other States, by excessive in-
dulgence in winter tipples. This may be
accounted for by the lower rate of wages
and salaries still ruling in the Speck, and
partly, perhaps, by the fact that the Tas-
manian winters are so cold that people pre-
fer to stay by their own firesides at night
rather than risk frostbite by travelling to
and from the pub. Besides, with six o’clock
(•losing there are other than frostbite risks,
for you never know when the police are
likely to descend upon the temperance bars
with a rush, and sniff with large hairy noses
and an air of cruel suspicion at a half-
emptied glass of dandelion-ale and lemonade.
But even in the old days and nights, when
the pubs were open from 6 a.m. till half-
past 11 p m., there was not much whisky-
drinking in the towns or the settled districts :
though the west coast mining towns, where
Ihe winter is severe and the population
largely consists of unattached males, used
to get away with a sufficient quantity of
“hard stuff’’ to make pub-keeping in those
blizzard-breeding localities a pretty lucrative
business. Of course, in Tasmania, as in all
the States, every reduction in the drink bill
and every closed-up bar is claimed by the
temperance party as a triumphant vindica-
tion of its own special brand of prayer and
propaganda and politics, and is used by the
bleating bell-wethers of the party as a war-
slogan to encourage the valiant soldiers of
Cold Tea to still bigger efforts against the
demon of strong der-rink. But it is at least
a little doubtful whether the apostles of . the
official anti-grog party are in any way re-
sponsible for the increasing to
sobriety in Australia. As a matter of plain
everyday experience, it may be said that
the routings and ravings and tactics
generally of the apostles help to retard
liquor reform more than they advance it. In
Tasmania the other day Premier Lee intro-
duced a Bill for a referendum on State Pro-
hibition. Happy in the approval of the
official anti-liquor party, Mr. Lee appears
to have entered upon his crusade with all
the cocksureness of a leader who sees vic-
tory waiting for him just round the corner.
Egged on by a number of solemn ladies of
irreproachable character of both sexes, the
Premier firmly rejected suggested amend-
ments which aimed at including in the bal-
lot-paper, questions dealing with nationalisa-
tion, compensation, etc. He also declined
to accept a proposal to substitute for Pro-
hibition a reduction of licensed houses by 25
pet cent, with compensation to be provided
by the “trade.” He would have a Prohibi-
tion referendum or nothing. He got nothing,
for the Assembly, by a six to four majority,
declined to have anything to do with the one-
eyed measure.
A Land of Small Industries.
Victoria has always been well in front as
the manufacturing State of the Common-
wealth, especially in those lines that are not
local manufactures by necessity and stand
quite apart from tariffs. (Gas-making,
quarrying, baking, flour-milling for local
purposes, watch-repairing, newspaper print-
ing, the making of clothes to measure, cane-
crushing, boot-patching, ship-mending, etc.,
etc., etc., are local by necessity.) The
latest return (year to June, 191 S) shows
that the factories and workshops of the
State produced goods to the value of
£67,066.735, and after paying for raw
materials, wages, fuel and light and making
the ordinary allowance for the repair
and replacement of machinery, there was
£31,182.292 left (in theory) for the manu-
facturer. This of course was mere theory,
for out of it had to come interest on bor-
rowed money, bad debts and discounts.
(There is a habit of knocking 21. per cent,
or so off the account when it is paid in cash,
or else paying by p.n.. which costs quite a*
much for discount at the bank : and, if this
habit becomes general. 21 per cent, on
£67,000.000 is £1.675.000. which makes a
hole in £11,000.000 profit.) Also, the
wages item doesn’t include wages of working
proprietors. Out of .the 5627 factories 1828
employ four hands or less, and 3638 employ
less than 10 hands. In almost all these
cases the proprietor is a workman —some-
times he is the only workman—-and often he
toils much harder than the average, because
his scrap of capital is at stake. Sometimes
the whole staff consists of two or three work-
ing owners. If payment for the toil of 3638
small proprietors, at say £3 per week, was
included in the wages the paper profit would
be reduced to £10,614,764, and even that
makes no allowance for the fact that the
proprietor is a worker in almost every case,
large or small, except where a company owns
the show, and that there may be several of
him. Anyhow, taking £10,614,704 as the
standard, the profit is on the average ot
£lBB6 for the year for each establishment,
less, as already hinted, interest. State
and Federal land-tax and income-tax,
war-profits tax, if any, local rates,
water rates, discounts, bad debts, dona-
tions to charity (an occasional item
which some of the bigger concerns can
hardly avoid), advertising, embezzlements
and* sundries. In the case of big industiies
these may absorb half the takings, among
the small men, of course, the _ amount is
microscopic. They are uncertain items. I lie
£lBB6, of course, isn’t the profit per owner;
the number of owners who have to divide it
may vary from two to 200 or more. In the
case of Melbourne Gas Co. they are numer-
ous. The steady increase is among the
small men. Factories which employed four
or less hands were 714 more in 1918 than in
1904, while the bigger places were only (On
more. And the small industries are possibly
five or six times as numerous as they appear,
for no place is a factory under Victorian
law unless it employs machinery worked by
steam, gas, electricity, water or horse, or in
some way other than by hand. Bringing all
things into account, the “taking over of the
workshops.” as proposed by the One Big
Union, doesn’t suggest grazing in a very rich
paddock. A slight slackening of energy or
efficiency among, the horde of working pio-
prietors when they ceased to have a direct
interest in the business and became the paid
servants of a Soviet would make the margin
of profit very small, if it left any margin at
all. If the Soviet reduced working hours
and put in its brother or the chief local
enthusiast to manage, there might even be a
stoppage, (las companies might survive by
doubling the price of gas, but the price ot
cheap pants can’t be doubled under the exist-
ing tariff.
The Women’s Industry.
From a factory work-table: —
One of the first jobs the lately-created N. S.
Wales Board of Trade had to grapple with was
incidental to fixing the minimum pay for the adult,
self-supporting woman by discovering the cost to
such a woman 'of living. The Board (wages £l2
to £2O a week) has fixed on the sum of 30s. The
result is that this automatically becomes the mini-
mum at which any adult woman engaged in industry
coming within the scope of the Arbitration Act may
he employed. In that respect the step taken by
legislation represents a forward movement of quite
half an inch. It was all very well to explain to
women workers that they were to be protected
against receiving less than the wage of subsistence.
For that they had reason, Gon knows, to be thank-
ful. But to follow it up with this pronouncement of
five whole shillings per day seems something new
in the way of an anti-climax. A Wage bf 30s. next
week is equivalent in commercial usefulness to an
income of 17s. 3d. per week four years ago. If this
is not the case, the calculations of Knibbb are open
to grave suspicion, and the common experience of the
working herd has been a delusion. And just as it
would take some stretch of imagination by anyone
on the outside to believe that a woman, earning
2s. 9d. short of a sovereign before the war got a
reasonably decent wage should require a big mental
effort in the same place to believe that 30s, is a
genuine living wage now. It is unfortunate that the
Board should have accompanied its 30s. verdict with
a long rigmarole about the relative significance of
marriage ‘and industry to the female worker and a
vast silence about the arithmetic. A living wage is
a matter of tuppences, not of sermons about some-
thing else; and until this writer sees the Board of
Trade manner of handling the tuppences involved in
305., he is not going to move that a lot of words
about matrimony are a satisfying substitute. Of
course, a woman can live on 30s. weekly. But the
present writer is prepared to make a solemn affidavit
that the life she will lead can be no better than a
miserably penurious existence.# She will be ill-housed
and shabby and addicted to a diet of bread and jam
and tea, with nothing between her and destitution but
a rapidly-waning vitality, and morally more distant
from the finer things of life than if she lived
in a humpy at Coorabangawong. A woman who
can sew and fry an egg over a gaslight in her bed-
room or practice any of the numerous miracles of
economy the poor are accustomed to perform, will
live right en;ough on the 30s. So, too, could a man.
If the Board of Trade calculated a living wage for
a male upon the basis of the individual who manu-
factured his own shirts and patched his boots and
grew vegetables and went fishing on Sundays, it
could, in all probability, come to a point where
things for the male would be cut fairly fine. This
is what the Board has done with the woman —and
with the greatest respect to everybody concerned, it
may be remarked that the decision is a scandal.
Still, there is not much to be surprised at.
The idea behind it all is to pin the worker
down to specially-selected diets and allow-
ances of Is. 7Ad. a week 'for raiment
and 2d. for newspapers and haircuts twice per
annum-—to give, on the strength of the testimony
of various “experts,” a multitude of bureaucrats
the right to lay down rules of life for different
classes of citizefis, such rules being the foundation
of “wages.” It is monstrous that a mere
hod-carrier who can be kept in physical condi-
tion on food purchasable at 12s. 6d. should be devour-
ing nourishment at a cc#t of 13s. 4d.—and outrageous
that his children should spend 4s. 2d. on clothing when
equallv-serviceable material might be acquired for
2s. 9d. if the mother wore out Is. 6d. worth of
shoe-leather looking for it. The 305.-a-week-for-
women finding goes on exactly these lines. If the
woman hires a room at 7s. 6d. a week and doesn’t
eat too much or too frequently; if she doesn’t,
patronise the “pictures” with excessive regularity
and takes walking exercise instead of tram-rides; if
she wears tw'o blouses at 6s. lid. per season instead
of three at 6s. 6d. and omits to buy the latest
volume of verse, and so :on, she can subsist according
to her “station in life.” This way of seeking indus-
trial health and happiness, or whatever it
may be, will not work, and he is an absurdly
unintelligent employer who thinks it will. Woman’s
place in industry is a difficult matter. But if
employers of female labor and Parliaments and
Boards of Trade are going to persist in regarding
the woman worker merely as something to be used
as an ill-paid, indifferently-nourished drudge between
the time she leaves school and gets married to live
in a slum, the difficulties (social and industrial) are
not likely to grow any less. One of the 'root
troubles is, of* female wbrUsr'T£ho s livej[ a
parasitic- fccfcMw? living pa™)''on Tier iow-grade
earnings and partly on the earnings of a fathei 01
brothers or other relatives. Everywhere the self-
supporting woman lias to compete agains ns >at x
legism and battle against the male workers who
sanction it. In Sydney the Board ot Trade will
secure women the advantage ot going on \vi 1 u
battle on the basis of a minimum wage which, on
pre-war wages, would have been scoffed at as the
dole of a sweater.
The Amalgamated Sloths.
The Amalgamated Engineers have given 14
days’ notice, as required by law, of their
intention to strike unless wages are advanced
20 per cent. —the 10 per cent, recently
awarded by the Court and the other 10 per
cent, which wasn’t awarded by the Court.
And the Amalgamated Engineers didn t serve
notice on their party in Parliament to seciua!
even 1 per cent, increase in the tariff, so
that the employers could pay the extra wage
without being overwhelmed by toe competi-
tion of imports from countries which don t.
pav the wages that prevailed here even
before the award. The ’.‘Metals and
Machinery” tariff is divided into 102 sec-
tions, with as many as a quarter ot a hun-
dred items in a section —in fact some maj
run to more than that, being simply things
“not elsewhere included.” Of these 102 sec-
tions 06 are “free”; seven are 10 per cent.;
two are 124 per cent. ; eight are lo per cent..
and 12 are 20 per cent. Considering that the
local people want 20 per cent, increase m
one act—this in addition to other increases
since the foolish ass of a tariii was con-
structed —it doesn’t look much of a tariff,
but a good deal of an ass. In addition to the
f'o low-percentage items (out of lo 2) men-
tioned above there are specific duties of a
few “bob” per ton which are equally depress-
ing. Things are bad, but they’re going to
he worse, for thousands of Amalgamated or
disunited or “scab” engineers who have been
engaged in war-work —the kind that wasn t
done here—at war wages are going back to
their old jobs at peace wages, and hundreds
of vessels which, by reason of the war, were
not available to carry Amalgamated imports
are getting back into their stride. The result
is that Australia, as a place that makes
things, as distinct from a place that patches
and repairs or puts together things, is pretty
dead, and is likely to be more dead unless the
tariff goes up or wages come down. Appa-
rently the Amalgamated sloths are content.
I l there are among the returned soldiers some
engineers for whom there is no work the
hours can be shortened at the lengthened
pay to make the work spread round. Other-
wise they can “go on the land" with Gov-
ernment loan money. It is all very good save
that when the war-time loans of over
£100,000.000 a year can no longer he floated
the supply of imported engineering goods
may run short for lack of money to pay for
them. That won’t cause new industries to
start, for no one will risk everything he pos-
sesses on a business that may perish as soon
as some more loans arrive. It will only
bring the country up against the alternative
of a higher tariff, or cheaper and less som-
nolent Amalgamated engineers, or the need
for some engineers who aren’t so badly
Amalgamated.

W. H. Holman, Financier, etc.
Holman is a politician with a beaming way
about him and an aspect of childlike convic-
tion. In a letter to the S. M. Herald
(3/12/’18) he professed to dissipate the
suspicion that there was anything incon-
gruous about the spending of £9,126,000
loan money in one year, he being at the
same time solemnly pledged to reduce bor-
rowing instead of increasing it 400 per
cent. He said that £4.190,000 of the amount
was spent by those good business men the
N. S. Wales Railway Commissioners, and
£977,000 by the Harbor Trust and Water
and Sewerage Board. Over these bodies, he
alleged, he has no control. Like Pilate
washing his hands, of a conjurer producing
a rabbit out of bis left ear to prove his inno-
cence, he asked the public to observe that
there was no deception. Regarding which
these comments may be in order
(1) The bodies Holman mentioned wouldn’t have
spent the money unless he supplied it. He has that
much control, and it is enough. No doubt some of the
appalling £9,126,000 was spent by the Railway Com-
missioners, and some by the Water and Sewerage
Board, and some by the Harbor Trust, and some by
the Education Department, and some by the Lands,
and some by the office boy, and perhaps some by the
Devil. The fact is that it was spent.
(2) Holman said the £5,167,000 expended by the
people he mentions was handed to them because it
was required. Holiian, as an allegedly sane
citizen, must know that money is always being re-
quired. If the fact of it being required
means, in his opinion, that it must be bar-
rowed, then he is a most impossible person. He
pledged himself to find money required and yet keep
down borrowing—it had got down below £2,000,000
before he took office. And seeing that the revenue this
year promises to be nearly double what it was when
he took office he should manage something in this line,
unless he is the last possible muddler.
(3) Having alleged that £5,167,000 was spent by
certain responsible authorities in a certain year,
because it was really needed, Holman omits to say
whether the other £3,959,000 was or was not fooled
away by irresponsible parties though it wasn’t needed.
It. is a lot of money.
(4) He omits all explanation about the almost
equally ridiculous amounts he spent in a number of
other years.
(5) Also, while speaking with impassioned plausi-
bility about the Railway Commissioners’ demands for
rolling stock, He neglected to point out that railways
were being built, wholesale and for the building of
them the Commissioners were not responsible. But as
business-men, groaning under the burden of political

Mothers of Men.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Out of Bethlehem and Balmain—
Fill the pipe with sweeter breath ! —
Sydney calls to Nazareth :
And the' King, the King shall reign !
We hare fought with Doom and Death
And the glory conics again!
Mary, Mother of Sons,
Fee, At other of All!
How the praise runs
From (garden to stall!
By the old stars above
Ye have borne us for lore —
Christ and Cain,
Of your pain,
For the hope of world-good.
Lo, the faith that withstood
All of ill that, might fall!
Lo, the smiles that ye wore
In your great motherhood
We bend and adore!
O ye holiest Ones.
Eve, Mother of All,
Alary, Mother of Sons!
We march with the plough and the loom
Over broad hills to the sea,
Where the great waves blanch and boom,
Shouting of things to be.
With hoisting of white sails
We follow a good star
Till a fair dawn pales
On new hills and far.
And the match of the plough and the loom
Ever begins anew,
With a strange flower in bloom
And an old thing to do.
Always our hearts are strong
By plain and valley and glen ;
For we have the marching song—
The song of the Mothers of Men.
Be sure the wonder of her face
T Vas in the loneliest land of all;
And with her tears in that far place
She cleansed us all, she cleansed us all.
But we have marched with sword and drum
And filled a war-horn with our breath ;
And in the darkness we have come
To look into the eyes of death.
Our loom we set to weaving pain.
Our plough made furrows bloody red ;
And we have toiled till we were fain
To look with envy on the dead.
The world we burned with leaping fire ;
It scorched and wasted us in youth
That we might win our soul’s desire
And crush the foeman into truth.
And while we grappled with the strong
And darkly perished one to ten,
We heard again the marching song
Of soldier mothers bearing men.
Be sure, for all her tears that fell,
Her courage shamed her fighting sons;
For she could, die —ice knew it well —
Before the guns, before the guns.
There is a little hollow
Behind a. bushy hill
Where the long shadows follow
The sun’s hot will.
This way they point at breaking
Of the first morning light.
And that at the leave taking
Of day before the night.
And always one long finger
From east or west
Seems a while to linger
On a small grave’s breast.
On a grave where one is lying
These twenty years and more,
With the night wind sighing
The long grasses o’er.
When the breeze comes crisper
Listen, listen then !
“Here lies.” it seems to whisper,
“A Mother of Men”....
And the men she bore are sleeping
Far and far away—
Oh. earth is wide for weeping \
From here to Suvla Bay !
Be sure she spoke the quiet, word
That made them strong and very wise
When, valor flaming like a sword.
They looked at death with mocking eyes.
O Mary, Eve and mother mine.
The world is crying at the door:
And he would quaff of deeds as wine.
The little child you loved and bore.
Not straight his path nor strong his arm,
Yet something in his blood will leap.
And something save his soul from harm
Because you sang him once to sleep.
And there are bugles blowing shrill
And voices calling like the sea
Because the world in heart and will
Draws nearer mother-victory.
For this shall be the final tryst
Of Truth and Love on earth’s green sod,
When every Mary bears a Christ
And every Christ is Man-in-Goi).
Out of Bethlehem and Balmain —
Fill the pipe 'with sweeter breath! —
Sydney calls to Nazareth;
And the Queen, the Queen shall reign!
She has vanquished Doom and Death
And her glory shall remain!
N.S.W.
DAVID McKEE WRIGHT.

BRANCHES in VICTORIA AND SOUTH
The Insolence of Censorship.
Last week partly-Prime Minister Watt
was busy assuring the House of Heps., and,
incidentally, the Australian people, that
under no circumstances would the censorship
established under the War Precautions Act
be used for political purposes. Almost simul-
taneously Administrator Gilruth was
attacked at Port Darwin by a rabble who
wanted to deport him. The facts about the
proceedings were, at the moment of writing,
in a rather disordered condition; but there
seemed no doubt that there had been some-
thing in the way of a riot, that Gilruth’s
premises had been invaded, and that he had
been ottered personal violence. When official-
dom got wind of the affair the Censor promptly
ukased that the newspapers must be dumb—
that references to Port Darwin and Gil-
rutii and the rabble were forbidden. The
Bulletin is not going to cnarge Watt with
direct complicity with this outrageous abuse
of the censorship; but it i s going to politely
remind him that the incident transpired at a
moment which imparted to it the strongest
possible justification for the charge that the
censorship is a dangerous and ignorant in-
stitution. An attempt to show that, unless
the public s liberty to know what is happen-
ing in its own back yard lias vanished, the
effort to put the lid on the happenings at
Darwin was scandalous would be waste of
both words and of time. It must be obvious,
too, that for the community to bo taxed to
pay salaries to the gang ' of bureaucrats
vested by Ministerial Regulation with
authority to issue prohibitions and regula-
tions of this kind is an ugly injustice. Aus-
tralia will not be making any mistake if it
remembers that this Darwin episode repre-
sents only one of tin* very minor offences
uerpetrated by the same blundering crowd.
The thing has its comic aspects, of course.
It inav be a fact that the censorship thinks
the war is still raging, and that precautions
must be taken to win it. There i s the dis-
tinct possibility of Holdfast. Barnacle and
Busybody Ltd. being persuaded that if news
of Gilkutii being chased round the land-
scape at Darwin by a crowd of truculent
slaughtermen penetrated to Europe the re-
sult would be to bring Huoiies into dis-
repute and light the torch of anarchy at
Monte Carlo. But, after all, these considera-
tions are only further reasons why Watt
should rid Australia of a wrongful institution
—of one that seems determined to bring the
whole community into ridicule.
The Territory and Gilruth’s Nose.
It is a matter of opinion whether, if the
butchers at the Port Darwin freezing works
took charge of the Territory and ran the
show with a cleaver, they would make a
much more elaborate mess of the job than
has been perpetrated by successive political
cliques gathered at Melbourne and running
the proceedings by the grace of hot-air. There
is certainly this to be said by the advocates
of Butcherism: That the man with a job in
sight and a cleaver in his hand probably
understands what he has to do, and if his
implement is sharp and heavy enough he can
be relied upon to begin at the right place.
And it seems incontrovertible that the man
who is several thousand miles away from
a job, and has been too lazy to build a rail-
way to get to it, and has no mental com-
prehension _ either of the size of the contract
or the ineffectiveness of blither, can
be relied upon to do nothing but pro-
dime _ muddle. It becomes a serious
question of whether Butcherism may
not be the handmaiden of Democracy, and,
while the latter is fumbling along in the
darkness created by Party, drag the Territory
and everything connected therewith into the
dawn of a new and better day. To date,
Australia’s policy regarding the Territory
has produced nothing—unless it is a
railway which doesn’t exist, a vast debit in
the cashbox, voluminous forgotten promises,
innumerable illustrations of shirk, and the
spectacle of an outraged Gilruth wiping
blood off his nose and sending out S.O.S. sig-
nals to Red Tape in Melbourne. When the
deputy of the Democracy is detected with a
damaged nose and the proletariat from the
neighboring slaughter-house threatens to tear
officialdom up by the roots the time seems
to be nearly ripe for bringing out the hearse
for something. Gilrutii’s gore may repre-
sent the climax of the Commonwealth’s great-
est administrative and political achievement
in dodging the issue. The Bulletin awaits
further information with hope.
«
Too Late.
The story of the Hughes Government’s
wooden ships appears to be, in the main, the
story of everything plain and useful that
that feckless body ever attempted. People
who believe in reincarnation might set the
Prime Minister down as the second coming
of Etiielred the Unready, who did some
extraordinary things a millennium ago in the
locality where Hughes’s people come from,
and may have left a moral influence there-
abouts, but the other Unready is reputed to
have been a tall man of imposing stomach
area, and that s_poils the parallel.
Early in the war the Ministry decided
that wooden ships, being in its opinion, very
temporary and experimental affairs, were
not on its programme. Much later, when
the war was obviously much nearer its end
unless it proposed to live for ever, they were
put on the programme, because no progress
worth speaking of was being made with
steel ships. (This was partly because the
big company which wanted to build steel
ships was warned off, the thing being a Gov-
ernment job.) The policy then was obvi-
ously to construct in red-iiot haste, for the
need was urgent and freights were enormous.
They were so high that ships were paying
for their cost in periods varying from seven
to 12 months. Any sort of vessel which
could be built in a month and was good for
three years was liable to earn a fortune.
Rut there was no haste except of the com-
parative sort about the Ministry. It was
like the mills of Goo which grind very
slowly, only it had the slowness without the
grind. There was the usual drawing of
plans, the building of wooden ships being
presumably a new and rare and dangerous
animal to be approached with caution. Then
followed the consideration of plans, the elab-
oration of plans, the arrival of a new func-
tionary from England, no functionary being
any good unless freshly imported, the re-
consideration of plans, the debate about
building sites, the debate about wages and
conditions, and the trouble because almost
everything except the timber and build-
ing sites had to be imported. It was decided
that everything was to be of the best; even
the iittings of the officers’ quarters
were to be high-class and long-winded
works of upholstery. The end of the
war came without a single wooden
ship being, ready. Now, apparently, the
brief day of very slow timber-craft is
over—these ones were to be six-knot affairs
lest their velocity should put the other
arrangements to shame. Work, however, is
being done, when it is too late, at the rate
of £lOOO per day, and as this sort of thing
looks foolish the decision will probably be
to stop short, and throw the debris of the
scheme overboard, and hand down the loss
to posterity. Posterity will find a wonder-
ful lot of things bequeathed to him when the
will is read after the funeral.
Hospitals, Homicide, etc.
Something special in the way of gall and
bitterness, intermingled, after a' fashion, with
town-planning, happened in N. S. Wales last
week. In the first place State Solicitor-
General Garland declaimed about the “in-
credible folly” and the incapacity and such-
like of the Australian Government at Mel-
bourne in having Sydney’s Quarantine Sta-
tion * adjacent to the city. Now, it
was the State Government which invented
the idea of a quarantine station on Sydney
Harbor, and Garland has been an orna-
ment of more than one State Government.
N. S. Wales ran the insane idea for over
100 years—whereas the Federal authori-
ties have only been in charge for 10 years.
The bubonic plague didn’t get the State
Government into motion, nor did the earlier
smallpox epidemic. The latter belonged to
the time when the Faraway was used as a
floating hospital, and after the scare and the
madness and the bungle were over, dead,
swollen, decayed people who couldn’t be
accounted for were found in odd corners of
the Faraway. They were patients who had
been rushed on board by the Garland kind
of Government and overlooked, so that they
were neither fed, doctored nor nursed. xYnd
it really seems as if the State Government
of which Garland is one of the top blossoms
is much the same to-day. It is hard to say
whether the yarn related of Newcastle hospi-
tal comes under the head of town-planning
or homicide. It is alleged that the old isola-
tion wards were pulled down ; that new ones
(much too small) were illegally planted
close to the general hospital; and that, with-
out any influenza epidemic to contend with
there were lately 12 patients, male and
female, in a single small room and others in
passages and casual places. Also, it is said
that one child was killed by the conditions;
it suffered merely from mumps, but having
to be squeezed in with diphtheria cases it
caught the disease and died. Perhaps being
only Newcastle, and not Svdney, this sort of
thing doesn’t matter. Still. Solicitor-General
Garland might consider the story, for it is
his < business, and the Quarantine Station
isn t. And the row about Newcastle Hospi-
tal has been going on longer than the epi-
demic at the quarantine place.
Wanted—A Bloated and Congested
Garden.
Sydney Water and Sewerage Board is being
reproached as usual about the scarcity of
water. It seldom gets anything but re-
proach, and contumely is its" regular break-
fast diet, and it goes to bed in scorn and
wakes in ignominy. In defence, it says that
it is a metropolitan water supply—not an
irrigation scheme. To which the S. M.
Herald replies by asking: “Are we, then,
to be a metropolis without gardens?’’ It is
a serious question. The ambition of all
town-planners is to abjure the tenement
house and the flat, described as “a glorified
slum,” and let every inhabitant have a fresh,
nicely-watered playground, with flowers and
a bit of grass and room for a dog. a cat and
possibly the mollifying and refining influence
of a hen and a poll-parrot. At the
same time the idea i s that, so far as possible,
the factories, workshops, warehouses, ship-
yards, wharves and Government offices of a
State larger than the late German Empire
should be in Sydney. Also, at the same time,
that everybody should live conveniently close
to his work. The area of Sydney and
suburbs is 95,259 acres (or, if some outlying
districts are included, as they usually are.
118,409 acres) for, say, 770.000 people,' while
in the county of London 4,500,000 people are
put on 74.816 acres. And Sydney’s area
requires a vast deal of irrigation at times,
while London's is mostly watered by the
gentle or beastly fog from Heaven. Yet even
big Sydney has its slums, where people live
six in three or perhaps two rooms, and
untidy women converse easily from the top
window with dishevelled women at the oppo-
site top window. If the whole place was
laid out on ideal town-planning principles
and a few more industries grew up, it would
presumably be much bigger than it is.
Already it is 25 times the size of the Hay
Irrigation Area, and nine times the size of
the Curlwaa Irrigation Area, and about one-
third the size of the boasted Yanco Irrigation
Area with its wonderful Burrinjuelt reser-
voir, only a fraction of which has been
settled yet at a loss of well over £500,000.
It is far bigger than the occupied part of
Yanco, and is more than twice the size
even of Mildura, the most famous artificially-
watered settlement in Australia and the
model for all that came after it. So Sydney,
having more than two-fifths of the popula-
tion of the State congested within its limits
would be, even if it was only settled in an
agricultural way, the biggest'irrigation pro-
blem in N. S. Wales, reckoning only the
settled and not the theoretical part of Yanco;
and almost the biggest in Australia. With
770,000 people to he washed and drinked and
laundered and churwomnned, it would be,
considering the climate, the biggest irriga-
tion problem in the world, assuming that it
was laid out on ideal town-planning prin-
ciples. That is to say, if, instead of piling its
families four on a common landing and six
layers of families on top of each other —in
old Edinburgh there are, or used to be. as
many as eight or 10 layers—with a small
common yard, each family has its nicely-
watered playground, with flowers and a yard
or two of grass, and room for a cat and a
Towzer and possibly two fowls and a fowl’s
husband. Among the eight Australian capi-
tals only Darwin has a better rainfall than
Sydney so far as regards the actual city, but
that has no bearing on the case, for tin*
local thunderstorm that floods George-street
isn’t drinkable. No community has solved
the problem of a great, bloated eongested
garden city which the idealists of Sydney are
iip against, and some communities were con-
sidering that problem before Sydney was
born.
—
No Minister seems to open his mouth about
the Federal capital without putting both
boots into it. Thus Watt, being prodded
recently on the subject by Catts and an-
noyed by Higgs, said he was favorable to
the place, but the finances couldn’t stand
the strain at present. Canberra must wait
“till the war-debt presses less heavily on the
people.” Always the place is described as
a perpetual pauper, which must be kept up
by a subsidy. Yet the ordinary town or
city, built where a town or city might rea-
sonably be, is generally considered an asset.
Ballarat and Goulburn, or even much hum-
bler places like Kelly’s Plains and Moolap,
don’t get subsidies. They are expected to
make their own way and contribute to the
general revenue as well. It isn’t considered
necessary to suspend Toowoomba or to close
up Gambier “till the war-debt presses less
heavily on the people.” And these places
haven’t the adventitious aid of being capital
cities, where the gathering of public officials
should provide a ready-made crowd of rate-
payers and customers in addition to the
crowd that any town naturally has by rea-
son of its own industries. Canberra, perhaps
by reason of its thirst and its lack of minerals
and its bad situation, is always the spot
that can’t be expected to pay for its keep,
even with great special advantages—the sort
of advantages that Tamworth and Sale have
to totter along without.
Vice-Chancellor Mitchell, of Adelaide Uni-
versity, mentioned at the Commemoration
that a Melbourne professor, who left his
family and enlisted when conscription was
lost, wrote that he had “two good and suffi-
cient reasons for so doing—one, what the
Germans had done to Belgium; the other,
what they had done to Shakspeare.”

“Friendly Enemies,” at the Criterion
(Sydney), is not afflicted with many
literary merits. Still, it lias many good
points. It is simple. It is direct in
action and in purpose. It makes no
“ Everybody's Doing
It,” at Sydney Tivoli.
Jose Baptiste,
eccentric violinist.
breach with proba-
bility. It has a
strong h u m a n
appeal. And it is
as clean as the
dawn. Having said
that much it is
difficult to avoid
the reflection that
absence of the mere
literary quality in
a play is, after all,
very often a mat-
ter having quite
minor influence on
the proceedings.
The friendly
enemies here are a
pair of German-
born Americans—
Henry Block and
Karl Pfeiffer. They
migrated from the
Fatherland in early
manhood, passed
the Statue of Lib-
erty, and prospered.
Block became a
good Yank in
speech, in conduct
and in thought, al-
ways ready to
thank the Provi-
dence which guided
him to a land of
freedom, and pre-
pared to confess
his gratitude for the
privileges of
American citizenship. Pfeiffer, his mate in
the steerage trip across the Atlantic, became
a good American, too, but half his heart was
somewhere near the Rhine. He insisted on
being known as Karl. Block persisted in
calling him Charley. Pfeiffer called his son
Wilhelm. Block always breezily greeted the
hoy as Billy—to the great resentment of
father Karl. Then came the war—Block
sturdily denunciatory in his attitude towards
Berlin; the other apologetic, resentful of
THE GREATEST PLAY.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A girl and ,1 man and the shade of a tree;
I hat is the scene that appeals to me,
With a Christmas sun in the bend of the sky
And wings of green where the small birds fly;
tor the world’s a stage and its people play
With a change of bill for every day.
Speaking of bills—how the villain cold
Stealthily stalks to look for gold !
Still from the darkness his papers leap
Shrieking of debts that refuse to sleep ;
And the threats that lie uses might well destroy
The middle and head and front of joy.
Observe where the heaviest fathers go,
Fifty thousand in one long row,
I hough the happy arrangement of set and scene
Puts gardens and walls and towns between-
Ah, how often is love kicked out by the roots
At the lifting of fifty thousand boots!
Here is a banquet of beauty spread
When the moon climbs up and the dusk is sped ;
And through half the casements of all the world
She peeps to see how the hair is curled
Over sleepy foreheads and shoulders white
Of the girls in the ballet of Dream-b.v-Night.
And now the pantomime giant wakes
And his “Fee-fo-fum” the firmament shakes,
W hile the milkeans rattle along the way
And the breakfast-smells proclaim the day—
Oh, a million legs show pink and bare
To the shivering blush of the morning air!
It’s well worth watching the angels play,
The long-drawn drama of Night and Day;
But a girl and a man and the shade of a tree—
That is th? scene that appeals to me.
The tragedy turns I can scarcely heed
While I play my part as a juvenile lead !
N.S.W.

gorgeous ballets, fine backcloths, tuneful re-
frains, the fun of Stiffv and Mo, and the
alluring grace of Dame Daisy Merritt. The
story is not exactly the one we read at
mother’s knee, but the babes are caught sight
of every now and again, and at the end the
conspirators in this happy adventure catch
them up and hand them over to the safe
keeping of Paradise, Queen of the Fairies.
Horace Mann, as the wicked uncle, is aided
and abetted by King Caterpillar (Cliff
O’Keeffe) and the Witch of Sherwood
(Caddie Franks), two fearsome figures spit-
ting wickedness as a siphon spits soda.
Queen Paradise (Belle Pollard) very tune-
fully proclaims herself on the side of the
youngsters (Stella and Myee Lamond), and
invokes the aid of Robin Hood, whose doub-
let and hose are worn jauntily by the grace-
ful limbs of Queenie Paul, who in the inter-
tils of wooing Mayflower (Rita Starr) does
her work well. A special word is due to
the dancing of Lydia Carne and Dot O’Dea,
and to those two serious-faced mites Jack
and Zenda, who won great applause in a
dance where the small boy swings the
smaller girl across his shoulders in
the most advanced acrobatic style. Vince
Courtney, as Idle Jack, helps the fun along.
Rita and Stella Starr, Peter Brooks, Walter
Jackson, Paulasto and Dan Dunbar add
their talent to a good show.
The first evening performance of John
Galsworthy’s “The Pigeon” at the Palace
(Sydney) last week attracted a big house.
The repertory cast was at its best. Gregan
McMahon excelled his previous acting in
his vivid portrayal of the vagabond French-
man. So finely was the characterisation
wrought that the philosophic loafer seemed
a reality rather than a mere product of
stagecraft and actor-study. In fact, some-
thing of the same kind may be said about
all the members of the cast; for the reper-
tory spirit makes for a naturalism which is
in delightful contrast with much of the work
of the current theatre. Actors whom we
have known and appreciated for years rise in
these plays to the height of their art. The
result is honest and unforced emotion and
laughter with the genuine sparkle in it. If
“The Pigeon” has no very profound thought
it has at least a view of gentle satire which
makes it a pleasure.
The noisy humor of 1 Shaw’s “How He
Lied to Her Husband” filled a useful place
as a curtain-raiser to “The Pigeon.” It
stands about midway between the old one-act
farce and the modern comedy of social life;
and it is distinctly refreshing without any
element of nastiness. To say that the actors
got a lot more out of it than Shaw put
there is the best praise. The J. and N. Tait
Repertory season has gripped the ordinary
theatre patron in Sydney as well as the
enthusiast for literary drama. The next
show will be looked for with keen interest.
“De Luxe Annie,” with Emelie Polini in
the star role, is to be the Christmas season
attraction at the Palace, starting with the
matinee on Thursday, December 26. The
Bulletin will attend to it next week—with
the permission of the Censor.

week, at Her Majesty’s. The managerial
