The Tivoli (Syd-
ney) keeps the
Christmas pot boil-
ing with “Every-
body’s Doing It!”
The sketch, “Oh, You
Reach!” where a
poor but devoted
bridegroom scorns
offers of cheap
oranges and free
oranges, and fin-
ally commits
assault and robbery to secure the only
peaches in town for his Eve because she de-
sired that particular fruit, recalls a good
O. Henry story. The stage-sketch and the
story have the same climax ; the girl, offered
the hard-won peaches, says she would rather
have had oranges. Which is very like a girl.
Fayette Perry makes the maddening remark
in the present Tivoli rendering.
* * *
At the Lyceum (Sydney) Madge Kennedy
occupies the screen in “The Danger Game.”
At the same house Francis X. Bushman and
Beverly Bayne are “Under Suspicion.” Olga
Petrova keeps the Crystal Palace and the
Strand in dividends, wearing “The Life
Mask” every day of the week except the
25th. The mask worn by Miss Petrova has
not the faintest resemblance to the influenza
mask. The interminable Charlie Chaplin is
repeated at the Lyric, Empress, Globe and
Grand.
Adelaide Repertory Theatre has revived
for one evening a former success —Arnold
Bennett’s “Great Adventure.” The play
already shows signs of age. Also, it has the
usual weaknesses of a play made out of a
novel; a jumpy succession of scenes —prac-
tically eight short acts! But it is a brisk,
unusual and witty story, and a safe success.
Wilfred Neill and Ivy Stacey were just as
good as before —she as the sensible house-
keeping woman, he as the shy genius (in
whom, by the way, our own Hilder claimed
to see his own faithful portrait). Good
character-studies were given by Muriel
Craigie, Ethelwyn Robin, and George
Clarke; and in the last act Basil Harford.
“That’s why I love him, d—n him,” said
Block about Pfeiffer; and it might be taken
as the central motto of “Friendly Enemies,”
the Shipman and Hoffman comedy produced
at the Royal (Adelaide). This is an old-
fashioned sentimental comedy with a newer
plot. Particularly moving is old Pfeiffer
when he begs his son not to join the Ameri-
can army. “German atrocities! Nonsense.!
All British lies. Would I do such things?
Would your Aunt Anna in Hamburg do such
things? Very well, then! Why do you want
to go and kill your cousins?” But the moral
is thoroughly rammed home at the last.
Color, comedy, music and spectacle are all
well blended in “.Tack and Jill” at the
King’s (Melb.). The big features are spec-
tacular, and the joint managements (the
Tails and Bailey and Grant) have contrived
one or two scenes which attain the maximum
of pictorial splendor. The Spanish Bal-
let in the second act, scintillating with
toreadors and their fancy girls, surges into
a picture wherein scarlet and yellow are the
main color-note. The Flower Ballet is beauti-
ful, and the rich Bagdad finale to the
first act, with its swaying Oriental multi-
tude against a background of minarets and
cobalt sky, throws one back to the atmo-
sphere of “Kismet” or “Chu Chin Chow.”
The concluding march, representing Aus-
tralian products, furnishes the usual spread
of contours. The discipline and quality of
the ballet are to be credited to the wisdom
of the ballet-master, Espinosa. Lupino is
again producer and M.O. of innumerable
small diversions. lie is premier danseur in
the Spanish Ballet, instigator of revelry in
a comic chemist-shop and hairdressing-
saloon, as well as playing a dame de-
pressed by the careers of eight tumultuous
husbands. He exercises his folly without riot
or mannerism. Bert Bailey, with his
flaming thatch and grotesque wardrobe
and make-up, is a happily-resurrected
Guy Fawkes. His droll and incisive
humor is seconded by Joseph Brennan.
Hassan, the donkey impersonator, is
quietly inserted into the joyous proceedings.
Dorothy Leigh, with a nice voice and many
personal graces, is temporary principal-boy
until the imported specialist for the part,
Virginia Roche, a passenger on the Makura,
emerges from quarantine. Violet Collinson
is a songful Princess. Ida Newton (Jack)
and Grade Dorran (Jill) carry the thin
thread of the nursery legend up the hill and
down again, Fred Macdonald is Ihe Demon
of the Well. The dancing and the dressing
of the show are herewith awarded special
prizes, and a vote of thanks is due to scene-
painters Clint and Rowell. They have some
fine imaginative cloths.
“Goody Two Shoes,” at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.), is made eminent by several warlike
allusions. In the first place there is a lis-
some army in khaki lending the weight of
their young voices to a couple of soldier-
songs, and in one instance the girls perform
some striking evolutions after appearing
from an ingeniously improvised tent in the
middle of the stage. Later on the damsels
furnish an awkward squad for Private Toby
Claude when she vows her readiness to slay
the bugler—a wretch who arouses her too
early in the morning. There is a dockyard
scene with a swelling anvil chorus by
shapely munitioneers; but the strongest
stimulant to patriotic sentiment is provided
bv a series of cleverly-framed tableaux, end-
ing a huge and impressive picture in which
the Allies’ recent victory is celebrated by a
symbolical group. The Firm hasn’t neglected
to exercise its spectacular craft. The march
of the playing-cards and the final array, re-
presenting the courses of an expensive din-
ner, are very attractive, and there are
other charming impressions in which the
efforts of Maggie Dickinson deserve a special
medal.
* * * *
The scenic-artists have put their best
brush forward in some fine designs, and
the cast works hard to make up a good show.
Arthur Stigant starts another season of
droll achievement as the Dame, and there is
nothing more recuperative to melancholy
minds than Arthur in a vein of reminiscent
fancy, assisted by an inglorious milliner who
borrows a cannon and a kettle as models for
hats. May de Sousa, the new “boy,” is a slim
contrast to the plump and pretty Madeleine
Harrison, who is not overtaxed by the part
of the principal girl. Both ladies have
pleasant voices, but May reveals the greater
range and cultivation. Toby Claude, re-
appearing after a long interval. is. the same
volatile midget with a more embellished style
of facial mannerism and a less assertive
’vocal outfit. Fred Walton is a brilliant
intrusion in the disguise of a toy soldier who
makes himself eloquent by a wealth of pan-
tomimic gesture. Maude Gray, another new-
comer, is dedicated to light duties as a con-
ventional fairy, and the same might be said
of Dorothy Hastings, Fioie Allan, Stanley
Odgers and Lou Vernon. Con Moreni is an
eccentric character, and though Herbert
Walton chatters effusively he never outstays
his welcome. Ernest Langford,_ as a long
and angular reservoir of patter, is partnered
by a duchess (Rene Tanswell) in one or two
dialogue contests, and Kelso Henderson is
Maggie Dickinson’s accomplished presenta-
tion. The expected “specialties,” Jewel's
marionette circus and Ivenna Brothers’
aeroplane act, had not arrived when this
notice went to press. These people were on
the Makura and have evidently been post-
poned by reason of quarantine.
“Bubbly,” at the Tivoli (Melb.), has a
wider range of episode than previous revues,
but even its best scenes are deficient in body
and soul, so to speak. The scene in the in-
terior of a prehistoric cave has no bite,
and the burglarious item where Hugh
Steyne robs a safe with the assistance
of Billy Rego is rather a feeble bit of merri-
ment. Most of the stuff is of the quality of “a
few powder-puffs” wherein Noel Geddes is the
prettiest puff in sight, but this sort of elegant
futility palls after a time, and one begins to
demand something more substantial. Hugh
Steyne is again the leading comedian, lie
is supported by the quaint Rego, who makes
a special appeal as a baby bear. John
Junior prattles, and Leslie Gaze has a song
or two. Charles Albert is conspicuous
in .a couple of happenings, and Herbert
Leigh and Lawrence Ilardinge are “generally
usefuls.” The ladies prominent in the show
are Beatrice Holloway, Clarice Hardwicke,
Ethel Bennetto, Marie Eaton and Noel
Geddes, and of the lot only Marie. Eaton has
a voice of any sizeable dimensions. The
ballet, about a dozen graceful girleens, occa-
sionally hits the eye as decorations of the
season. The scenery by the Clints, Waite
and Grist is picturesque enough.
According to Labor Senator Gardiner’s
view, the trouble about the Shaw wireless,
which annoyed Laborite Jensen and Labor-
ite Long, was really a capitalistic con-
spiracy. “Powerful companies in Australia
commanded the press, and that was why
the press described the purchase as a scan-
dal.” Still, wireless companies are very few
—they could be counted on the fingers of
one hand and still leave some room for
closer settlement —and they are not notori-
ously rich. Also, the press is numerous
enough to take a lot of controlling. Will
Gardiner get down to details and say exactly
which wireless company controls which
paper, including affluent rags like the Age
and S. M. Herald and Adelaide Adver-
tiser. The idea of a little factory in a
side street being rich enough to control the
Herald is 1 umorous. Yet it is in keeping
with the vulgar idea that any two-man or
ten-man Australian industry that does some-
thing more intricate than sawmilling is a
hotbed of political intrigue, and requires
watching.
LIGHTS UP AT SYDNEY ROYAL.
Thp shadows are gone and the ghosts are laid—
The ghosts that streamed from the lantern’s heart;
The singers are doing a roaring trade,
The comedy-man plays his red-nosed part;
The bandsmen fiddle and blow with art
And stagehands sweat for their union wage
Where silent photographs used to start
And end the night on a vacant stage.
Color comes back to the Royal once more;
Dressing-room chatter and scandal flies;
The dancers cluster about the door,
The greasepaint thick on their cheeks and eyes;
That hectic flush is a flush that dies
When all is done and it’s time to go.
The Peace dove wings through the summer skies
So the oid Royal’s holding a speaking show.
“Gallery” : Jens Jensen had a packed
house for his “explanation” in the Reps, last
week. For three hours he equalled a
matinge idol in drawing powers, and the
horde that clung to every point of vantage
allowable by the staff were not denied a
little entertainment. The immense Jensen,
striding up and down at the foot of the table
with his voice rising to a scream of anguish
or descending to a half-sob of self-commiser-
ation, showered theatricalisms on an assem-
blage that had never been highly endowed
with tragedians. I calculated that he walked
several miles during his three hours of
tirade, triviality and counter accusation, and
when he let go charges of prejudice against
the Commission that judged him, and
disposed of hostile witnesses, the air seemed
to swirl with the swaying of his gestures.
Much of his speech was pure hysteria, much
of it quotation for the confusion of alleged
enemies; but every word of it was plain with
the pain of his injured pride. That his
administration should be questioned seemed
t<> hurt him more than the inquiry into his
private integrity, and he cited special cases
to prove that his administrative genius was
something more than a personal superstition.
Altogether it was a scene made more poig-
nant by the non-committal silence on both
sides of the House.

The T. and G. continues to grow with won-
derful rapidity. During the past live years
the income in the Ordinary department has
expanded by over 58 p.e., while in the In-
dustrial department the income lias almost
doubled in four years. Go back to the first,
lines in the two tables, and the growth of the
figures is even more remarkable. In 1906
tli6 Society had been in business for exactly
80 years. Its total premium income for that
year was'£l93,Bls; during the next 12 years
it had grown to £755.887. Combined funds
in 1906 amounted to £568,789; at September
80 last they totalled £2,891,566. In the
meantime the income from interest and rents
has increased from £2.8.287 to £185,868. The
war has, of course, affected the loss rate
lately, and during the past four years the
Society has paid 2044 war claims. No
doubt the average of these claims is not
high—in the Ordinary department the aver-
age sum assured on 57.646 policies in force
at September 80 was £l2l. Nevertheless,
must of the claims represent the loss of
young policy-holders from whom the Society
had received comparatively little in premi-
ums. Here is a comparison of the claims
ratio for the past two years compared with
1914:

However, despite the war losses, the T. and
G. has been able to maintain its bonus dis-
tributions. At September 30 the Ordinary
department funds amounted to £1,661,797,
and the actuary estimated the liability at
£1,618,454. Of this surplus of £48.343 all
but £5lO is being distributed, equal to about
17 p.c. of the premium income. In the In-
dustrial department the surplus was esti-
mated at £37.191. slightly less than half of
which is being distributed.
But though the Society has'suffered heavy
losses lately, the conditions created by the
war have provided some compensation. Note
the growth of interest earnings on average
funds: —

That last line probably does not show the
actual results for the past year. Though
all current taxes have apparently been
charged to the P. and L. account, a further
£lO,OOO has been appropriated for taxes in
the directors’ report. Maoriland taxation is
now very solid, as the amounts paid out by
this co.'show: 1913, £5931; 1914, £6429;
1916. £7962; 1917, £11,827; 1918, £11,079.
This heavy taxation has come to stay; and
since it is very unlikely that the insurance
Cos. will do nearly as well in the next few
years as they have during the war, the
cautious Dunedin directors have probably
taken advantage of a record 12 months to
provide something towards future taxation
calls. If the £lO,OOO appropriated for tax-
ation be included with the figures in the
table the profit was £60,710 —equal to one-
tl ird of the paid-up capital.

Like practically every other insurance co.
whose accounts have been published . re-
cently the National of Maoriland shows a
record small loss rate for the year, while the
increased premium income has resulted in a
reduction of the expense rate. The marine
business has been extremely profitable; but
it might easily have been otherwise. For the
wonderfully small loss rates for the past
couple of years the British Navy deserves at
least a vote of thanks from insurance co.
shareholders. Wilhelm boasted that he was
going to sweep the seas of Allied ships; and
the insurance cos. for a while were not too
sure that Wilhelm was a false prophet. In
the dark days this marine business was a
risky gamble; nobody knew exactly what
were the limits of the Huns’ much-vaunted
TJ-boats. But when the insurance cos. backed
the British Navy they fortunately backed a
winner. Some amazing profits have been
made out of this marine business; for in-
stance there is the case of the six-years old
West Australian Insurance Co., which
plunged heavily into the business and last
year showed a surplus of premium income
over losses of over one-quarter of a million,
compared with £274.1 in 1914!
From the policy-holders’ point of view the
National of Maoriland is unusually strong.
Capital and reserves total £577.774 —suffici-
ent to cover more than twice the aggregate
losses of the past four war-years. And since
all but £2147 of the 15 p.c. div. for the past
year was met by interest earnings, practi-
cally the whole of the underwriting profit is
available for further strengthening the co.’s
position. A very satisfactory feature of the
latest balance-sheet is the improvement in
the investment of the funds. Go back only
three years:—

than three years ago, while £164,950 lias

shares of £5 paid-up to £l, 10s. of it repre-
senting reserves capitalised two years ago.

The highjy-satisfactory arrangement made
for the purchase of wool by the British
Government has helped this co. High prices
mean larger commission earnings, so
although handling costs have soared profits
show a good recovery. But iu the
recent comparatively dull years the co. has
improved its position, for there has always
been a very satisfactory margin between
profits and dividends. Even in the very
good years shareholders have not been
greedy. Since 1908 £98,000 has been dis-
tributed among shareholders, while visible
reserves have been strengthened by £76,316,
equal to over three-quarters of the paid-up
capital.
The following table shows the balance-
sheet movements: —
1900. 1914. 1917. 1918.
Mortgages . . £78,000 £45,000 £45,000 £45,000
Other debts .. 115,605 66,900 48,980 106,413
Freeholds .. 153,500 143,113 143,113 146,113
Leaseholds . . 10,486 8,786 12,186
Advances, etc.. . 173,082 153,733 135,467 184,034
War Loan . . 15,000 26,962
The advances, etc., are shown in the balance-
sheet as “amounts due to the co.” Exactly
how that item is made up is not shown, but
apparently owing fo the abnormal conditions
ruling the co. has had to increase its
advances to clients to the extent of about
£50,000. The result has been that lia-
bilities, apart from the mortgage on pro-
perty. are now more than double what they
were 12 months ago. Owing to the scarcity
of shipping the co. lias had to increase its
storage accommodation, and is laying out
£16,000 on these extensions. The result of
the policy of keeping a lot of the profits in the
business is shown by comparing last year’s
balance-sheet figures with those for 1900.
Though there is now £ll,OOO more out in
advances, etc., the co. owes £42.192 less, and
holds £25,962 of War Loan. Meantime it
has purchased £14,113 of new property and
written £21.500 off for depreciation ; and
goodwill, which stood in the books at
£40,000 18 years ago, has been written off
out of surplus profits. Whether substantial
inner reserves have been created to meet
contingencies The Bulletin does, not
know ; but in view of the directors’ conser-
vative policy in the matter of dividend dis-
tributions it is not unlikely that they have
put something aside to protect advances.
At any rate,, the past experience of all pas-
toral-finance cos. illustrates the wisdom of
making provision for bad seasons. How-
ever, with reserves of £110,882 accumulated
capital should be absolutely safe under all
circumstances. The size of the reserves, by
the way, draws attention to another point:
the present 10 p.c. div. is equivalent to
under 5 p.c. on shareholders’ total funds. If
anything like the past year’s profits can be
maintained shareholders might reasonably
hope for something better later on.

The war has not hurt Uncle; on the other
hand he has, in these dear money times, fol-
lowed the fashion and been able to ask a
little more. So. though this co. has
now only £2OOO more in pledges and stock
compared with five years ago, the income
from interest on pledges, profit on sales and
rents has increased from £10,798 to £14,472.
On the book value of the pledges and stock
last year’s income represented a return of
41.4 p.c., compared with 82.0 p.c. five years
ago. There are a couple of thousands in
property, which are no doubt earning their
bit, but those percentages give some idea
of how much more profitable business has
become. During the year two new branches
were opened in Melbourne suburbs, and the
Collingwood business in particular has
proved very sweet. Paid capital is £12.911 ;

had to be written down to cover the losses, lie-
sides the capital and the reserves (now
almost equal to the capital), the co. depends
on £lB.BBl of deposits to finance its busi-
ness. The future looks so promising that
the directors have taken up a city property
at a cost of £37.000, and have gone into
debt to the extent of £Ol,-150 to acquire it.

Taxation has taken a big bite out of profits,
but the results since the war have been glit-
tering compared with the co.’s previous per-
formances. For the past four years to Oct..
1014, the net profit was £23,171; for the
four years since then, after providing liber-
ally for depreciation, replacements, etc., and
for taxation, the net profit lias amounted to
£44,655. In the former period, after paying
a dividend averaging just under 7 p.c. p.a.,
only £6BB was added to reserves. Though
during the past four years tin total net profit
has been equal to 53 p.c. of the paid-up capi-
tal, the directors have paid dividends averag-
ing not quite 0 p.c. p.a., so reserves have
been strengthened to the extent of £15,445.

bonus). During the past year the mortgage
matured and the co. decided to work on a

Harper, of the X. S. Wales Mines Dept.,
has put together a long yarn about the old
Hill End goldfield which makes interesting
reading. If the examination of the con-
tribution is restricted to the dizzy figures
of the yields of the ’seventies, the outlook is
nothing but gold ; but if the results of the
operations since the big revival scheme was
launched with the formation of the Amal-
gamated Co. in 1911 are taken into con-
sideration the enthusiasm fizzles out. Har-
per 1 reckons that if there are any more
chunks of pure metal they are likely to be
found at levels lower than the present work-
ings. The saddle-reef formation is pretty
well exploded, Harper barracking strongly
for the bedded variety. He thinks a lot of
the regions about the contact of the Ordo-
vician and Devonian makes, and so far his
deductions have certainly worked out well.
The developments, however, have been local!
Nothing deep down will be proceeded with in
earnest until the water has been got rid of
by the completion of the tunnel traversing
the field.
Shareholders in the Amalgamated have re-
ceived very little information regarding the
reasons for the non-appearance of the big
makes of gold that were so optimistically
forecasted. Geologist Harper now ex-
plains that the repairing of Carver’s
tunnel and the pushing of the main drive
about 1000 ft. north, in addition to cross-
cutting and driving on various veins, pro-
duced only a little' auriferous quartz. Several
of the old shafts, put down in the? ’seventies,
were at a greater depth than the tunnel. The
IS-head battery at the mouth of the tunnel
was kept going largely in crushing the old
dumps without payable returns. “This is
hardly to be wondered at,” observes Harper,
“for the dumps had been picked over and
over again by the individual miners, and
only the valueless stone was left.” Again,
after the co. had driven the new Consolidated
tunnel below Carver’s someone discovered
that Carrol and Beard’s vertical shaft, also
an early-day work, had been sunk 290 ft.
below the tunnel level. The cash, Harper
points out, could have been more sensibly
absorbed in bailing out the old Carrol and
Beard shaft and in continuing the crosscut
east to intersect the main veins, some 300 ft.
below. The co. spent over £52,000 during
its existence, nearly £40,000 of which was
paid from capital and the rest from gold won.
The interests are now held by the Marshall’s
Hill End Co., with a capital of £15,000 in
75,000 shares, the Amalgamated retaining a
two-years’ tribute to work the Star of Peace
and Mica vein series below the Amalga-
nated tunnel level. Tributors have lately
opened up a nice make of ore by a winze on
the Mica vein, the vein varying in width
from a mere thread to 9in.; but as the gold
is also in the adjoining slates the full 3ft.
between the walls is being taken out. The
interesting point is that the high values are
still going down.
* * * *
Archie McKellar, who recently landed on
the two big reefs a little west of Mount
Dudley (N.S.W.) has sold the shows to a
Sydney crowd, who are taking steps to open
the makes. In each case there are good
indications on the free outcrop on the ridges,
and values are said to be immediately
assured. The consistency of the loom in the
neighborhood is a good sign, but nothing but
solid sinking will prove whether the stuff
exists with depth.
* * * *
Yerranderie silver (N.S.W.) is still able
to hand out an occasional sixpenny on
30,000 shares, and has put up a smart record
of 102 dividends in 20 years. A net profit
of £1589 was made during the year ended
Oct. 31 from silver-lead ore that realised
£7927. Shareholders received two sixpenny
divs. Of course the rich upper levels are
depleted.
* * * *
The N.S.W. Cement, Lime and Coal Co.,
which has put about £300,000 into its Kan-
dos proposition, is making a big hole in the
face of its mammoth limestone deposit. The
quarrying work is cutting across the deposit,
which is a couple of hundred yards wide at
that particular point, and runs several miles
north-west. Carne, Government Geologist,
has figured out about 15,000,000 tons in the
show. A method of displacement novel to
such workings in Australia is being tried.
Four 6in. drillings are being put down from
the surface to the deposit bed, these being a
few feet from the edge of the 120 ft. face.
The few cwt. of dynamite introduced will
heave about 5000 tons of stone at one go.
The process is relatively cheap with drilling
at the rate of from sft. to 7ft. a day. The
feature of this particular formation is its
uniformity of contents. It is only two and a
half years since the co. started manufacturing,
and it has to date turned out about 75.000
tons of cement.

No plums are in the Christmas pudding
o f Edna May (W.A.). The crushing of
36,593 tons during the past six months
brings reserves down to the following
figures:—-
Tons. Value per ton.
Above 300 ft. .. 2,559 21s. 3d.
„ 383 ft. . . 4,641 755. 6d.
„ 480 ft. . . 14,500 675. Od.
This makes 21,700 tons averaging 635. 5c1.,
so that the mine has proved stone containing
gold worth roughly £69.000. Costs in the
six months were 335. lid.; so if it be taken
that a complete extraction were got the
profit would work out on that basis at about
30s. per ton. On 21,700 tons that would
bring in, say, £33,000 to be divided among
43,000 shares. But the complete extraction
never happens. Therefore it looks as if. as
usual, the market hasn’t been wandering
in the dark when writing prices down. Of
course the disappointment is the bottom level,
where the shoot shortened and where the
grade fell off. Further, holders didn’t keep
in mind the working out of the accumulated
slimes, though the bears did. Mick Williams
is on the hunt in the western ground for a
fresh shoot, but his is a forlorn hope. lie
still murmurs about water and the need for
another pump unit, as the bottom stopes go
forward. What price the othed mines
directly Edna May blows off steam, as it
will do within the next nine months unless
the unexpected happens? But what a little
treasure Edna May has been? Out of the
treatment of 108,000 tons it has put divs.
amounting to £7 ss. per share into the
pockets of the Tricks party.

By tk» end of this year Westralia will
have produced approximately £137,000,000
worth of gold and paid somewhere about
£27,000,000 in dividends. Beginning with
£ll4B in 1886, the value of the annual output
increased in six years to £115,182. Then
with the advent of Coolgardie, Kalgoorlie and
other new finds, it soared until 11 years later
in 3903 it reached £8,770.919. Since then it
has, with the exception of a temporary check
in 1913. steadily declined. This year its
value will be about £3,500,000. To date the
biggest producers have been: Gt. Boulder,
£11,664,822: Golden Horseshoe, £11,227,004;
Ivaiihoe. £9,431.084; Boulder Perseverance,
£6.780.752; Gt. Fingall, £5,020,087; Oroya
Brownhill, £4,930,498; Sons of Gwalia,
£4,866.960 : Lake View Consols. £4,321,718;
Kalgurli, £4.348,221; Associated, £4,190,530.
The search for minerals other than gold has
never been so determined as. it has during the
past 12 months, and a largely increased out-
put is expected next year.
Lake View and Star (W.A.) expects to do
better after peace has been declared. Re-
striction of development and selective mining
of a limited tonnage of ore has, on account of
war-time costs, been the policy of the co. for
some time past, but directly more favorable
working conditions permit the Board pro-
poses to expand operations. Ore reserves
were estimated a few months ago at 305,000
tons, worth 265. Sd. per ton, which at the
old rate of consumption is equal to about 18
months’ supply. But then there is a big ton-
nage of partially developed ore to be taken
into consideration.
Lake View and Oroya’s accounts for the
last financial year show a profit of about
£21.000, out of which shareholders have been
paid a 9d. dividend. Apart from its West-
ralian interests, which include 571,844 Lake
View and Stars, 169,000 Yuanmi shares, a
big wad of Oroya Link scrip and the whole
of that in the broken-down Queen of the
Hills, the co. holds nearly £600,000 worth of
shares in the Burma Silver Lead Corpora-
tion and Zinc Corporation, etc. Then there
are some bundles of Great Fitzroys, North,
South and Central American mining stocks,
Russian oil shares, and other bad and doubt-
ful securities.
* * * *
This is one of the many ways that labor
conditions are evaded by gold-mining lease-
holders in the West. The apparently shep-
herded holdings of an apparently indolent co.
were jumped and forfeiture was applied for.
Instead of taking the ground away from the
offender the Warden imposed a small fine for
each block on which the labor conditions had
not been observed, and the Minister after-
wards reduced the penalty. But in the
meantime three months had been allowed
to lapse before labor covenants were
again obligatory, and during that period the
co. hadn’t to pay out a cent. Had the Court
ordered the leases to be manned during the
three months over £3OO would have been paid
in wages. As it was the co. was able to hold
the ground for that time on payment of a
small fine. It has always been easy to
shepherd gold-mining areas in Westralia, but
never so easy as it is now.
* 4 * *
Mining taxation is again being discussed
in the West, and a lot is being heard of the
argument used in S’Africa that any mine
which, without any direct or indirect taxa-
tion, could make revenue cover its cost,
should be enabled to continue operating. A
number of workers would, it is contended,
thereby earn a direct living, and the money
put into circulation by them a still
greater number of other persons to do likewise.
The Rand is quoted as the best example of
this. Its mines give direct employ to some-
thing under 30,000 whites and a little over
200,000 niggers; but the whole population
of 250,000 whites and 300,000 colored are
directly or indirectly kept going in addition
to large numbers of white, black and brindle
elsewhere in the country. A strong point is
also made of the indirect benefit derived by
the State from mining. In the case of the
Rand it is calculated that the direct and in-
direct revenue to the Government from the
mines amounts to over 9s. per ton.
Because union officials are angry over
Justice Powers allowing contract work
in alluvial mining, neither of the Langi Logan
(Vic.) shows can get a tender for work in
wash. This forces attention to pumping and
development. The first eats up money,
though the expense is not all waste, for the
better drained the gutter the cheaper is the
extraction of wash and the easier the open-
ing up of the ground ahead of the pauellers.
According to Bill Toole the New Langi has
5000 fathoms developed and dry off No. 2
shaft on the Cathcart gutter and 10,000
fathoms available down the main lead from
No. 1 shaft. The all-round valuation is
about 11 oz. gold per fathom, giving over
£90,000 of gold in sight—if the estimate can
be relied on. Taking Bryant’s statement
that 20 p.c. profit ought to come out of such
ground, then something like £20,000 should
be available for shareholders out of the areas
of wash. But the problem is whether the
dirt will sluice out the 055. So much work
has been done on the Cathcart lead as to give
data as to what may be expected there, but
there isn’t the same experience to go on over
Langi gutter wash. If it runs up to the oz.
it should pay under pre-war conditions if
Labor will only cease sulking, and Bryant
and Toole can be made to offer some bait to
allure crowds into the mine. It has cost
£122,000 to put New Langi where it is
to-day, and with £20,000 showing in profits
tin- drift in prices to a modest crown
shows that the market isn’t taking risks over
financing scrip. Toole will have got his
No. 2 shaft linked up with the No. 1 work-
ings in a few weeks. That will put the mine
in the strongest position it has yet occupied,
because water from the gutter can be shunted
up to the No. 1 shaft pumps. This will ease
the pressure in the Langi gutter below the
Cathcart junction, and it is there the co.
ought to have its best prospects if the tribu-
tary has shed a fair run of gold into the
main stream.
Duke and Main Lead Consols, Mary-
borough (Vic.), has had its final cleanup;
so rails will be pulled up and the machinery
man given a chance to pick up bargains.
Jimmy McCrorey was at his best when, with
Dr. Miller, he floated off this block on the
Duke lead, next door to the Duke United.
In those days North Duke was outputting
gold on a good scale, and George
Bryant was paying divs. from the Main
lead almost down to its junction with the
Duke gutter. Right through Duke and Main
Leads was well handled. No extravagance
on plant, and as the mine was not costly to
open it paid divs. like a toff almost up to
the time Jimmie McCrorey handed in his
checks. As the co. got down the gutter and
away from the junction with the Main lead,
the value of the wash tailed off and the runs
of gold narrowed. So. although the claim is
not worked out, the high cost of supplies and
labor put the extinguisher on the last of
the big productive alluvial mines of Victoria.
The only giants in size left are the two
Langi shows and that bob-tailed concern, the
Duke Extended.
* * * *
Dalmayne Colliery (Tas.) is to go the
way barrister Morley wants. He says that
the Board cannot ask shareholders to wipe
out £45,000 of debentures and pay another
£3OOO of debts. So he recommends plant-
ing the whole property m o the bond-holders’
back-yard to let them shape out a new r co.,
into which the men who have given consider-
ation for shares shall be admitted. The
rearrangement shuts out promoters, ven-
dors, the com. man and such others, in con-
sequence of which there is much groaning.
There also is to be an investigation into
the doings of sundry old-timers. However,
the leek had to be eaten if liquidation was
to be voluntary. The bond-holder is now
assured that he won’t have to bid against any
coal baron or shipping ringer if such want to
venture into the broad waste of waters off
Pickannie Pcint. He also knows that he
sits safe from calls till the shareholders put
up another five shillings.
* * * ♦
The high price of tin gave Briseis (Tas.)
a profit of £11,500 for the Sept, quarter. For
the first nine months of 1918 the yield was
181 tons of a net value of £61.093, which
gives an average of £330 per ton. The price
for the corresponding period of 1917 was
only £224 per ton. Briseis is giving a pro-
perty acquired under option at Gulgong
(N.S.W.) a trial.

The late Major Quilter —here with G.-G.
Dudley and a greyt favorite with all who
knew him —was killed early in the. war. He
has one beautiful memorial in Australia,
lie presented a sundial to Melba, also some
heavy flagstones for a rose-path which lie
designed for the garden at Coornbe Cottage.
The long path is now rose-lined and the
sundial is in position with its motto, “Count
Only the Bright Hours.” Melba lias a
quaint i\f gate leading from the Quilter
Walk into a kitchen garden, which is flanked
by sunflowers and hollyhocks.

lot of people in W.A., accounted for John
Forrest and for ex-Premier Frank Wilson,
at 59, after many years of desperate effort
to beat the dread disease. Perhaps the
haunting knowledge that lie was in the toils
of an incurable malady produced the some-
what cynical bitterness which characterised
the close of Wilson’s Parliamentary days,
alienated much valuable support and caused
bis defeat for Sussex at the last general
elections. Another victim recently was
George Whitten, who cut no political ice
nor, indeed, sought the public limelight in
any shape or form, but was kindly known

_ When the late E. W. Cole was one of a
vice-regal party on the Murray, about two
years ago, he claimed to have been an early
navigator on his own account, having built
a boat himself at Echuca in 1860 and voy-
aged down-stream in it.
John English, Sydney’s new Gaud Mayor,
is one of the few public men who success-
fully defied the Labor machine. In 1909
he was a leading spirit of the Belmore
(N.S.W.) Labor League. On the death of
E. W. O’Sullivan, who held the electorate
as a Caucus member a few months before
he went west, there was a miniature Donny-
brook when boot-manufacturer P. J. Mina-
han, then a vice-president of the P.L.L. Execu-
tive, secured the Labor endorsement and
romped home. English was not at all satis-
fied, so two rival branch Labor-leagues
sprang into being in the electorate and a
battle for the official selection for the 1910
election resulted. By this time Minahan
was Lord High Executioner of the Labor
Executive, and finally emerged with the de-
sired endorsement. Supported by a host
of angry workers, English opposed Minahan
as an independent Laborite, and despite -a
declaration by the executive that English’s
Labor-league was “bogus,” he got within a
few votes of Minahan. At the 1913 general
election English again stood as an indepen-
dent ; but on being beaten by more than a
thousand votes he stood from under. Mean-
time he had retained his seat in the City
Council as an alderman for Belmore Ward.
He then made several attempts to re-enter
tbe Labor movement; but there was nothing
doing until early in 1917 when lie was
admitted to membership of the Municipal
Labor caucus. At the municipal contests
last month he was endorsed by the Aus-
tralian Labor Party as an “official candi-
date” and won in.

Major Thomas Cherry, A.A.M.C., who left
Melbourne some time ago in pursuit of fur-
ther knowledge relating to the “bilhamosis"
disease, introduced to this country by sol-
diers who inid been stationed along the Suez
Canal, is now in England, after a variety of
experiences. Cherry, * whose eldest son was
killed in France not long since, began his ser-
vice with the A.I.F. as a hospital research
man. Bilharziosis was then just beginning
to make its appearance. An old friend of
Cherry’s happened one day to be talking with
lum about the symptoms of the disease, and
recalled the fact that a man at Pinjarrah
(W.A.) had suffered from a complaint with
similar symptoms. Cherry posted across to
the West and found that the case there had
come from S’Africa, the patient having
served in the Boer War, and it proved to be
the only case of the kind yet recorded prior
to the recent introduction from the Suez
Canal region. After his Westralian inquiries
Cherry went over to Egypt and did some
mere nosing around ; and at last, feeling the
need of a change, he went off to carry on
with the boys in Palestine. The latest news
is that he has “recovered”—though whether
this is from wounds or sickness is not known
to his family.
Edwin 11. Bayfield, who died at Nairne
(South Aiis.) on December 9, was born in
Adelaide in 1845. When a young man he
left with the overland telegraph workers for
the Northern Territory, and went through
to Darwin. He saw the first cablegram
come through from England. On the Gym-
pie goldfields in Queensland Bavfield was
fairly successful, and on his return to South
Aus. made a hobby of hotel keeping. lie
retired to consider the results about eight
years ago.
“Melb” : Pastor Kent, the adventist, from
Sydney, having- made good with a long series
of Sunday addresses in that expensive place
the Auditorium, which- the rich Collins-street
Baptist congregation is nervous in hiring
occasionally, has moved to a big tent in
Flinders-street East, a pitch once occupied
by Wirths’ Circus. G. V. Brooke, the
tragedian, lived in a brick villa at the rear
of this and fronting Flinders-lane. The
approach to Kent’s tent is made in a pic-
turesque way through a hole cut in a big
hoarding plastered with theatrical posters.
All this is deliciously romantic to the steady-
going religious folk, so Kent’s show is
crowded. He draws more people than Arch-
bishop Clarke’s nice, warm, comfortable
cathedral. Close by is Strong’s very fine
Australian Church, reached laboriously up
22 steep stone steps—a timber-yard of empty
pews, with a preacher perched on a distant
iceberg called a pulpit.
Just dead in Tassie, .T. H. Hodgson, well
known for years past to seafarers who have
called at Hobart. He was diver to the Hobart
Marine 'Board, and laid the underwater
foundations of most of the lights in the
Iluon lliver and D’Entrecasteaux Channel.
He built the Oil Butts light from 70ft. below
the surface, and was instrumental in refloat-
ing several sunken ships. A son put up a
good record in France and recently returned
wounded.
When Wilson, who died a few days ago,
was Premier of Westralia. he came to Mel-
bourne in the summer of 1916 to attend a
Premiers’ Conference,, mainly to screw as
much loan money as lie could out of the
Commonwealth fon “urgent” local public
works. Hughes told the States’ delegates
that their demands were impos-
sible of fulfilment and to cut
tjiem down by half or nearly
so. The temperature was over
lOOdeg. in the shade, and the
conference-room at State Par-
liament House was stifling. Wil-
son. who Was not a lean man,
had taken off his coat and vest,
and while lie argued for more
money he stood in front of the
large electric fan waving his
hands and distributing the cool
air about his head and shoulders
—going through the same move-
ments, in fact, as a youngster
splashing himself in a shallow
creek or a Chinese at; worship.
“Look at him!” said’ Hughes,
pointing to Wilson, “lie’s calling
on his joss to come to his aid in
getting the extra coin he wants ”
Lieut. .T. Basil Minehin, M.C.,
D. 5.0., at the age of 20 enlisted
as a private in Westralia, in
September, 1914, and embarked
two months' afterwards. lie
took part in the Landing and
then went to France. In Sep-
tember. 1917, he was awarded
the M.C., and in July. ’lB, the
D.S.O. Through all his en-
gagements he did not sustain
even a scratch or suffer a day’s illness. He’s
a Westralian of the third generation. His
brother. Lieut. ,J. 11. Minehin, who served
in the Boer War, was killed at Noreuil in
1917.
*\ Radio-Commander Cresswell (no relation
of the Bear-Admiral), the Commonwealth
Government wireless expert figuring in the
Navy Office sensation, is a young man in
the thirties. Intellectual looking and pre-
maturely grey, he has the typ’e of tall spare
frame that suits the sea uniform. He started
his career in the mechanical department of
Melbourne Telephone Exchange, later on
joining the Australia as wireless officer. Ilis
ability in research work brought him to his
present rank. Dad was Anglican parson at
Camberwell (Vie. ) for about a quarter of a
century. Late Commander Colquhoun, of
the erstwhile \ ictorian Naval Forces, was
one of the clan.
“Mookingah,” about one of the old hands:
John Conrick, of Napper Merri station, on Cow-
per’s Creek, Bananaland, in the year 1880, trans-
ported the first gold from the Mt. Browne gold-
fields near Milparinka to the Melbourne Mint. He
look up Napper Merri (for a while known as
Burke’s Depot) just 11 years after the first explorer’s
visit. In those days Wilcannia was the nearest
town, a little over 500 miles distant, with very few
residents any closer. In 1880 he left Napper Merri
for the Melbourne races, and, having heard of the
Mt. Browne rush, decided to go that way. To
reach there he crossed 300 miles of virgin unin-
habited country with no permanent water, and one
dry stage of 70 miles. At Mt. Browne 3000 men
were on the field, some with tents, but mostly the
blue sky was their roof. On a hill, where the
town of Milparinka now stands, the Commercial
Bank had a tent with a box for a counter and a
water-bag. Layton, now of Sydney, was the mana-
ger, staff and office boy! He asked Conrick to take
80 ounces of gold for the bank to the Melbourne
Mint, and the treasure was placed in a saddle-bag
aboard one of Cobb and Co.’s coaches, the over-
lander going as passenger. Four inches of rain
held the coach up at the Bunker, and Conrick, carry-
ing the specie, walked the last 60 miles to Wilcannia,
eventually landing his load safely at the Mint.
Just passed, at Hobart, aged 70, George
Gerresford Franklin, the Speck’s best-known
old-time detectiveL Franklin’s fine up-
standing figure and strikingly handsome
appearance, combined with his popularity
in sporting circles, handicapped him profes-
sionally, and some of the big fellow’s-
attempts to disguise himself are still the
subject of joy among survivors of the old
regime. But he was all there when he
sighted the man he wanted—no weapon
■would stop him. Since he retired from the
force some years ago. the ex-detective had
worked as a farrier. ■

“RR.” reports some hard-doers J
My sympathy is with the writer uutl artist in the
filing-line every time, (,'tune across Norman Wilkin-
son doing his water-color sketches of Anzae one
morning as we were returning from “Hill 00.” The
dust and flies made sketching almost impossible and
several times Wilkinson almost gave up the job in
disgust. After 10 weeks in a London hospital I
crossed over to France. We had entered a French
village some miles behind the firing-line, and seated
in a small restaurant, writing as if he was on piece-
work, 1 met rifleman Pat McGill. Beside biin was
a pile of copy covered With neat handwriting, lie
was dressed in the usual dust-stained Tommy uni-
form with the Ii.A.M.C. badge on his arm. He
had been in the firing-line since the commencement
of the -fighting in France; had been wounded, once
•seriously, and had written “The lied Horizon”
wholly in the fifing-lipe under the greatest difficul-
ties. The first chapters; were written at Richenbourg.
Here McGill’s billet was shelled by the enemy and
he had to seek shelter .in a cellar. The walls fell
in and the author had to be dug out. The copy for
his book was destroyed. Two weeks later he re-wrote
the previously-last chapters and added a few more.
While helping to resfcue a wounded man he left his
pack containing the MSS. on a parapet and when
he returned Hater the parapet and. pack were gone!
He used all kinds of copy-paper from tea-wrappers
to cigarette packets. Back in Palestine I came
across artist George Lambert doing official sketches
in the Jordan Valley and taking little notice of the
shells from a Turkish gun cleverly concealed in
the hills of Moab. Lambert was very popular with
our mounted men, and several of them treasure
sketches which he presented to them. When the
Huns tried to break through to Jerusalem last
August a motor-car dashed furiously along the
ancient Jerieho-road. Once or twice it nearly
skidded over a cliff, and I was on the verge of taking
notes for an obituary par when I was informed that
the occupants were war-correspondents Gullett and
Massey, hurrying to see Billjim wipe the floor with
the Kaiser’s picked infantry from the Russian front.
DIGGER.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A writer protests in the press against certain
civilians cheapening, by misuse, the sacred name of
Digger.
“Comrade” and “Fellow-worker,” “Brother men" —.
Such names are cheap on Sunday down the Doni.,
Mouthed and remouthed and shouted yet again,
With red-rag tags and sure-salvation tips;
Who cares what grinning jaws and writhing lips
Of ranting knaves he takes such titles from?
All meaning is gone out of them long since—
Dead husks that stir on every idle breath.
But “Digger,” aye, that makes the dinkum wince—
Living and dead that sacred name enrolls—•
They wrought it: on the anvil of their souls,
Red War for hammer, at the forge of Death.
“Digger”—that name’s his own by all that’s true;
As epic-worthy as his old felt hat,
Whose roof was all the homo his wandering knew—
It suits all wasters as some jewelled crown
Suits the patched fustian of a thievish clown.
Let spineless dawdlers keep their tongues from
that!
“Cobber” and “pal” and “mate” —there’s good and
bad,
In spite of Shakspeare's tilt at names, then,
For you might hang a dog with “Kamcrad” ;
But names like Digger can’t be cadged or bought
Like titles. ’Tis for those who fell or fought,
Battered and splendid—men’s own name for men l
N.S.W.

HIS IDOL.
MRS. NAGGE : "Are you drunk, sir, taking
your boots off at the front door?"
MR. N.: “ Nota-tall, in' dear—gorrer new
religion. Mush take boots off entering
place o' worship—worship you, y’
know, dearshest."
The Commonwealth is 18 years old and
already it has paid for the portraits of 19
imperishable statesmen at an average of
£229—19 men whose absence from the
nation’s gallery would make posterity the
poorer. Five more immortals are in pro-
gress, including the overwhelming personali-
ties of Senator Givens and Speaker John-
son. No country can turn out great men at
such a rate without exhausting the soil by
excessive cropping. Also, the rush 'and
pressure exhaust the public memory, arid
some of the marvels of intellect are already
forgotten—in fact, some were born that way.
The pace increases as time goes on, for the
Hughes Government is fast at everything
except work, and its order for five Fathers
or Old Dads of the Country (including
Givens) all at once is a record. There is
only space for four more in the Queen’s
Hail, so the fifth (possibly Givens) may
have to be hung in the street as a horse-
frightener. The show is almost entirely of
politicians who appraise their own immor-
tality and indispensableness, and the indis-
pensables are a poor lot nowadays. In fact
the stream is running dry. At tne beginning
of the Commonwealth there were a few men,
such as Barton, Kingston, Forrest and
Turner, who had a claim to be put on canvas.
They were all constitution-builders, which
might be an accident, but then Washipgton’s
job as a constitution-builder was also an
accident based on the fact that he happened
to be there. Anyhow three of the four had
served many years in State politics with
some success, and had climbed to the, top of
affairs over the heads „pf many weaker con-
temporaries in the process of creating their
claim to be remembered in the Federal
sphere. It took something like 50 years to
grow that little original crop, and a similar
crop can’t be raised every day. After the
first half-dozen pictures at most—perhaps a
quarter Vlozen would have sufficed—tnere
should have been a dozen or 20 years’ rest.
Then perhaps John Monash, who is a rare
personality, might have come in. As it is,
the cheap men of the ruck are inflicting their
heads and necks and shoulders, also their stiff
collars, at the public expense on a public
whose sons . won’t know them from tick-
inspectors.

Solicitor-General Arthur Robinson, of Vic-
toria, who used to attend Scotch College as
a lad, has been fluting about the war-record
of his school as being unapproached by any
other in Australia. This raising of tactless
joy-paeans about one’s own connections in
the war is an unprofitable and irritating
business. Senator Blowhard, rising on the
public platform at a welcome-home to re-
turned soldiers, casually mentions : that his
own son Bill has been wounded seven-times;
and in one of the back seats there sits an
inarticulate widow who has lost both her
sons and has nothing to say about it—can’t
say it and wouldn’t if she could.
Much excitement was created in
a section of Little Bourke-street,
Melbourne, at abont six o’clock
one evening recently by the eccen-
tric conduct of a somewhat
elevated square-head, in sailor garb,
who had taken to a shop-front and
was swarming up it with the seem-
ing ease of a judicious tarantula
from the house-wife’s
slipper. The ridiculous person
went up over the verandah, and
negotiated two storeys after that
via the front-window* ledges, while
Ihe crowd accumulated, gasped and
shouted in the street below, every
man-jack of the assembled multi-
tude satisfied that he was on the
eve of witnessing a hair-lifting
tragedy. But the sailor gained the
top and went over, and then was
reported galloping a north-easterly
course across the roofs. The crowd
followed as best it could, getting
an occasional blood-thrilling
glimpse of the infatuated tar
■sprawling up cne side of a gable
or slithering down another, or tak-
ing the space- between buildings
in a flying leap. At length he was
intercepted and collared on the
tiles of an establishment by an
intrepid John Hop, who had gone
up on his errand of mercy through
a skylight. The mariner offered no
resistance, was brought to earth
and severely interrogated. He was
a Swede, he said. “Eet ees all ride,
I tolt you,” jje protested. “I vos
poart by dot poartink-haowse in
Nigolson-sdreed. unt I half take
der short cut home.”
“Gouger”: a scorching, burning beat
rose from the desert sand all through the
never-ending day of the Bir-el-Abd battle.
All along the far-stretched line the one
thought was water. Could it not bp got
from somewhere? Surely there must be
some little oasis close by where a man could
make a sudden dash with the troop’s water-
bottles. But the Turks suddenly attacked
in great force. Our rifle and Maxim fire
swelled to a quickening roar, and all chance
of a long cool drink was finished. The bar-
rels of our rifles, already burning hot from
the sun, quickly got hotter from the rapid
fire, until it was almost beyond human en-
durance to hold a weapon. The attack was
finally beaten down, the firing steadied to
independent firing, with sudden outbursts of
machine-gun fire, and the never-ceasing,
long-drawn whine and crash of bursting
shells. It was then I began to notice,
dully, the man lying behind a sandhill on my
THE EVENING'S ENTERTAINMENT.
HUSBAND: “I wish to Heaven you'd -turn off that infernal thing! The neighbors are
sure to think we're having another row."
WIFE: "Well, why spoil their enjoyment?"
left. He was continually turning his head
and looking behind him, then facing forward
again, for all the World as if he hated to look
Behind but must. I thought he was trying
to stop himself from making up his mind
to rush back across the desert for water.
Suddenly he did jump up and run/ back. I
looked over my shoulder as he ran, for
I thought he might know of some well',
quite close. But he ran back only 50 yards
to his,, fa lien horse. We had galloped into
action? under shrapnel fire, and scattered
horses, with here and there a tumbled form
of a man, testified to thfe accuracy of the
Turkish batteries. Their shrapnel bullets
were still biting the sand, searching for our
led horses some little distance away. The
-trooper standing by his horse half raised his
rifle and slowly lowered it again. The poor
beast, wounded three hours before, was still
feebly writhing. Its mouth was open, and
the long, black swollen tongue had dropped
between the jaws and was lying helpless in
the burning sand. Slowly the man took his
water-bottle, pulled out the cork, and
emptied the precious contents down, the
horse’s throat. And I had thought not a
man in the line had a drop left! His great
act of charity done, that splendid sportsman
suddenly took off his hat and laid it .--across
his charger’s eyes. .Then, holding the
muzzle of his. rifle steadily for a second in
his mate’s ear he pulled the trigger. Then
he ran back bareheaded to his place in the
line.

The University of Adelaide annual public
examinations indicate that the German lan-
guage has fallen into disfavor "with South
Aus. students since the war. In 1913 the
number of candidates who sat for German
in the junior, senior, higher and commercial
examinations was 264, and at the same
period there were 553 for French. This year
only 68 students took German as a subject,
and the number of those examined in French
was 661. A comparison shows that the pro-
portion of two to one five years ago has
changed to 10 to one at the present time.
However, there is a lot to be said for getting
familiar with the language of a rival, and if
Australians can see any prospective enemies
about they had better commence learning
that tongue. The time seems ripe for find-
ing out what the alien is up to, whether in
the dumping of his goods or men.
Tasmania is trying to give the rest of
Australia a lead in the treatment of its men-
tally-defieient citizens. The Government of
the Speck has imported, for the occasion,
Prof. Richard Berry, of the Mejfe University
A.natomical School, to offer some expert
advice on the matter. Any such sign of
awakening interest is a welcome indication

the Peace speaks highly of Clements Tonic to those subjected to nerve strain.
Here is a copy of Staff-Sergeant Mac Nevin’s letter, written from his home:
v 82 Surry Street, Darlinghurst,
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD. N - s - Wales > 23/6/*iB.
I was so much a martyr to ill-health and bad nerves that I used to “jump out of my skin” at the least shock or
noise. This condition continued until I used your Clements Tonic, and now I have a different tale to tell.
Where I could not eat or sleep, I now enjoy my sleep and meals.
When I was C.P.S. and Chamber Magistrate, Registrar of District Court, and Deputy Clerk of the Peace, under two
of the leading Judges of N.S.W. in various country towns, for many years I relied a great deal on your tonic for mental
and physical energy during the exhaustive trials at the Criminal Sessions when I had to act as Deputy Clerk of the Peace.
After leaving the service in 1893, and following commercial pursuits for eight years, I still looked upon the tonic as
a fine standard medicine. lam now in my 53rd year and am Sergeant in the A.Q.M.G. Home Service Staff.

received from'it, and my recommendation of it to others. (Signed)

Staff-Sergeant,
“G.Iv.D.” from Suva :
Influenza has stopped this town dead. Banks
closed, shops open only in forenoon with few hands,
no deliveries, provisions giving out, banana trade
murdered. The Medical Department is muddling
along, and the Government Offices are closed, the
staffs either being down sick or out helping. Every-
body, white, black or brindle, lias had the curse, got
it or getting it. We lost 40,000 Fijians in the
big nieaslo outbreak years ago. The natives
won’t eat and haven’t the first idea of caring
for themselves- —just lie in draughts or near water
and die. In Suva wc have soup and sago cooking in
cauldrons, and distribute it with medicine; but it
has to be forced down their throats. The
Chief Medical-Officer and others arc running rival
organisations, and every boss is dictating and order-
ing—with the best intentions, no doubt, but not
always 'with the best results. The long-suffering
understrappers are doing what they can—including
the dangerous work near the sick. There
are a few heroes. IV. 11. Terry is nursing 00
single-handed. Charlie Holmes lias his house full of
sick Cable men, so as to give the well men in the
Cable quartets a chance. Half the staff is down, and
the other half is working double and treble shifts.
There are some 18-carat men among these boys.
There are crowds in the hospital, drill-hall, Gram-
mar school and other places. Australia is the only
country which has kept it out so far, and if the Aust
tralians are wise they'll keep on guard. If it gets
into Australia, here’s the treatment. (1) Go to bed
at first symptom, and stoj) there'; (2) take opening-
medicine and your favorite influenza mixture, arid get
to sweating at once ; (8) keep it up and stop in bed,
and stop in bed, arid stop in bed. That’s the whole
cure. I’ve seen it, experienced it, and I know. Inci-
dentally this crisis has driven it home to me that
colored labor is rotten. I have seen our Town
Clerk with double, treble arid quadruple wages in
one hand and prayers and entreaties in the other,
trying to get the niggers to stick to necessary sanitary
work. But the maddening answer is: “Nalii, sahib.”

gance of the Civil Service is wholly the fault of the

Hughes comes back, and Hughes’s comparative silence
seems wholly unaccduntable, unless his voice has
given out.
These mysteries are open to competition.

An unusual touch of romantic pathos has
been added to the story of the tragic death
of the chauffeur, Harold Jaques, of Adelaide,
who, it is suspected, was most
foully murdered somewhere near
Port Pirie. While detectives
were scouring the country in
search of the missing man, Mrs.
Jaques, two days before her hus-
band’s body was found, gave
birth to a son. She anxiously
perused the daily papers every
morning to see if anything had
been found out about the fate of
I' M- husband, but when the facts
of death were discovered she
was very weak, and the doctor
thought it would possibly be too
great a shock for her if she read
the full account of the tragedy.
In order to save the unfortu-
nate woman, the proprietary of
the Advertiser did something
which hasn’t very often been
done in newspaper publishing.
The house printed a special copy
of the paper, first having with-
drawn the story of what had
happened, and this was supplied
to Mrs. .Taques. Later, of course, the news
was gently broken.

In 1911, 171 persons out of every 10,000
in the Commonwealth were old-age pension-
ers ; in 1918 the number was 193 out of
every 10,000. The old-age pensions bill was
£1,871.240 in 1911 and £3,793,037, or more
than double, in 1918. That is nothing to
grieve about, even though some £4,500,000 a
year of war pensions have also been added
and the insatiable baby-bonus grows out of
proportion to the visible supply of babies.
But production, which has to pay the bill in
the end, doesn't grow in anything like the
same proportion as pensions, and it isn’t
given to everybody to be a disabled soldier
or a patriarch or a baby.
According to the Mayor of Adelaide that
town is either the healthiest in the world,
or it is the city of model mothers,
or both, because last year, he an-
nounces, it had the smallest in-
fantile mortality of any city in
the world. During the year end-
ing September 30, 1918, the death-
rate of children under 12 months
of age was only 41 in 1000 births,
which was less than half of the
preceding 10 years’ average of
84.3. Home was
supposed to
have possessed
the previous
best of 56 in
1000 in 1915,
although another
list places
Maoriland o n
top, with 50 in
the same year..
Since the pre-
s e n t Health
Act came into
operation infantile mortality in Adelaide
has dwindled from about 150 per 1000 to
its present low level.
A Westralian newspaper editor, who was
for a year or more in the hands of the Huns,
doesn’t think much of the morals of the
German peasant women. “It was not un-
common,” he says, “for a cynical prisoner,
sent to work on farms, to take the place in
eiery Tespect of the husband away at the
Front. Some of the girls and women had
little above the manners of a cow and the
morals of a barn-door fowl, and to hear and
see them eating was to be forcibly reminded
ox a pig trough.” Hardened Tommies —and
some of them can give granite a start —have
put it on record that the jests and conversa-
tion of these women gave them the sea-sick
feeling. There’s a quaint custom in some
villages of “billing the town” with the
names of erring matrons and maids for the
edification of the liock. The liunness is a
fit mate for the Hun !

A band of 10 returned soldiers recently
apnroached the Collingwood municipal coun-
cil, asking permission to play music in the
streets and take up a collection. The
request was refused. Perhaps this may not
he the most satisfactory way for a soldier to
earn a crust: nevertheless, for regular
wages, numbers of self-respecting citizens
thump pianos or sound the loud timbrel to
entertain the public; and there doesn’t seem
much to choose between a discharged soldier
playing on the; strand and a-musical unionist
pull-and-pusbing the trombone in a theatre
orchestra. Anyhow, for fully 20 years before
the war started a German band tootled con-
tinually up and down the streets of Mel-
bourne and suburbs, soliciting contributions
without hindrance: and they kept on doing it
right iq) fib the day war was declared.

THE BLACK INFLUENZA.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Pains traverse through me in a casual way ;
Flooding along like some impetuous river,
Ceasing suddenly where heat-waves quiver.
The wild deliriums dance and fevers play.
Ilv past was purple, but my future’s grey.
My reason rocks and reels ; my bones all shiver;
Microbes carouse within my heart and liver;
And altogether there is Hell to pay.
Send for a parson, quick, before 1 croak !
Deatli leans against the bedpost beckoning me.
The stranger seems to think it quite a joke
To rock there like some boozer in a bar,
While just outside the flaming window, see!
Satan is waiting in Iris big red ear.
MAORILAND.
BARTLETT ADAMSON.
A CHRISTMAS CAROL.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I am a Christian orthodox
Now gurgling o’er each Christmas-box.
From ninon frills to ivory fan
My heart' exults “Good-will to Man 1”
That soap, Parisian in make, .
Is priced at ten and. six a cake.
A motor-hood in softest tan
Thrills my glad soul—“ Good-will to Man!”
A gauzy scarf for one he tore
Bert sends, and from that old Brown bore
A chain purse comes —each link I scan—■;
Silver right through -“Good-will to Man!”
I am a Christian Orthodox,
in silken hose with 'broidered clocks,
The flames; of Charity I fan.
All men I love. “Good-will to Man!”
JC.S.W.

GREGORY NORTH RABBIT BOARD.
Applications for Superintendent of
Works.
APPLICATIONS are invited until Tues-
day, 17th March. 1919. for the posi-
tion of Superintendent of Works.
Applicants to state age and general experi-
ence in the construction and maintenance of
rabbit-proof fencing and rabbit destruction.
Salary four hundred pounds (£400) per
annum. Camel outfit provided in dry coun-
try, applicant to find necessary horses and
plant for the other portions of the district.
Applications, together with copies of testi-
monials, to be addressed to the Chairman,
Gregory North Rabbit Board, Roulia.
Queensland, and endorsed “Application for
Superintendent of Works.”
For any other particulars apply to the
Clerk of the Gregory North Rabbit Board,

POLITICAL
POINTS
Thomas Joseph Ryan of Queensland is an
unadulterated Lajior Premier, and is sup-
posed to be a man of new ideas. Yet when
he appeared on a recent day before a
Domain audience in Sydney the tunes he
played were rather ancient. He said that
we must wake up to the necessity for edu-
cating the people, so that Australia might go
forward by “leaps and bounds.” Education
is certainly very backward in Australia,
but perhaps not the kind that Ryan, the ex-
teacher of classics, or Ryan, the student of
books about the reorganisation of society,
is accustomed to deal in. The proportion of
people who know how to make a watch or
a locomotive or an airship or any bit of
intricate machinery is so small as to amount
to illiteracy. But no man who is a success
in a long race —and the race of civilisation
is as long as the human race itself—pro-
gresses by leaps or bounds or starts or sud-
den jerks or even by convulsions. Ryan
also stated that money must be found so
that “the vast resources of Australia might
be developed.” “Develop” is a much-used
word and “vast resources” are two of the
same sort.
Mention of Mclver, Victoria’s Secretary
for Lands and Chairman of the Closer Set-
tlement Board, as an eligible for the vacant
Auditor-Generalship suggests that things
cannot be running too smoothly under the
new settlement organisation. Mclver has
heaps of courage and is probably standing
up to Clarke, his political chief, who also
likes a bit of his own way. While climbing
the departmental ladder, Mclver became a
certificated auditor and accountant. He con-
ceived and worked out bold schemes of de-
partmental policy, learned how to handle
men, and championed the claims of diligent
juniors. If Mclver becomes a candidate for
the Auditor-Generalship, Weldon, Norris
and Agg will find in him a formidable rival.
The big battle at the forthcoming
Easter Labor Conference is likely to be on
the question of abolishing selection ballots.
For years the Labor Party has been the
happy hunting-ground of underground en-
gineers and wire-pullers, ' particularly in
those electorates regarded as “pocket con-
stituencies.” With the advent of propor-
tional representation and five- and three-
member electorates, it is argued that al
aspiring candidates should be allowed to
fight it out in the open at the ballot-box,
thus widening the choice of supporters. This
will do away with the bitterness following
all selection ballots. At least one Labor
Electorate Council has sent along a motion
urging that selection ballots be scrapped. It
will be considered by the annual conference,
and on the decision arrived at will probably
depend the difference between defeat and
victory in 1920.
Dear Bulletin. —My theory is that poli-
tics fall into these five categories:—
(1) Brains and honesty. (.So rare as hardly to
be worth mentioning.)
(2) Brains without honesty. (A thing to be cul-
tivated if the owner of the brains is willing to be
dishonest in moderation.)
(3) Foolishness with honesty. (The drawback is
that the foolishness often lasts longer than the
rectitude.)
(4) Foolishness with something that looks like
honesty within the strict meaning of the Act, but
also seems to be rather a strain on the Act. (This
is the sort of thing that leads to Nortongriffithism
and is very expensive.)
(6) Plain, crude fraud. (Costs the country little,
for the practitioner’s career is short.)
I think these about fill the list. —Yours, etc.,
Henry Horsecollar.
In recent debates on the Soldiers Settle-
ment Bill in the Victorian Leg. Ass. strong
complaints tvere made by Laborites because
the Minister for Lands (Frank Clarke)
was situated in the House of Fatrv As
long as the absurd Upper House exists a
proportion of the Cabinet must belong to
it, but whatever portfolios are given to mem-
bers of the Council there are bound to be
complaints. The trouble is made more acute
by reason of the fact that the bulk of the
Liberal party’s talent is in tlio propputy
chamber. Ilagelthorn, the strongest man in
Peacock’s team, was an M.L.C., and so is
Robinson (Minister for Public Works), who
is about the best of the Lawson bunch.
Lawson himself —a plain country lawyer, with
a commonplace mind and equitable tamper
and a fairly strong strain of wowserism—
stands above the members of his party in the
Assembly, but in the mausoleum across the
way he has at least four equals, if not
superiors, in Robinson, Baillieu. Hagelthorn
and .Adamson, all of whfun are strong anti-
Laborites —not fervent Lawsonian*-
UNDER THE WAR PRECAUTIONS
SHADOW.
THE COP: “Yes my child, with my constant

“H.”: The Maoriland idea of depriving
military defaulters of the vote for 10 years,
with disqualification from Parliamentary
membership and public office generally, is
one very commonly adopted in many pro-
gressive countries and applied to many
offences. It goes on the logical principle
that the right of a certain kind of person
to vote is of no account compared with the
right of other people not to be outvoted by
that sort of person, or to be ruled by him,
or to be ruled by the soft of person that sort
of person would naturally select. It isn’t a
punishment, but a reasonable precaution,
like the isolation of lepers.
When the Electoral Amendment Bill was
before the N. S. Wales Assembly a rash repre-
sentative proposed the reduction of members
from 90 to 60. No colder blast ever blew
out of an ice-chest than the one that fell
upon him, but Chairman of Committees
Levy corked up the vitals of vituperation by
ruling the proposal out of order. The sub-
ject would be a good one for a referendum
which could be taken along with the Liquor
vote, but the referendum is like the Hague
Arbitration tribunal—nobody
wants to take the risk. Thus
Hushes, when he was a Sydney
member (before he felt that
Victoria was less likely to
break a cracked plate) and one
of the plausible gang which
engineered the disastrous Can-
berra metropolis at Sydney’s
back door, declared that the
selection of an Australian capi-
tal was not a matter that the
common herd could be trusted
to vote on. And in like man-
ner when a referendum was
taken on the N. S. Wales Par-
liamentary membership ques-
tion. the right of the people to
express an opinion was strictly
limited. In 1891, under
Parkes’s law, there were 141
members, and as they increased
automatically according t o
population there might have
been 200 by now. So for
shame’s sake, the number was
limited to 125. When half
the work was taken over by the
Commonwealth in 1901 it was
uurdly possible to avoid another
limitation, so in 1903 the people
were allowed to vote for eon-
tinuance or reduction, but nut fur reduc-
tion to less than ()'0 members. The figure 9(i
was voted so enthusiastically that it looked
as if people wanted 80 or 70 or even GO.
But the common herd was muzzled lest it
should show its ignorance.
Business firms in Adelaide have been con-
siderably perturbed by a request from the
S.A. Commissioner of Taxes that returns be
ti.rflished by them of all business done with
representatives of firms without branches
permanently established in the State. It
was pointed out to the Commissioner that it
would be very difficult, if not impossible, to
furnish such returns. He admitted the
strength of the arguments, and told the objec-
tors to return the form with a statement
that they were unable to supply the informa-
tion sought. A. E. Allnutt, president of the
Adelaide Chamber of Commerce, drew
attention to the Maoriland method of dealing
with such trades for taxation purposes. The
Act over there requires the representative or
agent of a foreign principal upon entering
the Dominion to declare himself and deposit
with the authorities a definite sum of money.
\\ hen leaving the country the agent has to
give a statement of the business done, on
which, he is assessed for income at 5 percent.
Any difference on the amount of his deposit
is then adjusted. It is suggested that the
Government ought to follow that procedure.
Bjit what about taxing- foreign agents at
25 per cent, on their business?
In the thoughtless, untidy scramble at the
close of the session Vic. State Parliament
enacted that returned soldiers may be
allowed to take-up forest lands —the State to
hand over to the Forest Department a simi-
lar amount of land, probably quite bare of
timber, to keep the timber supply going.
A returned soldier, not being a builder or
saw-miller, has seldom any use for giant
trees on his small allotment, though he
might burn them to clear the ground for
potatoes. But a group of soldiers might be
useful dummies for speculators with money
enough to go into the business, and even a
warrior who has lost half of himself may
be a whole dummy.
There is still a possibility that President
Wilson may receive cabled notice of his
deposition. The Republican party, which
always held that he and his Democrats were
too slow off the mark in the war business,
has captured both Houses of Congress. It
m annoyed a little that the President took
only members of his own party to
Europe. It isn’t wholly pleased with his
“freedom of the seas” pronouncement. A
theory has always prevailed that a President
who left U.S. territory forfeited his job,
and became null and void, and the 'Wilson
party hasn’t disproved that contention —
merely ignored it. The precedent' of Taft,
vho went to Panama without forfeiting his
billet is quoted to death, but it is no prece-
dent, for Taft stuck to U.S. territory. A
strip of Panama on each side of the canal
was annexed before he went there. If some
stickler for rule and line asks the Supreme
Court for a decision on this point Europe
may be astonished to find that it has been
burning fireworks for a visitor who isn’t even
a member of Congress.

JANE’S VOTE.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“An astonishing feature of the English elections
was the eagerness of women to vote. The propor-
tion of women to men in one London division was
100 to 1, while it is asserted that the proportion at
Hammersmith was 50 to I.”— Cable.
An age has passed; another age begins.
All that we counted ours is blown away.
The moral values alter; brand-new sins
Shall wait the newer code of Judgment Day.
In polities there are new tunes to play.
Parties re-shuffle, Progress seeks new goals.
Observe the British women at the polls 1
When Prussian Bill set out to break the world,
With Hell’s death-factories in fullest blast,
lie did not mark the female’s flag unfurled
Nor note her colors nailed upon the mast.
Wildly she screamed ; but how should these things
last
When murder's" red hysteria came to call
And thrones and nations tottered to their fall?
She put on trousers with an easy grace,
Ploughed the long furrows, drove the clanging
tram.
Her brothers fought; she kept a steady face
And clung to truth against the show and sham.
In fact she really did not care a damn
For all the menace of the German horde,
Hut proved her hatpin keener than the sword.
That hatpin was her weapon long ago;
And large policemen knew it to their pain.
She fought with zeal the Ancient British show ;
She made her meaning very clear and plain.
From each defeat she proudly rose again.
Her war was war. She did not mean to lose it;
And so she got her vote and starts to use it.
What of the future? Statesmen’s hearts grow faint.
It was not thus in any ancient day.
What of the tinsel trappings and the paint
That helped the parties in their pose and play ?
Must every grand tradition pass away ?
Well, hardly. Though it isn’t safe to bet,
I’ll risk the guess that England’s England yet. •
Australia walked this path some years ago.
Our women trotted to the poll and stayed.
The garments, cut for change, were there to sew;
And politicians called to dame and maid.
But do we tremble ? Are our souls afraid ?
Not much. The vote Jane casts she never misses;
But oh, her lips, her lips are red for kisses 1
N.S.W.
PAT O’MAORI.

“G.H.”: llow does this strike you as a
sample of love’s labos, lost ? Recently saw a copy
of the New Testament that had been translated into
*abo. language. It is the fruit of about 19. years’
solid labor by an ex-German missionary, who in his
great work was confronted by the quadruple tasks
of first learning English ; second, learning the blacks'
lingo, then translating the book, and latterly teach-
ing the discolored brother to read it. It is very neatly
written by hand, and with the exception of proper
names, which, of course, remain the same, it is the
most incomprehensive turnout imaginable. Unfor-
tunately niggers from adjacent tribes, only three
•or four miles away, don’t understand a word of it;
and as the blacks of that tribe as well as almost of
every other have dwindled down to a mere handful
of filthy, germ-carrying derelicts, this man’s life
work has gone for nought or its equivalent.
* * # *
“Paul Cupid”: Of all the beasts, birds,
reptiles and mugs that man skins in this country
the hardest to separate from his whipping is the
wombat. The koala is a tough enough proposition,
especially that portion of him in the vicinity of
where his tail ought to be; but peeling a bear is
as easy as peeling a fiver off a roll of notes compared
with the process of undressing a wombat. The skin
seems glued to the carcase nearly all over; but
when you get to the part of him that he uses when
lie sits down, as in the case of the koala, you really
don’t know whether you ought to get a cleaver, a
pit-saw or a maul and wedges to separate the beast’s
pelt from his posterior. The only reason I can
attribute this circumstance to is because of these
animals spending such a lot of their time in a sit-
ting posture. I’m real glad I haven’t got to skin
politicians for- a living.
