“Camelero” : I’d like to write a dainty
Christmas booklet entitled “The Charm of the Camel.”
Its lips hang luringly down, smacking each other,
and sometimes allowing a corner to droop down
just as if it were going to say something confiden-
tial. It is only one of the animal’s habits to beguile
the rider into thinking he is as harmless as a babe.
All the grace and charm of the brute, however, is
• seen at its best when it is
either getting up or lying
down. The process of settling
down begins with unearthly
guttural noises and screeches
on the part of the rider and
a tugging at the rope attached
to its mouth. The programme
the camel itself carries out
never varies. At first it makes
a show of protest with many
grunts and deep-throated
noises; then it sinks gradu-
ally on its front knees and re-
mains there for a while till
it has suitably voiced it 3
opinion of the whole matter.
Once it lias come to the con-
clusion that after all it might
be. more comfortably off lying
on the sand than standing, it
arranges its legs some more
and finally sinks down on its
stomach With its limbs half-
buried in the sand. The re-
verse process is equally-intri-
cate, and it takes some little
time before the camel makes
up its minu, then it lurches
backward and forward with a
suddenness w'hich makes the
rider long for the calm of a
hatter would feign to throw the beast a lump of
gristle or fat. and would laugh uproariously as the
go’s sharp-cut head darted out to catch the expected
morsel. “Thought yon had it that time, Spots, didn’t
you?’’ would say the ancient; “but you didn’t.
Never think you’ve got a tiling till you’ve got it.
And never trust a human critter. They’re the
worst critters alive. They’ll give you nothing but
hard blows, then cut your throat for your socks.
Good thing you and me don’t never wear socks,
ain't it, Spots?’’ The old bloke put a lump of
fat with other pieces close beside him. Presently a
big March fly, with a great hum, sailed through the
open door. A sleek little brown lizard by the fire-
place darted his head about quickly in search q£
the noise. The insect hovered a moment, tlio
settled on the old man’s big toe. It took a full
minute for its boring apparatus to perforate the
leathery skin, then suddenly the owner’s mouth
jambed on a lump of half-chewed beef, his one
eye, now beginning to open wide with the sharp
sting, searched out the still puzzled brown lizard.
Lifting his foot gently, the hatter eocked his toe
suddenly straight up. .Instantly the lizard jumped,
a quick thrust of the little head and the big
fly was gone where he would do most good.
Then the old man’s pent-up feelings burst. “Why
the flamin’ blazes weren’t you quicker?” h 1
yelled. “Couldn’t you see the d—d fly eatin’ me
ton off? You’re gettin’ your inside too well lined,
that’s what you are. You’re that darned fat and
lazy you won’t look after a man’s interests at all.
You _ oughter go an’ live with a hemperor, you
ought.” He glared at the small lizard, while it
stared back with bright, beady eyes that never
blinked. “You’re too d —d stuck up even to answer
a man back when he speaks to you,” growled the
hatter. “Anyway, Spots gets your breakfast this
mornin’ as well as his own. A bit of a starve’ll
do you good. Here, Spots.” The big gohanna
caught the scraps thrown one by one. The
aged hermit pulled out an old pipe and began
filling it with the dried leaf of the wild tobacco-
plant that grew on the creek near by. He threw
another angered look at the little lizard. “Oughter
live with a hemperor, you ought,” he' mumbled.

“J.Q.”: “Rarawai” (B. 21/11/’18) must
have come upon a most unusual animal if his horse
won’t deflate himself after a little walk or a short
wait, which expedients have always proved success-
ful with all prads of ordinary lung-power that I’ve
ever been knocked down to. Horses addicted to
the habit have been severely galled at some time or
other by a tight girth, or a girth improperly
adjusted. For the life of me I can’t see why riders
must have the girth tight. It’s a cruel and entirely
unnecessary. Most bushmen with any regard for
their mounts will agree to ride over any country
with the girth so loose that you can easily pass
your hand between it and the body. For my part,
give me a crupper and I’m ready to discard the
girth altogether.

A FAIR COW.
[ OR THE BULLETIN.]
The finest bullock-team Outback
Belonged to Jack.
It. was his language, not his whip,
That made them skip.
Hts curses were his country’s pride,
Famed far and wide.
Uninterrupted was his wit,
Except to spit.
And when Jack volunteered one day
His team did not rejoice;
His bullocks seemed to pine away;
They missed their master’s voice.
Jack feared that he would get no chance
To curse in France.
Without his bullocks he’d get slack
And lose his knack.
lie hoped to come back with each curse
No whit the worse,
And worried lest liis language might
Become polite.
Imagine his delight when he
In charge of mules was set!
He learnt a new vocabulary
Of muline epithet!
With care he mastered all the rules
For slanging mules.
When he came back —so went his dream—.
Upon his team
He’d try them out, those insults cool
That nark a mule.
•lack’s fame so far and wide was spread,
A major said:
“This cursing mules docs little good,
No proper practice gives;
This man upon a camel should
Unloose his adjectives!”
So Jack became in deserts drear
A camel ier.
And he could stand an hour or so,
And let it go,
And still find stinging epithets
For these sweet pets.
But when the camels merely yawned,
On Jack it dawned
That words that would a bullock pain,
And make a mule feel wild,
Upon a camel fell in vain—
Jack merely spat and smiled.
They heard with bland, unruffled brows
These words, “You cows!”
But Jack translated every terse
And bitter curse.
In Gippo language curses seem
More like a scream;
And when he their own language spoke
Their great hearts broke.
And C.O.’s said admiringly,
“Not in the universe
Is there a man so skilled as he
At insult, damn and curse!”
But then the mean and spite/ul Turk
Spoiled all Jack’s work.
And Jack became, in Galilee,
A casualty.
And when he woke and saw a nurse
He didn’t curse.
He thought he’d take his curses home,
No more to roam.
“Shell-shock!” the doctor sadly said,
“But he will not succumb!”
Jack tried to speak, and shook liis head.
Shell-shock had left him dumb!
When he came home they handed back
To silent Jack
His now lethargic bullock team —-
His cherished dream !
But no, they wouldn’t move to dumb
Opprobrium.
When on his fingers deft and terse
He hurled a curse,
They never stirred. The whip plied he.
They gazed in mild surprise.
And Jack, the teamster, wept to see
Mute anguish in their eyes!
Jack waved to each poor, suffering brute
A farewell mute.
For his dad’s farm he took the track,
And so came back.
And all those words within his head,
Not to be said !
fit didn’t matter much to Mum
That he was dumb!)
Though he had still his strong right arm,
Jack wasn’t much good now,
Except at mooching round the farm
Or bailing up the cow.
One evening Jack went round the ..plot
To bail up Spot.
She was in a contrary mood —-
In fact, qnite rude.
And when a piebald cow gets thus
Cantankerous,
The things to say would almost choke
A wowser bloke;
knd, thinking of Fate’s last affront,
Jack wiped his fevered brow;
You know the words a chap would want
In bailing up a cow!
The hundred clods he hurled at Spot
Availed him not.
He called her, inwardly, a fool,
A blessed mule,
A camel; and he conned each word
That he had heard.
He even thought that Spot wasVnow
A fair blank cow !
Then as her flank he vainly kicked
He saw a sheet of flame;
And in his thorax something clicked—
The dammed-up torrent came!
Upon that most astonished cow
Descended now
The words that made a bullock leap
And C.O.’s weep,
Invectives that made proud mules blink
And camels shrink,
In one hot blistering lava-flow
He let it go!
IJis adjectives, all flaming hot,
The cow’s composure spoiled;
So shell-shocked was astonished Spot
The milk she gave was boiled !
N.s.w.

“jjor’-West”: “Loo Lear” (B. 12/9/T8),
writing about different building linbit.s of Austra-
lian birds, mentions the swallow as a master mason*
The swallow is certainly a mason, but by no means
a master. The master mason of the swallow family
is the little fairy martin, which builds a bottle-
shaped nest of mud beautifully put together, while
the swallows humpy is rather a slovenly contrap-
tion that falls to pieces almost at a touch. The
fairy martin likes to build in a railway culvert,
road tunnel, under an overhanging bank or under
the huge knobs of our river gum-trees. Some of
our very neatest nest constructors are missing from
“Leo Lear's” list, notably the flycatcher tribe, of
which the white-shafted fantail’s and wagtail’s nests
are models of dainty construction. The fanlail’s
nest, cup shaped, has a chimney at the wrong end
and is constructed of soft bark, hair and wool
covered outside with cobwebs. Willie Wagtail’s
dainty little residence is put together on the same
lines minus the chimney. Mrs. Willie is a non-
chalant and confiding little customer, and places
her nest at times in some peculiar situations. I
found one placed in the hollow top of a corner-post
right in the main street of a N.S.AV. village; another
in the fork of a root some distance down an old
shaft; while another Mrs. Willy perched her humpy
on the intersection of two acetylene-gas pipes under
the verandah of a pretty big Western village store,
where she raised two broods in one year.
“Thomas”: Now that the reptile season is
here the following advice in the event of a snake
tearing into you should be useful. It comes from
!’’• A. be Souef, Director of the Perth Zoo. First,
says he, ligature if possible above the part, then
open the wounds made by the fangs with a sharp
pocket-knife or lance and promote bleeding by all
possible means. Sucking is not to be recommended
as a rule, and never if one has a sore mouth. The
atest investigations on snake venom have shown
that permanganate of potash (Condy’s crystals)
haie a destructive action on the poison, and in case
of a bite from a poisonous snake, after ligaturing
and scarifying or cutting out the wound, dry per-
manganate of potash should be applied freely right
to the bottom of the wound, as it will destroy all
the poison with which it comes into contact. After
a snake lias bitten the marks should be examined,
and if two small punctures are visible it is safe
to assume that the snake is venomous. If three
or more marks are seen on each side, the snake is
not poisonous. To tell a venomous snake from the
non-venomous one, open the mouth with two small
pieces of stick, after killing it, and if there is only
one large fang on each side of the top jaw
the snake is poisonous, but if there are a number
of. small teeth of the same size the snake is not
poisonous. The ordinary carpet and the green
snake are not poisonous, but sometimes these names
are applied to poisonous species. Now, you know
what to do.

“Jacko”: I’ll gamble in favor of Reg
Norris, a western (N.S.W.) railway worker, as a
record-breaker in the way of wheat loading. At
Grenfell the other day he amused himself by loading
two 40-ton trucks and one 15-ton truck with full
hags of grain, and to fill in time for the remainder
of the eight hours he slung on eight tons of chaff.
The wheat was actually carried from the stack to
the truck and placed in position, while the chaff
was lifted directly from a waggon on to the line.
And all this with the thermometer about 102 deg.
in the water-bag.
\\ cri'imee : It is not all beer and
skittles being a rouseabouts' cook at shearing-sheds,
for when the rouseabouts are “found” by the
station the cook has to please them and please the
squatter too—and they are two bosses with opposite
views of the tucker question. It is customary at
both shearers and rouseabouts' huts to feed and give
rations to all “travellers” who call. This adds
to the mess account. Pastoralists are not unani-
mously in favor of it, and the cook, however de-
sirous he is of pleasing his conjoint boss, is never on
firm ground. When he applies for the position of
shearers’ cook he is judged by his table- and his
hoard-rate; and in the other hut the rouseabouts,
when “found,” consider only the table, whilst the
sqn.itlei, ns a rule, looks seriously at his previous
mess accounts. In slack times there are more trav-
ellers than usual, and the eopk finds it impossible
to keep down expenses, (t is sought to distribute
the callers fairly between the two huts; but it
otten happens that one hut gets more than its
share, and then the cook in that, hut says nasty
things about his brother over the way. The
shearers’ cook does well, and he can drive from shed
to shed with his buggy and pair, but his rough
hoarding-house requires good management and
causes him a good deal of worry.

“Melaleuca” : Birdologists have been argu-
ing for years over the little penguin that is to be
found round the coasts of Australia and Tas-
mania. Some authorities who are not authori-
ties contended that there were two species,
the “little penguin’’ and the “fairy penguin.’’
Others who thought they knew better said that
there was only one species. The argument appears
to be drawing to a close. After the murder of
many of the “vicebills” (I’ve been bitten by ’em),
the taking of innumerable measurements and even
a report by a well-known sawbones on their Wood-
heat, the decision appears to be that the penguin
tribe is in the midst of a transitional period, and
that probably in years to come two distinct clans
will be discernible. At present, at any rate, they'
can be classed as one.

“Silas Snell”: This is a hard-luck story,
the true tale of an old fellow named Walk-right, a
veteran miner who, half-beaten by phthisis, was living
alone in a hut a few miles out of Eaglehawk.
Charity provided his main support, but after summer
rains the ancient contrived to gather a little gold
specking along the creek. His eyesight was not too
good, and the sunlight had to be keen after a shower
to enable him to pick out the shining atoms. He
became a familiar object in the district, with an old
sack over his head, a ball of clay in on e hand and a
knife-blade in the other, peering into the clay-bank
of the creek through broken spectacles for the
particles of gold washed clean by the tains. One
morning, while Walkright was thus engaged, the life
was almost driven out of his feeble body by the glare
of clean gold from an exposed surface as large as half
a crown. The old man’s knife-blade snapped when he
tried to move the treasure. Digging wildly with a
stick, Walkright unearthed a magnificent nugget,
SOoz. if a grain. Almost mad with excite-
ment, the fossieker hid his find in his shirt and
carried it to his hut. That day he hobbled into
Eaglehawk and laid his wonderful nugget on the
table before a local bank-manager, offering it for
sale. The banker examined the gold and then spoke
to a clerk, and while the story of the find was being
told a constable entered and dropped the claw of the
law on Walkrighfs shoulder. Walkright had dreamed
of final days of comparative ease on the strength of
the £3OO his nugget was to bring him. He got two
teais gaol, a trifling- allowance for his offence, but
the Judge was merciful to old age, however recreant.
Walkrighfs nugget, stained with iron, thin clay
washed into its interstices, was realistic enough to
deceive any ingenuous observer who did not seek to
pry into it; but it was not gold. It was nothing but
a hunk of lead carefully veneered with two or three
layers of foil. Walkright had served seven months
when the story of his “crime” reached the ears of an
ex-resident of the district, who remembered tipping
the bogus nugget, with other rubbish, down the bank
of the creek when on the eve of moving from the
locality. This corroboration gave strength to what
had been regarded as a puerile and impudent defence,
and Fate played another dirty trick on old Walkright.
She liberated him from gaol, a little demented, and
he spent th e last few months of his life, finding half-
bricks in Yellow Creek and hobbling off to Bendigc
banks with them, demanding four pounds and half a
crown an ounce for his lovely nuggets.
Ragar : There’s a small black hornet up
-Sfiih ( he s about half an inch long) who carries in
his business-end the concentrated venom of the Devil’s
divorced wife. The nest is about the size of a two-shilling
piece, umbrella-shaped and grey in color. I barged
into one the other day while going through the bush,
and the occupier buzzed out and injected some
essence of lfell that made me do a record mile. I’ve
only once struck anything more convincing in
Die hornet line, and that was in Zululand.
lnle scouting with three other mounted men we
dislodged a grey-colored arrangement like a young
balloon. Black hornets, two inches long, came out
or '* in a r; l°ud. We were all punctured before the
nest reached the ground, and did a record get-away,
our mounts being terrified by the stings and language’.
I was stung on the trigger-finger as I held my arm
across my face for protection, and it was over a
week before that finger was down enough to crook
inside the trigger-guard again.
Hay': Those unaccountable footprints
described by “Myall” (B. 14/3 l/’l 8) in the St.
George district of Bananaland were probably the
work of a black nature-faker, if Binghi is still
about there and has the same characteristics as his
Gem rah ah brother. These blacks are very expert
and spend many an hour squatting on the ground
imitating the tracks of all sorts of live things from
a lizard to a kangaroo. Two or three dabs with
fingers or palm, and perhaps a finishing touch with
a bit of stick, and an impression of a footprint is
presented that .would deceive a practised tracker.
In this pastime, as well as in his other art work,
Binghi was apt to draw on his imagination for
grotesque exaggeration, and a six-toed emu track'
would be quite enough to raise a roar of laughter
from the audience.
DUE TO-DAY.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little den Jim called his own
M ith roses round the window-sill
Is furbished up, an’ Mum alone
Has been allowed his vase to fill
With mignonette and white fox glove—
The flowers that he Used to love.
An’ in the oven apple-pie,
With quaint dough-flowers round the rim,
Is bakin’, an’ I chanced to spy
Mum makin’ almond-rock for him—
He’s coinin’ to us from the war.
The train is due at half-past four.
An’ ere we help him climb the stairs
A shy bush girl, as a surprise,
W ill run unto him unawares
An' kiss him right before our eyes,
Foretellin , what he’s ’fraid to guess,
Her answer, when he asks, is “Yes!”
An’ dear oid Dad —a bunch of nerves—•
AVill drive him from the station, for
lie is a hero, too, who serves
At home, and does his bit to score
Success for lads who’ve been away
Like our Anzac who’s due to-day.
Though Jim is maimed we know that he
AVill ne’er regret his fighting days,
An’ through the peaceful years he’ll see
That love can spread its warming rays,
And all that joy and life can bring
Are his for happy squandering.
VICTORIA.

The Soul of the War, John Masefield's Gal-
lipoli, Walpole’s The Dark Forest, Sydney
De Loghe’s The Straits Impregnable, the
purely war poems of Leon Gellert—the two
latter having chiefly an Australian interest;
and many excellent short-stories and poems.
The Russian Revolution, it is now clear,
was arranged and executed solely for the
benefit of the Russian novelists. Since Dos-
toevski’s death things have been very slow
for the Russian novelist. Tchekov showed
that in his plays and stories; but now that
chaos has come again to Russia the Russian
literary renaissance will arrive. It is a
saddening reflection to make that the sole
use of the Bolsheviks is to supply the plot
and background for another immortal The
Brothers Karamazov. Lenin and Trotsky
aren’t real at all; they seem suspiciously
like the heroes invented by Dostoevski.
There is still to be mentioned the most
important credit result of the war. That is
the Billjim yarn. It sets a new standard in
humor; and, thanks to Billjim’s popularity
overseas, it threatens to set a new standard
of humor for' the world. Some day—we may
not see it, but our children’s children will —-
a dinkum Bill jim yarn will get into London
Punch.
So the war will be worth while, after all.

The Hilder Book
.T. J. Ililder died young, but left a deep
and lasting impression on Australian Art.
This fine book is his memorial; and to say
that it is an adequate memorial is high
praise indeed. There arc twenty-nine col-
ored plates, in addition to the decorations
of Dorothea Maekellar’s poem, “My Coun-
try,” and the volume is further enriched by
fifteen pcn-and-pencil drawings and a few
good photographs. The color-printing re-
presents the high-water mark of such effort
in Australia, and is probably equal to any-
thing done overseas; in fact, if in the rapid
increase of book prices, this work were to
go to five times its present selling value it
would still be well worth the money. Ililder
has many lovers, but the number is bound to
increase wherever this collection of plates
goes. It is a whole Ililder gallery bound
up with such reading matter as gives a
clear and vivid picture of his personality.
Julian Ashton contributes the introduction;
but the brief and interesting biography is
from the pen of Bertram Stevens, who, with
Sydney TJre Smith, is responsible for com-
piling and editing the book. The artist’s
life story was short and without much that
was striking or unusual; but the biographer
has done well with the material at hand;
while the short notices of the man as he
appeared to his brother artists, by Harry
Julius and Sydney Ure Smith, make him
live in the pages. A full list of the Ililder
paintings, with their dates of production, is
given at the end of the volume. There is
an introductory scrap of verse by Ernest
O’Ferrall, very simple, tuneful and like the
artist’s own water-colors in its effect. Of
the pictures themselves it is unnecessary to
speak. No modern artist is sure of his
place until time gives its perspective; but
the general effect of the work of the-young
Australian is well summed up in a letter
from a friend which Julian Ashton quotes:—
Often, in a. disturbed mood, wrapt in black
thoughts, I go to our National Gallery and sit in
front of the “Holder’s,” and by and -bye 1 come
away filled with peace.
The editors and publishers are to be con-
gratulated greatly on the appearance of the
book. It is a far step forward in art pub-
lication ; and it would be hard to discover a
blemish. (The Art of J. J. Hildcr. Angus
and Robertson, Sydney.)
Art in Australia
The fifth number of Art in Australia is
well up to the high standard set by the four
preceding numbers. It is a pleasure to
handle these books and to reflect that, wher-
ever they may go, they must awaken or re-
vive an interest in and respect for Aus-
tralian art and artists. As art publications
they will command attention anywhere. The
editors’ taste is as sure as the craftmanship
of the block-makers and printers, and the
contributors, knowing their subject, always
contrive to be interesting and informative.
The present issue contains 21 illustrations
(14 of them in color) and 11 articles. The
artists represented are L. Bernard Hall,
E. GruneV, Rupert Bunny, W. Hardy Wil-
son, 11. Van Itaalte, M. J. MacNally, E.
Phillips Fox and Hugh Ramsay. Norman
Lindsay signs an appreciation of Gruner’s
cool little masterpiece, “Morning Light,”
now fortunately in N. S. Wales National
Gallery. The accompanying production of
the picture fairly transmits the bright
morning shimmer that the observant painter
got on to canvas at last after many cold
hours at his country hermitage. Lindsay
illustrates Gruner’s tireless watch on Nature
with this story
Seeing Gruner away on a railway trip once, I
said to him: “What about something to read on the
way?” “No, thanks,” said he. “I prefer to watch
the sky.” There you have the creed of the landscape
painter, and of a religious fervor deeper than any
formula of a belief in Heayen.
Lionel Lindsay, always a bright commen-
tator on art matters, contributes an article
on L. Bernard Hall, Director of Melbourne
National Gallery, and another on H. Van
Raalte, A.R.E., an etcher of note, who re-
cently settled in Western Australia. Norman
Carter supplies the note on Bunny’s work,
L. Bernard Hall writes of the Connell Col-
lection in the Melbourne Gallery, and car-
toonist Harry Julius naturally goes for the
character note in celebrating M. J. MacNally,
water-colorist and good fellow. Bertram
Stevens writes on Hayley Lever, Will
Ashton and while C- Lloyd
Jones has a note on art and the war. But
perhaps the pleasantest thing in the issue
is W. Hardy Wilson’s description of an
artist’s day entitled “On Richmond Bridge.”
The essay is accompanied by reproductions
of two of his own water-colors, and gives
the impression of having’ been done as care-
fully as a Hardy Wilson picture—and that
is very carefully indeed. The quiet charm
of it will entice the reader back before he
puts No. 5 on the bookshelf alongside its
four elder brothers —or as many of them as
he is lucky enough to possess. (Our copy
from Angus and Robertson.)
The Inky Way
THE CRITICS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
EDDYSON.
I had five placid critics once
Whq would not treat my books
As if I were a hopeless dunce,
And one of these was Brooks.
We fell out over old-time scenes—
The churches’ tricks
With heretics —
And now he gives my novels beans.
Biggs was a critic who was kind,
But foolishly one day
I hinted that his splendid mind
Had gone a bit astray.
I said Grid had a mental squint,
And now these twain
With great disdain
Treat anything I dare to print.
Still were a couple left who found
Some virtue in my page.
By chance I put Smith’s horse in pound—
He left me in a rage.
And Boland treats my volumes fine
With bitter hate.
It seems of late
His wife has fallen out with mine.
At length whate’er my book may be
These critics fine and fit
Will seize on it most greedily,
And box my ears with it.
Yet I am not extremely pained;
Five friends I’ve lost
At little cost,
Five constant readers I have gained !
John McLaren, a frequent contributor to
The Bulletin under the pen-name of
“McNorth," lately descended from his cocoa-
nut plantation at Cape York (Q.) with a
couple of tropical novels included in his
luggage. The first of them, entitled '‘Red
Mountain.” which was completed in 20 days,
was wrenched from the nervous author by
the N. S. Wales Bookstall Co. The second
yarn, which is larger in size and achieve-
ment, is now in the hands of Cassell’s, and
will probably be published as soon as the
price of paper makes the proceeding profit-
able. Somewhat encouraged by these suc-
Jcesses, McLaren has concealed himself in
Melbourne to release another romance im-
prisoned in his brain.
“Jaeko”: The mention of Bendigo Inde-
pendent in the “Inky Way” (B. 5/12/T8)
recalls that the sheet came out in improved
form on September 15, 1862, with the cry
that it was the first morning paper in Aus-
tralia published at a penny. I have a bound
bundle of the 1862 numbers, and novice that
the imprint makes Thomas Wilton responsi-
ble for any accidents, with service accept-
able at the Commercial Printing Office,
Williamson-street, Sandhurst, “in the colony
of Victoria.” For that time the Indepen-
dent was a decent-sized journal, having five
10-inch 13-em columns with a copious supply
of “quacks” and a standing “house” column.
The reptile Advektisek seemed to provide
most of the argument for the editorial effer-
vescence.
A Satchel of Books
Long Live the King, by Mary Roberts Rinehart
(John* Murray, London). The king is 10 years old,
with very strong leanings to democracy and some
inclination to piracy. There is a good plot, with
Court intrigues and other matters mixed up in it;
and the small-boy monarch is good enough and
human enough to deserve release from his royal
bondage.
From the Australian Front, Christmas, 19FI (Cas-
sell and Co., London). This is a particularly fine
volume of war pictures and photographs. There is a
brief introduction by General Birdwood, dated France,
September, 1917 ; and the whole book breathes the
real spirit of the wav as Australians saw it. The
photographs are reproduced from official negatives
taken by Capt. F. Hurley, Lieut. E. Brooks. Lieut.
H. F. Baldwin and Lieut. G. 11. Wilkins. Will Dyson
contributes a couple of fine pictures, “Cook” and
“One of the Old Platoon,” and Daryl Lindsay, I.
Picking, Stuart Shaw, S. Perks, Alf Saville and John
P. Davis supply drawings of varying merit, but all
redolent of the great game. But the photographs,
even more than the work of the artists, keep a pei -
manent memorial of the days before the German
resistance broke. They are better than anything the
picture-shows have brought us for vivid detail. The
cover-design is by Fred Leist. A book to get hold
of before the supply gives out. The sales are for the
patriotic funds.
Gudrid the Fair, by Maurice Hewlett (Constable
and Co., London). This is a rather slight yarn of
the discovery of America by the Norsemen. It is
based on the old Saga; and, as far as it goes, has
some value as history. A few pictures are well
enough painted to remain in the memory; but the
people and their doings are not particularly inter-
esting.
Dogs and Men, by W. J. Carroll (Ross’s Book
Service, Melbourne). Some very poor little essays
that make an heroic effort after humor, and some
surprising drawings that are much more humorous
than the artist meant them to be.
An Australian in the R.F.A., being Letters and
Diary of Adrian Consett Stephen (Angus and
Robertson, Sydney). One of the most brilliant young
Australians of his generation, a man of fine literary
and social gifts fought with the British army in the
great war and died after winning the Military Cross
and the Croix de Guerre avec palme. The book
records some of his experiences—mostly the lighter
and more entertaining ones. It is a good war book
of its kind, altogether apart from its intense per-
sonal interest; for the author had the eye to see and
the heart to understand. The jottings wer e not in-
tended for publication, but they have been gathered
lovingly together as a memorial of the young life
Austraiia lost in paying the price of victory. If we
had nothing but this record we might from it alone
form some estimate of the extent of that loss.
Soldier Songs from Palestine, by the late Private
W. M. McDonald (Edward A. Tidier, Melbourne).
There is promise in these verses and a hint more
than promise. The man who wrote them paid the
price of his devotion to the great cause. His photo-
graph shows a fine, manly trooper of the Australian
Camel Corps, There is a man’s ring in the work.

“Hawk”: If you try to figure out how the
man over the way balances accounts, you
are bunkered, as thus: —Income per week.
Dad, rag-juggler, £3 10s.; son, skipper of
fountain-pen, £2; daughter, carbon puncher,
15s. Total, £6 ss. Expenditure : Bent, zls.
fid.; tucker for parents and six buds at
10s. = £4 ; train, tram or ferry for six workers,
10s.; clothes, two suits at £fi each for Dad
and son, ditto dresses and hats for Main
and Mary, 205.; boots, two pairs at 205.,
for parents and two elder nippers, same tor
four■ juveniles at 12s. fid.; repairing boots,
total for boots per week, 7s. Pause here
and take a refresher. We have already
spent on paper 14s. fid. per week more than
the Brown income, and have not mentioned
clothing for the children, underclothing for
family, house linen, lodge, beer, tobacco and
cigarette money, time-payment on furniture,
firing, lighting, daily paper, entrance to pic-
ture show, pocket money, to
Sunday plate and daily war-cause boxes.
Mother and daughter must have a number
of dress items not here mentioned ; and Dad
with no financial fuf lire must splash a crown
now and then in Tatt.’s. Now you can t
squeeze the family expenditure to much less
than £lO a week, not if you sit on it.
The more you consider this problem the
more certain you are that the Browns are
on the financial rocks, and that the bailiff
must be waiting to take immediate posses-
sion ; yet he never appears. How is it done,
in not one, but thousands of instances, and
that by families neither thrifty nor provident?
I have lived with such people, worked with
them and had drinks with them; by all
means known to me I have tried to surprise
their wondrous secret and failed. When I
prove that the Browns’ financial smash can
only be a question of days, Mrs. _ Brown
appears in a new hat that my missus is
positive cost at least 40s. ; or the family
lias a new piano on the hire-system; or a
party to entertain Mary’s new boy.
* vr * *
South Australia, which had hardly any
rain at all in September or November, is
making up its mind to a wheat harvest of
something under 10 bushels, instead of over
12 as last season, with a total yield of 20
millions as against 2S. The record for the
State was the year before, with 164 and
454. The area sown with wheat is slightly
but steadily decreasing, owing to the double
uncertainty of getting the labor for harvest-
ing, or a proper market for the grain when
harvested; also, sheep are becoming more
popular with the small farmer. Thus the
acres under wheat have come down from
about 3i millions in 1915-6 to 24 this
season, and a lot of those are cut for hay.
* * * *
“Melos”: Timber-getters made big cheques
on the Richmond River in the early days.
In 1855 cedar was worth from 10s. to 12s.
per 100 ft. and a “raft” anything from £5OO
to £l5OO (exceptionally fine ones, of course,
realised many hundreds of pounds more).
The work of felling and rafting to the mouth
of the river, where schooners received the
logs for shipment to Sydney, occupied a few
months ordinarily. Rafters, men who spe-
cialised in this sort of work, linked the logs
together by driving into each an iron “dog”
and attaching thereto a long chain or rope,
and sometimes even lawyer-vines. Long
poles were used for propelling and guiding
the raft down stream. Sometimes a flood
would hasten matters, and take the precious
floatage out to sea. There was an element of
danger in the business, and many hardships.
The flood waters, besides jeopardising the
work of months, often cut off the lines of
communication, thus creating a shortage in
the cutters’ tucker-bags and demijohns—-
especially demijohns. Rum, “Black Charlie”
(tobacco), salt beef, flour, tea and sugar
constituted the necessaries of life. The
greatest drawback to the industry was the
difficulty in shipping the timber. For many
years the bar at the mouth of the river was
a veritable death-trap; in one year eight
vessels were wrecked whilst endeavoring to
negotiate it.
* * * *
“Azac”: Late in 1917 the Canadian De-
partment of Trade and Commerce made
arrangements for sending a trial shipment of
wheat in bulk from Vancouver through the
Panama Canal to the United Kingdom, and
the results have now been published in the
form of a bulletin. It was generally thought
in Canada that the heat of the tropics would
be too severe, and that the grain would be-
come too heated, but the trial proved satis-
factory. The conclusions arrived at were: —
(1) Only sound wheat should be shipped by this
route, and should not contain a moisture content oi
more than ID per cent.; (2) temperatures against
the false bulkhead, and near the engine-room and
stokehold are evidently too high for safety, and
therefore thoroughly ventilated air spaces should he
kept in these places; (3) excepting near the bulk-
head stokehold the only damage done was due to
“sweat,” or admission of water; (4) the shipment
was a very fair trial indeed, seeing that tiie boat
was 3| months on the voyage altogether. Slow ships
are no good for the purpose of transporting grain in
bulk.
In conclusion it -was stated that there
should be no trouble experienced in the bulk
shipment of wheat by this route, provided
that the abovementioned precautions were
taken.

“Halta”: The clever and artistic one-act
play, “Kitty Nobody,” the best thing in the
Tivoli revue, “Iloni Suit,” was not im-
ported—it was written by two Mnorilanders,
Hampden Booth and John Patrick. Both
these young men belong to Christchurch
(uI.JL.) and have been regular contribu-
tors to Tiie Bulletin. They wrote
“Kitty Nobody” with their eyes on the U.S.
market; but the tense little drama chanced
to be shown to Bob Creig, who reckoned it
would make a hit in these parts. It proved
to be the plum of “Iloni Suit,” and the great
success scored in it by Beatrice Holloway
and John Junior showed that Greig's judg-
ment was alert.
“S.”: Never was an undeserving Object
treated with such generosity as that the Mel-
bourne critics brought to bear upon Fritz
Hart’s attempt at comic opera. I saw the
section of “Malvolio” produced by little
Fritz at the Playhouse, and it- suggested
Wagner in a burst of mental aberration
sounding the loud timbrel in the kitchen.
Fritz has taken the Sliakspearean “Twelfth
Night” in its noisiest moments and added
more noise, and the impression conveyed is
one of two people laughing uproariously over
nothing, while two others are intensely grave
over inconceivable things, and a fifth (Sir
Toby) wallows round and trumpets like an
agitated elephant at fixed intervals. No con-,
ception of the Sliakspearean joke is con-
veyed. Melba’s faith in Fritz Hart has
become Melbourne’s faith, a faith justified
neither by his pupils’ handling of Gluck’s
“Orpheus” nor by his “Malvolio.”
An admirable work of reference, “The
Dictionary of the Drama,” was projected and
partially realised by the late W. H. Daven-
port Adams, of England. The first volume,
covering the ground from A to G, was pub-
lished and is an exceedingly valuable collec-
tion of facts and biographical information re-
lating to the theatre not only in the British
Isles, but also in America, Australia, Maori-
land and elsewhere. But for some reason or
other the second volume concluding the work,
although repeatedly promised, has not yet
been brought out, though it is known that
Adams had finished the manuscript before his
death. Another “positive assurance” has
been given that it will soon see the light.
It is now in the hands of John Parker, the
editor and compiler of that useful annual,
“Who’s Who on the Stage.” lie is just the
man for the job—a walking cyclopaedia of
stage lore and theatrical history.'
“Ticket Snatcher”: Anent “0.K.” and
Dickens on the stage (B. 12/12/T8). At
least two other plays besides “The Only
Way” have been produced in Australia in the
past 30 years. J. L. Toole did “Dot,” a
dramatisation of “The Cricket on the
Hearth” by, Dion Boucicault. at Melbourne
Princess’s in 1890; and D’Orsay Ogden
put on “Oliver Twist” at the old St. George's
Hall, Melbourne, in the early 'nineties.
From London: —
The gorgeous historical pageant at Drury Lane,
organised to celebrate 21 years of continuous man-
agement by Arthur Collins, crowded that most
famous of London theatres to overflowing and yielded
a five-figure cheque for distribution amongst ibe
theatrical charities. Australia loomed large in the
ler.gthv and diversified proceedings of a memorable
afternoon. Marie Lohr was “great” as Lady
Teazle in the screen scene from “The School
for Scandal,” receiving admirable support from
Matheson Lang, Henry Yibart and Holman Clark.
Madge Titheradge made a powerful impression by
her earnest and emotional intensity in the drugging
scene from “The Best of Luck.” The veteran octo-
genarian Genevieve Ward received an ovation at
the close of her wonderfully vigorous and im-
pressive impersonation of Lady Macbeth, and she
received the unusual tribute of an enthusiastic kiss
from tlie notability of the occasion, manager Arthur
Collins. Lillah McCarthy was seen in a selection
from “The Humorous Lieutenant,” by Beaumont and
Fletcher, the piece with which Drury Lane started
its career on May 7, 1063, under the Royal patent
and patronage of King Charles 11. The humor of
three centuries ago is not particularly laughable
nowadays. Ivy Shilling was associated with the
veteran K. W. Iloyee, who toured Australia with the
Gaiety co. of the; ’eighties, in a scene illustrative of
pantomime as it was played at Drury Lane in the
period of the most celebrated of Christmas clowns,
Grimaldi. Royce, who is now nearing 80, gave a
remarkably realistic and appealing impersonation of
Grimaldi, Ivy CQ-operating as a nimble and delight-
ful Columbine. Fred Wright was a vivacious Harle-
quin. This interlude was one of the most pronounced
successes of the afternoon. It was on the stage of
Drury Lane that the most perennially popular of ail
British operas, Balfe’s “Bohemian Girl,” was first
produced, and three of its well-known airs—“l
Dreamt I Dwelt in Marble Halls,” “When Other
Lips” arid “The Heart Bowed Down”—were sung
b.v Miriam Licette, Frank Mailings and Fred Rara-
low. Garrick, Kemble, Mrs. Siddons and other
illustrious players of bygone centuries who had
strutted and fretted on the boards of old Drury were
impersonated at intervals, and at the close of a
long programme homage was paid to a bust
of Henry Irving, whose farewell season was
played on the same historic stJige. At the end all
concerned in the afternoon's variegated doings

“M.C.-T.”: In Sydney there has been
strong moral support for Verbrugghen's
plea that the Conservatorium orchestra, a
collection whiolf is certainly not excelled
in Australia for its musical standard, should
not be allowed to drift apart. It is one of
the iinest educative factors we have. To
country students w r ho have the luck to be
present at a Conservatorium orchestral con-
cert it is a lift up from the dead level of
the ordinary species of musical educa-
tion. It is almost certain that the necessary,
money to keep the orchestra together wall
materialise. Sydney is a music-lovng com-
munity. The Minister for Education is—or
was —thinking out the monetary side of the
problem. Private Enterprise may also offer
its cheques to stem the threatened break-
away. Since the signing of the armistice
many prominent members of this magnifi-
cent orchestra have been “approached” with
the prospect of remunerative engagements.
Verbrugghen suggests giving his staff a re-
taining fee to hold them. Anyway the
orchestra should be held together.

been an absentee from the Australian boards.
Fuller revives it more or less at the
Majestic (Sydney). The last big “Blue-
beard” production was done by George Rig-
nold at Her Majesty’s, Sydney, in 1892-93.
Maggie Moore was principal boy; Nellie
Napier. Fatima; George Walton. Bluebeard:
and Lionel Rignold, the dame. Jimmy Ilaz-
litt, now at the Criterion (Sydney), was
stage-manager. Joe Tola no (now a Woolloo-
mooloo publican) was pantaloon; and Cyril
Keightley was the policeman.

"T. I). Emdee”: Many will disagree with
the dictum of “0.K.” (B. 12/12/T8) that
much of the popularity of Dickens is due to
the stage. In that respect Charles D. differs
from Shakspeare, who is understood much
better when acted. Anyone may read
Dickens and appreciate his fine character
studies. It needs quite a different brain to
grip “The Tempest,” for instance. “0.K.”
overlooked one important Dickens play pro-

“E.”: The actor-manager entertained me
with his idea of the most casual Australian.
“He was an old pro. His name would still
be remembered by Australian playgoers who
have passed the meridian. He took life so
easily that he appeared as well content when
giving recitations in hotel billiard-rooms for
a bare living as when taking down a fair
salary during a long run for which his
undoubted talent was in some measure
responsible. He was a member of a com-
pany with which I was connected for over
two years, during which time we went to
and fro a good deal together. One day the
co. was well on its way to Sydney when Dick
suddenly jumped up and clapped his head
"There, dammitall!’ he cried. ‘I knew
there was something. I meant to marry Ann
this trip !’ Ann was the proprietress of the
small establishment at which Dick always
lodged, when he could afford to lodge any-
where. We parted a few- months later, and
I did not see Dick for over a year. When
we met again I said : ‘Well, old man, did
you marry Ann?’ He looked at me in some
stupefaction for a moment. ‘G —d’s truth
It was Ann, then?’ he said. ‘And I’ve been
and married the other one.’ He had married
the woman at his Sydney lodgings.”

“L.11.P.” : “0.K.” (B. 12/12/’18) says he
does not. remember any performance of a
Dickens play in Australia in 30 years, ex-
cept “The Only Way.” Besides Nance
O'Neil’s too-realistic performance of Nance
in a version of “Oliver Twist” at Sydney
Majesty’s in 1900-01, Charles Holloway
staged "Bleak House” at Sydney Criterion in
1906, with daughter Beatrice as Jo, Johnny
O’Neill as Chadband. Wilton Welch as
Guppy, Bob Inman as Tulkinghorn, and
Rosa Conroy as Lady Deadlock. Frank
Thornton, in 1890, played John Hol-
lingsbead’s sketch, “Bardell v. Pickwick”
from “Pickwick Papers.” Thornton’s Ser-
geant Buzfuz was a joy, while E. C. Cnrlesse
as Pickwick looked as if lie had stepped out
of a Cruikshank plate. The operatic ver-
sion of “Pickwick” (music h>y Solomon, of
“Vicar of Bray” and “Billee Taylor” celeb-
rity, and libretto by Frank Burnand, for
years editor of London Punch) was fre-
quently given all over Australia in the ’9o’s
by Billy D’Ensem, stage-manager at Sydney
Tivoli in 1894-5-0.
“L.H.P.”: Next time you go to a picture-
show take your opera-glasses with you, no
matter in what part of the house you may
sit. You will discover that a motion-picture-
seen through opera-glasses takes on enough
added depth to give it the third dimensional
effect produced by the stereoscope. The
realism of any picture taken out of doors is
particularly enhanced by an opera-glass, and
with eyes focussed on the screen the specta-
tor gets the impression of being actually at
the spot where the action is taking place.
At the age of 43, Frank Gray, who a
While back was a lieutenant, A.1.F., has
discovered that his voice is worth £2O a
week. For nearly 20 years prior to enlist-
ing he sang regularly at Melbourne concerts,
but it was when, as an invalid in Blighty, he-
warbled at a soldiers’ function that he was
"discovered.” The result was a three-years’
contract at the price mentioned. At present
he is appearing in London with Arthur Bour-
chier’s co. in Bairnsfather’s “The Better
’Ole, ' and his song, “Some Day when I
Come Home,” is one of the hits of that
success.
MIS BUKIED PAST
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VICTORIA.
With Jimmie Hogan yesterday
In town I chanced to meet—
• Jim Hogan who could make a play
With two sticks and a sheet.
And act it too. An artist who
Contrived to live, as could but few,
On air and that strange trustfulness
Which rural publicans possess.
lie was a genius of worth,
Evasive, pleasing, slim;
There never was a man on earth
Could duck a bill with him.
That chap could stay or get away,
Just as he pleased, and never pay.
His style with duns appealed to me
As sublimated sophistry.
Alas! he’s spoiled for good, I fear.
Some fifteen months ago
Poor Jim inherited per year
Twelve hundred pounds or so,
And nowaday, where’er he stay,
Whate’er he do, our James can pay.
Possessing money all the while
He has no use for subtle guile.
’Twould break your heart to see poor Jim;
He’s bored and much too stout.
And when the cash they ask of him
lie simply forks it out.
He need not flee nor fake a plea.
“I lived for nothing once,” said he.
“Ah! if those times would come again—
One something had to live for then.”
BILLY T.


INFLUENZA.
“Aft Ounce of Prevention Worth a Pound of Cure.”
/
. / . -v '
It has been truly stated that “an ounce
of prevention is worth a pound of cure.”
The Australian public are face to face
with the possibility of an Influenza
epidemic similar to that which has been
raging elsewhere. It has already devas-
tated New Zealand ■ and is at the very doors of the
Commonwealth. At such a time It stands each individual
in hand to keep a close watch on themselves and maintain
their usual standard of health.
A dose of Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills two or three times a week or one
or two pills each night is advised. It would also be advisable to take from
5 to 10 grains of ASPIRIN two or three times daily if feeling out of sorts,
or 10 grains of QUININE on retiring at night at the same time the pills
are taken. A common cold can usually be broken up if either QUININt
or ASPIRIN is taken with Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills as directed on
the first indication. Petroleum Oils, Salts, Tonics and other so-called
remedies are of little use to cleanse the system and keep these germs in
check; in fact they will have a tendency to aggravate an already deranged
gastric condition. Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills are a searching, cleans-
ing remedy, and will drive these germs out of the system.
Should, however, an attack of Influenza develop, lose no time in sending
for the family doctor, and go to bed at once. This is the best advice that
can be given. Don’t try and doctor yourself. If you have the real Influenza
your digestion will be a wreck, your head, legs and back will ache, your
heart action will be affected, and you will have a temperature. These con-
ditions need the attention of a skilled medical attendant. He may con-
tinue the treatment indicated above or he may not, but it will only be safe
for the person affected to at once go to bed when the first definite
Influenza systoms appear, and place yourself wholly and unreservedly in
the doctor's care.

On Dampier’s Track.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
DAMPIER'S Archipelago, encircling the
nor'-west coast of Australia, perpetu-
ates the name of the famous buccaneer
who roamed tue southern seas some two
hundred years ago, when this continent was
almost a terra incognita to' the world. It is
an interesting cluster of islands, in some
places so closely packed together that north-
ern-bound vessels venturing sometimes (only
by daylight) to make the narrow and intri-
cate passages between them, boiling with a
multitude of currents, require the best steer-
ing skill to guide them into the open waters
beyond. One can picture the bold adven-
turer of the Main threading his way among
these islets in his frail ship, the Roebuck, the
first to enter the now flourishing port of
Nor'-West named after his vessel. Many?
spots are made attractive by Hampier’s asso-
ciation with the locality, even if there had
, been none of the great pearling trade which
subsequently brought it world-wide celebrity.
At Roebuck Bay, on whose low-lying shores
stands the town of Broome, are fascinating
reminders of the famous sailor’s sojourn.
Buccaneer Rock is a dark, outstanding mass
situated in the heart of an inlet; there is
also the creek where the pirate sought further
refuge from the fierce blows which periodically
sweeo over and devastate this hurricane
region of Australian waters. Arid putting
out from the foreshore of the town is a
causeway, obviously not the work of Nature,
but considered by local historians to have
been built by Bampier and his men to facili-
tate loading and unloading when the Roebuck
came into port.
Tradition says, as it always says when
referring to the obscure anchorages of the
old piratical days, that somewhere in
the sand adjacent to the causeway Bampier
buried treasure; but, so far, the secret of the
covered-up hoard, if there be any, has hot
been solved.
Curiously enough, in none of Hampier’s
chronicles is there any mention of pearls; -
indeed, even now, on the spot, you only hear
of them in a vague, general sort of way, and
few people are privileged to handle the big
gems that come from the deep. The Broome
pearler loves to surround his discoveries with
i lie cloak of mystery. Occasionally, how-
ever. Robinson and Norman, the'leading local
merchants, wnose luggers are numerous on
the grounds, permit the stranger a view of
some prize brought in. One, an oval-shaped
gem of scintillating loveliness, lay embedded
in the heel of a shell, awaiting the critical J
operation of release to decide either a flawless ■
pearl of great value or mere baroque. “If it
comes out unscathed,” murmured Norman, “It
will be worth £2000.” Subsequently he
was, able to announce the successful
deliverance of a gem eagerly bought for
the London market at tin? figure men-
tioned. Broome waters have given up
pearls of much higher price, and there is a
story of one, found about six years ago in
si allow depths by a swimming diyer (a
black), which brought the phenomena! sum
of £16,000. The famed “Southern Cross,”
lifted at Cossack in 1874, was a remark-
able amalgamation of nine pearls, two of
them disposed laterally forming the arms of
the cross, and was sold for £lO,OOO. That
there was no doubt of the beautiful and
complex combination of brilliants being of
nature’s arrangement was clearly demonstra-
ted by the manner in which the gems fitted
into one another by reciprocal concave and
convex surfaces, though sceptics still attrib-
ute the pearl cross to cunning artificers.
Then there was the pearl presented to a well-
known Westralian scribe which he hugged to
his bosom until, under the moistening atmo-
sphere of a Broome summer day. it melted
away. He had been treasuring a prominent
brand of pill put up on him by an unfeeling
leg-puller.
Notwithstanding the war, the pearling in-
dustry at Bi'borne has continued fairly
robust, the value of shell produced for
one year of the conflict (1916-1917) reach-
ins: £158.597; while 360 vessels, employing
2064 adults, searched the Ninety-Mile Beach
and other favored spots of the Nor’-West
coast for their submerged wealth.
MUSTARD GOLD.
“Silas Snell” : All Yabby knew there was
trouble up at Biggie’s. Higgle showed it in
his countenance, Mrs. Higgle made it plain
in her fiery rebellion, the little Higgles
manifested it' in their unusual unkempt-
ness and their preference for any place
other than home. The neighbors were con-
sumed with curiosity. Higgle had not taken
to drink, he held his billet, and, to all
appearances, was the steady, reliable house-
father lie had been right through the family’s
eight years’ stay at Yabby. Mrs. Higgle had
not been seen “running the rabbit” to any
noticeable extent, but her stormy interviews
with Higgle drove him out of the house and
stirred the township from end to end. Yabby
was indignant, hurt, not so much over poor
Diggle’s trouble as at the lack of confidence
Mrs. Higgle displayed in keeping the cir-
cumstances of her case from the neighbors.
Higgle explained the situation to me. He
wanted such legal advice as I was capable
of giving. “Yiv heard . how the missus is
playin’ up?” he said. “She’s makin' a
feller’s life not worth livin' with her whims
’n’ fancies, ’n I bin ez good a husband to
that woman as there is livin’. Is there'a
soberer husband than me in all Yabby? Is
there a harder-workin’ husband than I am?
Ain’t I a true husband ’n’ a kind husband?”
I admitted be was. “Wimmin’s never satis-
fied.” said Higgle. “Here am I, her faithful
husband for nine years, ’n’ look how she’s
goin’ on!” “What is it she wants?” I
asked in some perplexity. “She wants me
to marry her!” said Higgle.
“Trooper Bluegum”: Hamascus could be
made a beautiful city. Many sparkling
streams gush out from the sides of the sur-
rounding hills and go rippling through the
narrow defiles of the town. And bordering
the rivulets are towering poplars, also wal-
nut-trees, peach and pear, pomegranate and
fig, with grape-vines in rich profusion. The
Australians mopped up about 20)000 prison-
ers in Hamascus, and then took it easy for
a week, camped in the Wadi Barada samp
ling the walnuts and wine.

Here’s a true yarn from the North Sea
mists. One night it was discovered that
the wife of a famous admiral, who had beer,
aboard in the afternoon, had left her hand-
bag on a battle-cruiser. A boat, in charge
of a midshipman, was despatched witu it
about 11 pan. In the darkness, however,
the boy found himself with his boat on the
mud. He had orders to deliver the purse
personally, but it was impossible to get otl
the bank. After a minute’s consideration
he made some sort of a raft, undressed, put
his clothes on the raft, paddled ashore,
dressed, delivered the bag, returned to
the beach, undressed and swam back to
ttie stranded boat. Conditions for the event:
North Sea temperatures, 11.30 on a murky
night, fair tide running.
“Leonore Gillies” (from London > : 1
strike some amusing incidents in my inter-
viewing job in the Repatriation Dept., Aus-
tralia House. The Australian soldier, with-
out exception, seems to have a store of
humor within him. A digger was in the
other day, and in the middle of a serious
discussion as to how we would arrange for
his wife to proceed to Australia, he re-
moved his turned-up hat and remarked, “Do
you see anything wrong with this?” “Yes,”
I replied ; “it’s brick color.” “That’s what is
known by us as a 14-days’ hat,” he ex-
plained. "We’re issued with these hats when
we arrive in^London on leave, and at the
end of oui - leave (14 days) they’ve changed
color. That’s a put-up job by the military
police to nab unfortunate diggers who want
tj have another look round.”
This happened at one of our best hotels,
the night the news came through that Bill
Ilohenzollern had skied the towel. Most of
the regular staff had gone out to celebrate,
and the management had enlisted some re-
turned Billjims for further active service.
At one of the tables the Duchess of Toorak
sat with her party. Above the din her
wail was heard asking, for the fiftieth time,
“the wnitah to bring her a glass of watah.”
The understudy finally got. fed up and replied
with much petulance: “Oh, ’ave er bit er
patience, old dear, an’ I’ll bring you the
blinkin’ Yafra.” Resuscitation took fully an
hour.
Among the collection of old books from
the Sunreme Court library which lately
came into the possession of the Red Cross
, committee for pulping purposes, were note-
books belonging to former judges, including
lliginbotham, Cope and Kerford. These
were duly sold to paper-mills for 30s. a ton.
In another drayload of books which has just
been delivered some of Redmond Barry’s
notebooks have been found, including his
complete notes of the evidence given at the
Kelly trial. The Red Cross people intend
holding these books for a while, in the hope
of some collector making an offer that will
save them from pulping.
“R.B. J.”: “G. Arden Hill’s” yarn (B.
5/12/T8) about the telegraphist's efforts to
signal the district inspector’s whereabouts,
reminds me that country school-teachers
used to do the same thing, though the word
did not always travel by wire. In a timber
district, where there are still bullocks and
high-level blasphemy, Jones of Gidgee was,
without warning of any kind, met on his
school step one fine morning by a strange
inspector who knew not Jones. There was
a gap somewhere in the spv circuit. Blinkitt,
at the Two-Mile, must surely have got drunk
or dropped dead not to have flashed the
news. But his own misfortune did not
allow Jones to forget that Forby, up at
Rodd’s Creek, must be warned somehow or
other. Both schools faced the same road
along which a mutually friendly bullocky was
just then driving, and it was to him that
Jones entrusted the doleful tidings. Some
time later, when Forby had likewise re-
ceived the shock of his life at seeing the
inspectorial buggy approach unheralded, he
had slipped out with a wild idea of get-
ting word away to Bronson over at Beck-
ville. Presently the inspector looked up from
the. copybooks to listen to the snorts, rattles,
whipcraeks and blisterings exhortations
which denoted the arrival of Bill and his
team : “Whoa—er! Whoa, Ginger ! Woo !”
Bill walked in and reported: “Jonesy down
the crick's in a fair perish, runnin’ round
like somethin’ with its throat cut. Sez to
tell you to 'look for a wire-whiskered little
—— in a buggy any time ter-day. Sez he
give him a hell of a time. Sez you’d know
who. So-long.” He was a good sport that
inspector. He never said a word.
Corboy, the new M.H.R. for Swan(W.A.),
holds an official discharge from the Aus-
tralian 6th Division. Though the Division
never reached France and was broken up
for reinforcements some time after its estab-
lishment, it was almost high treason 12
months ago to suggest that the unit had
ever existed. Indeed the Censor fell furi-
ously on everybody who remotely alluded to
the subject, and some of those who felt the
Censor’s heavy hand held proof of their con-
tention in Government black and white.
“0.L.” : I met an old pal of mine in Mel-
bourne to-day—a lately-discharged Billjim,
returned from overseas. He left Australia
as a husky, big-chested 40-year-old, anxious
to do his bit as near the front line as possi-
ble ; and he has come back a bent, somewhat
downcast, gently-polite shadow of his old
self, with a new plaintive manner that is
difficult to get used to. He hadn’t seen a
day under gunfire across-Channel, but bore
all the marks of a man who had been
through a dozen first-class battles. I heard
afterwards that he had been in London for
some time and had seen the results of an
air-raid just after it happened—children
mostly.
The Commonwealth owns three railways
—the western transcontinental, the unfin-
ished northern transcontinental (the two
ends of it, the middle being still lacking),
and the Canberra capital affair. The two
first are absolutely necessary for defence
purposes; the last is a frill which is only
required to give access to a dead loss and a
sink of political corruption. None of them
pay expenses as. yet, let alone interest; but
the. Federal Capital concern is in a category
by itself, seeing that it doesn’t pay half the
working expenses.

FOR BAD COUGHS USE TAR SYRUP.
HOW TO MAKE IT.
Coughs, colds, catarrh and bronchial
troubles yield quickly to the concentrated
healing properties of pine as found in
bitrate of tar. The most effective way in
which to use it is to add two ounces of
bitrate of taf to a syrup made by dis-
solving half a pound of sugar in half "a pint
of hot water. Stir thoroughly and pour into
a pint bottle. One or two teaspoonsful of
this tar syrup will quickly stop tickling
coughs, and taken regularly two or three
times a day will soon relieve a most stub-
born cough, cold or case of catarrh. It is
also of special value in all cases of throat
and bronchial trouble arising from exposure
to the damp and cold. Care should be used
to get the bitrate instead of other forms of
tar, which are frequently useless for this
purpose. All high-class chemists stock it.
—Advt.

Lacly Edeline—Strickland's death at the
\ ilia Bologna. Malta, was cabled' last week.
On the morning the message reached Sydney
a friend received a letter from her, Christ-
mas cards and a second epistle from our re-
cent Governor’s eldest daughter, Mary.
This coincidence brought the invalid very
near the thoughts of those who had seen her
daily struggle, borne with remarkable
courage, in the grip of a slow, relentless
malady that partially paralysed her limbs
and had almost destroyed her speech as
ordinary women understand that inestimable
feminine weapon. Her insistence on attempt-
ing to preside at innumerable charity and
patriotic gatherings had tangible results.
These meetings, for obvious reasons, bad to
b * held at Government House. And —such
is life!—it was easy to whip up a big
assembly of Best Hats (and lay the train
for subscriptions) under the vice-regal roof
for even the most “unfashionable” cause.
One asset remained to this suffering invalid.
To the last, as shown by letters that reached
Sydney a few days ago, Lady Edeline was
an accomplished correspondent. Stricken
in speech, she could express in the most
fluent and easy style all her thoughts on
paper. These turned so affectionately to
Australia that, while the cable mentioned
her death, her just-opened letters were full
of urgent inquiries re women’s work in Syd-
ney. She died in the beautiful hillside villa
two and a half hours’ drive from Valetta,
which was part of her husband’s estate in
Malta. Sir Gerald and his daughters go on
presently to England, to make Sizergh
Castle, the historic Strickland home of many
centuries, and one of the oldest feudal
castles in Britain, their headquarters.
Papua’s athletic Lieut.-Governor Murray,
who has for some time administered his scat-
tered kingdom by boot —often walking 20
miles a day in all sorts of sweltering heat—
is now able to take some of his official trips
by boat. The new Government craft is
adversely criticised in two ways, first (by
some commentators) as “cheap and nasty, a
shoddy craft”; secondly, as a “palatial
yacht.” One recent visitor to Moresby re-
marks: “It can’t be both ‘palatial’ and
‘cheap and nasty,’ but I suppose everything
depends on the point of yiew. Papua
is so accustomed to seeing its tall Adminis-
trator set off cheerfully on foot, like a swag-
man, that it mildly wonders at the alleged
luxury of the little launch.”
Details have oozed through the delayed
English mails of the wedding of Lieut.-
Oommander Darley. R.N.. eldest son of the
Cecil Dnrleys (long resident on the other
side) and a nephew of the late Frederick,
the one time G-T. of N. S. Wales. The bride
was the widow of Captain Anthony Brown,
M.C., R.F.A., and a daughter of the late
Henry Holdship, of the Sydney timber clan.
Maggie McCann’s visit early this year to
Australia vfras to see a sister who died soon
after she arrived. In private life the singer
who made the Sydney Town Hall ring with
“Caller Herrin ” on January 1 is Mrs. Col-
quhoun Thompson. Worse news awaited
her arrival in England—the word that her
husband was dead in London.
Judge Backhouse, so long chairman of the
Anniversary Day Regatta committee, is re-
viving Sydney’s most historic sport—boat-
ing. On January 26 the resumed famous
regatta will be a notable feature of the New
Year.
Mrs. Claude Macdonald, whose frocking
ixsed to be the joy of race-week parades
(Flemington and Randwick), has been
wearing the white starched linen of V.A.D.
work in London. She has been recently
alluded to as “in charge of a hospital.”
Hubby swung a baton with the special
police in England.
The Governor-General unveiled Wahroon-
ga’s Honor Roll last week. About 200 names
will be the total, and two-thirds of that num-
ber are already inscribed. Dr. Antill Pock-
ley’s son, Dr. Brian Pockley, was actually
the first Australian, so far as we know at
present, killed in the recent war. His grave is
in New Guinea. Since then the Wahroonga
man has had another son killed in France.
It was natural that he should speak
feelingly of the spirited past that gar-
den suburb had played. In fairness,
however, to more congested areas,-it must be
remembered that Wahroonga, way up on the
North Sydney highlands, is essentially the
home of the well-to-do. There was no har-
rowing argument in the minds of its males as
to whether the “missus and the kids” might
go short of rations if Dad went to the war.
A Newcastle (X.S.W.) girl, Queenie
Goninan. was lately married at Wolferee,
North Britain, to Captain Palliser Hudson,
of that town. The' Australian bride was
beautifully garbed in a foam of white, em-
broidered with seed pearls and silver motifs.
White heather was tucked in amongst the
orange-blossoms on a wreath that garlanded
the tulle veil and held it in place. The
’groom gave his best girl a glittering pendant
of diamonds. The Palliser Hudsons are
going to make their home in Wolferee
(X. 8.). For about three years the Corn-
stalk maiden was’honorary see. of Newcastle
Red Cross. Since, she took up V.A.D. work
in England.
The winter-garden of the Wentworth
Hostel—where a former memorable gathering
of the 13th and 45th took place to welcome
Colonel Burnage (of the 13th) —glittered
under swinging electric bulbs the other night.
The Christmas greeting was not so complete
as it might have been. Some of the just-
home warriors of the old Gallipoli regiments
were impatiently gazing at the moon from
the Quarantine Station. Colonel Burnage,
in charge of the official side of the party,
handed * to Mrs. Laseron, from her
sister Susies, a beautiful wristlet watch.
Mrs. Laseron has been a tireless honorary
secretary of the Comforts section, and any-
one who has tried the billet for a few weeks
understands what it means when the work
is continued through years.
Ex-Ited Cross Commissioner Murdoch, re-
cently home in Sydney after doing good
work abroad (re-organising comforts’ dis-
tribution to our wounded) gave a picnic at
Clontarf last week. Ilis guests were about
500 members of the business firm of Mur-
doch’s, Ltd.
May Moore, the artistic photographer,
who hails from Maoriland, has had a mes-
sage from Auckland of the deatli of her
mother and brother. A young sister was
lying at the point of death at the same time.
The horror that is chained to North Head
was the evil agent.
The French-Australian League takes off
its aprons till the end of January—and no
band of Sydney wbmen-workers have earned
a breathing space with more magnificent re-
sults this,year. Miss Soubeiran, who depre-
cates the frequent; “When are you going
away?” hurled at her from every quarter,
will return to France shortly. She gave a
farewell address to a crowded depot, the
other afternoon and enlarged on the grati-
tude of the French for what Australia has
already done. Miss Soubeiran, an ardent
patriot of the country wffiich had the Hun
on its soil for long "years of tragedy and
destruction, voiced the hope that Australians
might see their way to keep on helping
battered France. In this respect, she
pointed to Pozieres. All that remains of
that historic town is a signpost: “This was
Pozierfes .” That spot was the scene of much
heroic fighting by our own men, and Miss
Soubeiran suggests that Australia should
rebuild or help to rebuild the town as an
imperishable monument to the glory of Aus-
fralia’s soldiers.
Neville W. Cayley,
only son of the late
Neville Cayley, the
artist who did so
much to make Aus--
tralia’s birds so
popular, is begin-
ning to find his
course. Overshadowed
by his father’s fame,
he never seriously
thought of his skill
ay a bread-and-but-
ter line till after his
dad’s death in 1903.
Then, to keep the
home fires burning,
he got to work, and
he is finding a good
market for land-
scapes, birds and
wild-flower studies. Water-color is the
medium in which his best work is done. This
Christmas finds another of his books on the
city counters —from studies of Australian
flora —a valuable addition to the lore of the
bush.
Mrs. Joseph Playoust, a French woman
whose indefatigable service to the cause of
her country, through the work done by the
French-Australian League’s headquarters in
Sydney, is to get the Medaille d’Honneur.
The French Government is sending this
decoration, which is rarely given to women,
very shortly. To Mrs. Jewett, a slender
Australian woman who has been twin hon.
sec. with Miss Soubeiran and for a long
time alone in the arduous job, comes the
French Medaille de la Reconnaissance. Miss
Gurney, hon. superintendent of the depQt,
gets a similar recognition.
Gertie Purcell, a slim w’isp of a girl with
a big voice, is going to Italy to study grand
opera and all the trills that experts put into
the business. A pupil of de Alba here, she
sang before a big audience in the King’s Hall
the other night.
Music, to w’hich
the older poets tenta-
tively alluded as “the
language of the
spheres,” is appa-
rently the favorite
medium ■by which
Australians think
aloud. The extra-
ordinary success with
which our girls, es-
pecially, compete for
scholarships, prizes
and medals against
all comers, in Lon-
don or elsew'here,
has been a matter of
world-wide comment
A young Sydney stu-
dent (pupil of
Madame Ada Baker)
has just Collected the gold medal, at the
recent exams, by the Associated Board
of the Royal Academy of Music and
Royal College of Music, London. She
is a youthful songbird, Dorothy Gill, whose
photo is by Judith Fletcher. The girl’s
local achievements are emphasised by gold
medals from Eisteddfod competitions in piano
and singing. At the precocious age of 13
Dorothy passed the L.L. College of Music
exam for the piano. In 1916 she added
the medal for senior pass, London Col-
lege of Music. This accomplished young stu-
dent is on the doorstep of a “career.”
Curator Baker, of the Tech. Museum in-
dustry, Ultimo, had the joy of showing the
French Mission, then in its last lap, what
fine furniture grows in Australian forests.
Made into cabinets, chairs, sideboards, tables,
etc., designed and put together by local
craftsmen, this exhibition (still on view
in the Museum) ought to attract a
stream of visitors. Australian hardwoods are
entitled to a front place in the world’s tim-
ber-yards. The Governor-General, who is
an enthusiastic collector of Commonwealth
woods, will probably move off this bit of the
man some time next year. When he goes he
will do ns the service of being, via his
pet hobby, one of our best advertisers.
Black bean, silky oak —a satirfy wood in its
beautifully polished grain—blackbutt, cedar,
hoop-pine, Tasmanian myrtle. Queensland
maple, etc.—samples of all these and more
have been ma<Je into books at his Ex.’s order.
A unique library of 50 apparently calf-
bound. volumes have been standing, almost
unnoticed in the cabinets at this ex-
hibition. They were so exactly like the
handsome tomes the dux of the school piles
under his arm on prize day that their secret
remains unseen. They are, as stated in
each volume, mode of wood —two score and
ten of then!. When some Scotch neighbor
looks in to have a crack with Fairguson in
his homeland the timber Collector .will pull
down, some of his “library.” and Australian
wood, in this fancy dress, will make Cal-
edonian forestry men have button-eyes.
The American-Australian League of Help
(Mrs. Brittain, president! has temporarily
gone into recess till over the holidays. Before
pulline- innumerable pots, of sweets and can-
dies off the stove the depot brought forth
the old Glory Cook Book. Proceeds for Aus-
tralian Billjim.
Major Norman Clowes, D. 5.0.. M.C., of
Warwick (Bannnaland), was married a few
weeks ago at Weymouth (Eng.),-to a nice
girl named (take a long breath) Vera Eliza-
beth Inglehy Pengilly, of Lostwithiel Lodge,
Weymouth. The bride made the day seem
brighter in her georgette finery. Major E.
A. H. Randall (A.1.F.) w r as second-best
man.
An illustrated travel talk, by Paul Mont-
gomery, late' official camera man wdio fol-
lowed the Y.M.C.A. war-work caravan in
the “East,” was full of marrow. The ancient
towns of Mesopotamia (the lecturer joyfully
announced that their dirt could not be repro-
duced on the screen) and the desert which
has blossomed like the full-blown cabbage
under British occupation, were fully
described iu the King’s Hall. Any romantic
young person in the audience wdio imagined a
coast or “marsh” Arab as a dashing person
mounted on a snow-white steed and clad in
silken robes must have had a shock when
a cunning, unclean face of Fagin type,
attached to still more unclean shoulders. ivns
sprung on the screen. Cullen, C.J., presided,
but he wms not responsible for the Arabs.
The raffle of Early Egyptian furniture
heavily carved and inlaid with mother o’ pearl,
got a brisk push ahead last week. Anyone who
has glued an innuiring nose to the Challis
House window during the past 10 days has
probably yearned to add it to their" front
parlor’s decorations, and Florence Young’s
beguiling “Won’t you take a ticket?“on the
adjacent pavement has weakened the desire to
shun temptation. The depot is be-
hind the goods (given by merchant Mock-
bell) and the War Chest gets a thick wa<:
of the profits.
The Kindergarten Training College had
an evening out in St James's Hall in the
dawn of last week. Peter Board, Under-
Sc eretary for Education, held down the
chair. Diplomas and other certificates of
mtrit to students were handed over to the
lawful owners while musical items drowned
the blushes of the successful maidens.
The Apollo Club had a packed audience to
listen to its Christmas carols in the Conser-
vatorium. The Apollos, greatly depleted
during the war, are getting back to their old
form. Alfred Hill points the way. King
Olaf’s Christmas —with Harry Ferguson and
Ernest Williams as the soloists —was a can-
tata that breathed of “wastes of snows,’ -
while Sydney mopped its perspiring brow.
The choir was notably strong in this item,
but one soloist added too quavering a tone
to strengthen the theme. Alfred Hill’s set-
ting of Tennyson's “Crossing the Bar” —as
a part-song—wasn’t equal to some of the
best-known compositions of this musician-
composer. Henry Penn was a forceful inter-
lude at the piano. Elsy Treweek, a Mel-
bourne songster who is a prime favorite with
Sydney audiences, floated in from the artists’
den entirely surrounded by a cloud of cream
tulle. Her contributions were gratefully
accepted. Balboni, whose vogue iu opera is
not quite sustained on the colder concert
platform, was also on the programme. The
tempestuous tenor needs the orchestra and
scene-shifter to give him big histrionic
chances.
Since the break-up of training-camps, the
Soldiers’ Club has iiad a steady influx of
over 1000 men in and out per day. To give
the boys a rousing Christmas treat the
numerous circles of voluntary girl workers
who help to keep the club’s pots a-boiling
organised various successful raids on their
friends. The Victoria Circle chose last
Thursday evening on which to extract the
uttermost toll for Jinvbill’s Christmas tree.
“Rosnn,” from London :
That pluck# and adventurous Sydney girl, Olive
Kelso King, has been on leave in London from the
Serbian front. She wears the uniform of a Serbian
sergeant, and any number of decorations as a tribute
to three years’ fine work among a most unfor-
tunate people. She says the Serbs had a great hope
that, the Aiissies would be sent to save them, and
were always asking: “When will the Australians
come?”
This summer's white frocks are not
appreciably narrower than those of last
December. But the lines are perpendicular
pleats, flat insertions and long sash ends
heavily tasselled that they may not hit th<’
hat in windy weather. And how it blows
of late! March should.* if its present sar-
torial threat is carried out. see the last-
minute froekor painfully hobbling along in
silk or gabardine bolsters. Narrow— that’s
the idea.
There’s a cry from the Alexandra
pital for sick kiddies- —“Como and help us !”
Like every other haven for healing it’s had
a subscription drought: since the war calls
were so urgent. Anyone who feels that the
world is better and brighter than- it was a
few short weeks ago should send a peace
offering this Christmas to help the sick
children—our own sick children.

Unitarian-parson Wyndham Heathcote,
having been offered the pulpit of the Uni-
tarian Church at Adelaide, has given up his
job at the Eastern Hill tabernacle. Heath-
cote is an independent sort of soul expert.
He used to belong to the Anglican establish-
ment, but got into hot water for taking
sides iu polities. It happened to be the
pre-war Labor party which Heathcote
backed, and he found himself heavily frowned
upon by the Best Pews in consequence. How-
ever, the Melbourne Unitarians were glad
to get him and are sorry to lose him.
Colonel Wanliss goes to Tasmania almost
immediately to succeed Colonel Clark as com-
mandant. Clark has been military chief of
the Speck since June, 3912, and is now up
against the age limit.
