Mrs. Robinson, the young wife of Captain
Jack Robinson, M.C., has died of influenza
in England. She was the daughter of Mr.
and Mrs. T. Garnett, of Waddow Hall,
(Titheroe, Lancashire, and was a great
favorite among the young people she met in
Melbourne. She came to Australia to visit
sister Mrs. Geoffrey Syme before the war.
Sheep-farmer Inglis and his wife are in
great distress. Their schoolboy son has been
missing from Trinity Grammar, Kew, since
November 30. Anyone having news of the
boy should communicate with L. W. Inglis,
Monegetta South. Victoria. The lad is 14
years of age, sft. Oin. in height, medium
build, very fair, and has a white mark on
the pupil of his right eye. He was wearing
a rough Norfolk suit at the time of his
disappearance. /
The Y.M.C.A. Red Triangle allegorical
procession was a most amazing turn-out. >
bunch of beauties from ITer Majesty’s
Theatre was borne along upon one lorry, a
sort of plum-pudding of the globe, stuck all
round with plums of loveliness. What it
symbolised nobody knew or cared—it was
good to look at. Similar tableaux of Aus-
tralia, America, Canada, Belgium, England,
France, Greece, India, South Africa and
Italy were fixed up by the ardent Y.MiC.A.
The war has brought this live organisation
l<> the front. The young man of years ago
distrusted it, fearing it was a nest of wow-
serism about to thrust a hymn-book at him.
The young man of to-day sees loads of
beauty passing by and wonders why his elder
brother was so scared.
Theological students from the University
Colleges of Trinity, Ormond and Queen’s
who have been at the war claim that their
years of service in the field must be reckoned
as student years. But Rentoul differs.
However, pastorates become vacant in the
course of nature, 'and as the colleges have
only been half full, half the future pastors
must necessarily be short of dry theology
and full of practical Christianity. No black
hats can be got from abroad. Incidentally,
what easy times the professors, like Beeper,
Rentoul, Picken and Sugden. must have
been enjoying of late. ,
Alberto Zelman, when he invited several
hundreds of school-children to the rehearsal
of his orchestral concert, made a very
enlightening speech. He gave his young
audience a simple explanation about toue and
the meanings of the works about to be
played. The dainty Nutcracker Suin' was
a great success with the small people. Zel-
man is very keen on improving music in the
schools.
Dr. Frank Keone (lie’s an Anzac captain)
is just engaged to Vera Newman, a niece of
Dr. Frank Newman, and one of the gilded
Newmans, of the Castlemaine Brewery.
The Russian Dr. Klatchko, who was the
sensation of Collins-street for months, has
been sending invitations to his Melbourne
friends to attend bis Russian wedding in
Petrograd. Klatchko married “Billy”
Duckett, daughter of the big iron merchant,
at the Independent Church, Melbourne. The
Russian ceremony is to eventuate in a Petro-
grad church, if the Bolsheviki hasn’t burned
it down. The Ivlatchkos have just reached
the scene after a stay in Japan and Vladivo-
stock.
Jack Sommers, poet and painter, has a
little show of delightful pictures in the
Arts arid Crafts
Room, Clyde House.
Jack, who has a
good fighter’s chin,
did his share on Gal-
lipoli, in the Sahara
and in France. From
time to time, The
Bulletin has pub-
lished some of his
verse, written in the
field. He is the son
of an artist. His

Instead he plays

notable figure in art

photograph.
Former Sister Bett, sometime of the Pres-
byterian Inland Mission, headquartered at
Oodnadatta, is on her way back from the
Front as Mrs. W. P. Boland. Her hus-
band, to whom she was married recently at
the Marylebone Presbyterian Church, Lon-
don, is Lieut. Boland, M.C. and bar. Both
of them saw more than four years’ service.
Hubby is an AnzaC, and won his M.C. at
Pozieres.
Alice McCulloch, daughter of Colin
McCulloch, _ of North Woodlands, who
was married the other week to Sandy
Laidlaw, of Hamilton, will have a well-
gilded battalion of relatives on hubby’s
side as well as on her father’s. Sandy is a
son of Tom Laidlaw. who has been stock-
and-stationing at Hamilton these many
years. The elder Laidlaw married a daugh-
ter of the late .James Thomson, of Monivae,
and a Learmonth and an Armstrong each
picked one from the same flock. Thomson,
of Monivae, by the way. was one ot the
State’s all-wool pioneers. He used to be a
shepherd in Sutherlandshire, Scotland, be-
fore adventuring to these parts, and bis
knowledge of sheep helped him to gather in
four squattages before he died Monivae,
Pirricul, Crawford and Lake Condah, in
that order—and he held them all to the last
gasp. The La id laws are also deeply rooted
in the soil of the Western District.
The golden wedding of biscuit manufac-
turer C. D. Haywood, recently celebrated in
Hobart, looks like a sort of record. At
the breakfast the best man and bridesmaid
of 50 years ago occupied their old places.
The cleric who tied the knot is still going
strong. Haywood is a good Australian who,
starting in a small way, has built up one
of the finest businesses of its kind in the
Commonwealth. He has also been Mayor
of but nobody’s bringing that up
-■'Thinst him.
Two Australian writers around whom the
war circled without touching them, although
they did their best to get into it, are the
James Lindo-Webbs, of Majorca, Spain.
ITubby had a flutter along London’s Inky
Way after leaving Australia, and presently
took a iob as editor of a paper at Mombasa.
British Fast Africa. There he went down
with malaria, and the pair had to leave for
England again. British specialists said it was
a matter of the right climate for James, so
the partnership settled down in Majorca,
remaining there ever since. When the war
began both of them tried to join up in.one
capacity or another, but. the authorities
willed otherwise.
“Bungalook Basketry” is the catcli-
name of a cleverly-written and practical
book dealing with stringy-bark (“bungalook ’
in aborigines!*)—the latest and therefore
fashionable substitute for rafia, tin* price of
which these days is prohibitive. The author
is Mrs. Henrietta C. Walker, who explains
how to utilise this easily obtainable fabric
for the making of baskets, floor-mats, bags
and hampers of endless variety. She recom-
mends this new industry for the physically
handicapped.
In the Emerald Hills the air must have
some special intellectual tang in it. One
quaint; bush cottage in the district lias the
distinction of housing in turn three Vic-
torian women who have made their mark.
Number one was Mrs. Louis Esson, for-
merly Dr. Hilda Bull, a graduate of Mel-
bourne University, and a clever amateur
actress whose performances at the Univer-
sity Dramatic Club arid Melbourne Reper-
tory Theatre attracted much notice. The
second tenant was Mrs. Vance Calmer, for-
merly Nettie Higgins, who lived there with
her author husband. Nettie is a keenly-
observant writer, whose chats on books
appeared, weekly in .Melbourne a few months
ago. The latest and most widely-known
dweller on the hill is Katharine S. Prichard,
author of poems in the Engltstt Bevikw
and the prize novel, “Pioneers.”
Carlyle Smythe, long absent from Collins-
street, his favorite haunt, writes from Maori-
land: “Real bliss is not possible more than
10 miles from the G.P.0., Melbourne.” The
showman is at present in a state of gloom.
The influenza in Maoriland caused all shows
to be shut down —including Alexander \Vat-
stm’s recitals. Alexander promptly departed
on the same oversea boat that carried Mar-
garet Wycherley.
Fur coats are now obtainable in Mel-
bourne for 2d. each or thereabouts. They
represent spoils of war that were not pur-
chased out of separation allowances, and are
being freely yielded as peace offerings to
avoid possibly awkward explanations when
the wearers’ hard-ups return. The furs have
served the purpose of keeping Susie, warm,
and she is anxious to get rid of them lest
they should make things unpleasantly hot.

What a good, honest horse the English-
man, Rebus, is. There may have been a
better one or two handicapped at Randwick,
but if there ever were, this writer couldn’t
mention them. In the Epsom Rebus with
9st. aboard put up a wonderful performance.
In the Villiers Mile, at the opening of the A.J.U.
Summer meeting on Saturday, he did the same
thing again, with the exception of carrying 9st. 101 b.
Not a conspicuously good beginner, he drew No. 1
marble and promptly moved back in the betting
from B’s to 10’s. There were 10 starters, at least
half of which were good-quality horses. Badly
served at the start, Rebus was nine nags back at
six furlongs. Then he started moving steadily
through. lie was seven back at the turn, Syd-
ney Damsel at this stage being well out, with
Ardrossan and Panacre and King Ritchie. When
they hit the straight Rebus began to unwind mm-
self, and electrified the crowd, by running clean from
the leaders at the last furlong. It was a con-
spicuously brilliant performance. Rebus ran the
mile in “37#.” The course helped a, bit; hut a
horse that wins a Sydney Cup, then takes an
Epsom and annexes the next Villiers with 9st. 101 b,
in time like that, is even better than the watch
makes him. Rebus, as a handy, reliable moke, is
going to be remembered a long time. Trainer Jack
Murray was very confident on Saturday. So, too,
was owner Joe Lemon. Lemon has won a fortune
with this prad within nine months.
The Villiers was an exceptionally open
betting race. It was 8 to 1 the field, 10 to 1
bar four. Nobody could want anything better than
that. There seemed to be most money for the im-
ported mare Irritation, trained by W. Booth, winner
of a six-furlong event at Randwick in the autumn.
It was alleged that Dame Acre had been pulled out
in her favor. This page suspects the allegation to
have been a rumor invented by some irresponsible
comedian. Irritation was the conspicuous failure in
the race. She was not particularly well favored at
the beginning, but still not badly. At six furlongs
Bracken had her up in the first flight and kept her
in a good winning position to the turn. Then she
turned on the reverse gear and fell back to the
tail of the procession. She pulled up absolutely last.
Wedding Day put up a great struggle, and so did
Sydney Damsel. The Doncaster winner took second
money safely enough. But Sydney Damsel, who, with-
out a doubt was entitled to third money, got nothing,
the judge bestowing the affluence upon Par.acre.
Panacre really finished third last. It was pretty
rough on the little Damsel who had made a gallant
race of it and pretty tough luck for the tote
bettors. Nor was it the only mistake made on the
day. This writer simply refuses to take the third
placing in the Nursery, allotted to Nautch Girl,
seriously.
If Saturday's running in the Holiday Handicap
threw any light on the Summer Cup the illumina-
tion did not show that Greg, a well-backed candi-
date, would have a walk-over or anything of that
sort. Tie was made a red-hot favorite, and never had
a hope. Garibaldi, the improving and useful brother
to Prince Bardolph, won all the way.
* * * *
Owner-of-Oetigne Tom Stirton has
acquired Biplane. All he is waiting for now is the
evaporation of the ’flu’ and a ship to bring the prad
from Maoriland. Then Biplane goes into Scully’s
hands, and we shall see what we shall see. The price
Stirton paid for Biplane has not been mentioned with
precision. There are allegations of “about 3000
guineas.” If Biplane will stand a preparation that
sum was not over the horse’s value. As Biplane
would not stand a preparation last Spring 3000
guineas shows that Stirton is ready to take a fairly-
substantial risk. When at his best, Biplane was, of
course, a wonderfully fast horse. What his value as
a sire would be in the event of him not training on
is a matter of mere speculation, but judging from the
prevalence of the imported sire and the certainty of
the over-sea animal being still more frequent in
future the value of the locally-produced article
scarcely seems to be in danger of going up all at
once.
“Mujik” : This business of the N. S. Wales
Government appropriating large helpings of cash
from the totalisator and iiorses racing for miserable
stakes is a gross scandal. And what is more it is a
complete inversion of the only possible argument by
which the introduction of the totalisator could he
defended. Bringing the machine into the game to
act as a State revenue-collecting agency, and main-
taining all the old-established outfit of Ike and pro-
prietary clubs and paltry stake money for owners,
only makes putrid what was rotten before. Can
Premier Holman name a single individual or paper
which advocated the introduction of the totalisator
into N. S. Wales that asked for this or anything
like it ? The case for the machine all along rested
upon the contention that it provided a rational
substitute for Ike and that from the proceeds stakes
could be materially increased instead of the money
left behind by the public going into Jerusalem.
There was the argument, it is true, that the machine
could be made a source of public revenue, and so
it should, but no one was ever mad enough to yell
for the Government to make that the first and
only consideration, and leave all the others go hang.
In M.L. the local Ike carries on precariously in a
d'ugou’t. There the division of the cash between
racing and the State is on the reverse scale to
that insisted on in Sydney. And there the stakes
of tinpot clubs are incomparably greater than is
given in N. S. Wales, except by the A.J.C. Perhaps
it doesn't matter. The scandalous conditions inci-
dental to “sport” on most of the tracks around
Sydney must inevitably be cleaned up some day, and
those outfits which escape getting hurt are going to
be very lucky. But to see the Government using
the machine to become a financial partner in these
proceedings is enough to drive old-time advocates
of the tote to emetics.
The V.R.C. lias changed the autumn menu
for two-year-olds by moving the items about. Instead
of having three races for young ’uns on the second
day one of the events has been adjourned till the
fourth afternoon, thus having two on each occasion.
The result of this will, of course, be to give more
juveniles opportunity for exertion as even the most
rabid advocate of winding them up early has some
doubts about starting a young horse more than once
per day. So those which couldn’t ■ run more than
once on the second day of the V.R.C. proceedings
will have a chance to hurry up on the fourth day in
a race they might not have joined in if it had tran-
spired earlier. A splendid thing for the Australian
horse to be kept busy as a baby. It gives a lot of
him a chance to get a stern view of the imported
quadruped’s action later on.
“Jaeko”: The mention of the string of
gallopers bred from time to time by the Lees in the
Bathurst district brings to mind the family of The
Gardener’s mare, all of which did well on the course.
The lady got her name through the fact that she was
brought to Woodlands, Tom Lee’s home, by a gardener
who had been in a job at Lithgow. When the
plant-man left he did not regard the mare as being
worth overmuch, and gave her to Lee, who made her
the foundation of his stud. Something more about
the Lees has been told by Arthur Battye, who, in
his 75th year, never tires of recalling the Boer War
exploits. When a youngster, turning ost., he mounted
a champion pony and accompanied Lee on a tour
of the Lachlan stations, starting from Bathurst.
Lee mounted at list, on an imported stallion, ami
made the pace so hot throughout that Battye was
always in tow. Which indicates the work they put
into the neddies those days. Lord Roberts was per-
sonally intissted in the old man during the South
African campaign and invited him to stay with him,
remarking, “Seven of the brave Battyes died in ,
march with me to Kandahar.”
Died recently in South Africa, Edward Sel-
wyn Smith, better known as Ted Smith. An old
King’s School boy and footballer, he played for
N. S. Wales in the ’nineties, and found ins way
over yonder with the first N. S. Wales Contingent
in 1900. After the war lie settled down as a trainer
of millionaires’ horses, winning some good races, on
the Rand and at Durban', where his stable wagjtwell
known. Influenza beat him. Ted was 45 at the
finish—a good fellow and a sound judge of a horse.
* * * *•
“Camelcro”: Don’t judge the racing abili-
ties of the camel by the torpid specimens seen in zoos
and elsewhere. The brutes can travel all right.
When the Imperial Camel Corps was formed in
Egypt most of the camels came from the Soudan.
They were tall, wiry-looltfng beasts, and Billjim
on his horse scratched his head when he first saw
his camelier cobber racing past him as if pursued
by seven devils. Later on, camels were imported
from India, but they did not possess the speed of
the Soudan beast. I was a spectator at a carneliers’
race meeting at El Arish, and the latter animal
beat the heavier but stronger species from India
in every race. Although somewhat afraid of camels
when the Corps was first formed, Billjim soon proved
himself a much superior rider and all-round camel-
man than Arabs and Indians who had ridden the
beasts for years. Remember on one occasion when
we were near the Tripoli border one of our patrols
failed to return to time, and six men with picked
camels covered 40 miles of desert sand in two
hours. It takes an Arab some time to get used
to the hump, and during the first week’s riding an
illness, somewhat similar to seasickness, worried
him ; but it did not trouble Billjim. Our camelier,
with singlet, shorts and minus puttees or leggings,
was a terrifying spectacle as he raced madly across
the desert with water-tanks, cooking-pots and fire-
wood swinging from his livestock.
* * * *
“Fleurbaix” : Talking about these betting
systems. A well-known racing man mysteriously
inquired if I was B——, a mathematician. “Yes,” I
replied. Thereupon he informed me of a system of
making money in bucketsful, bound me over to
secrecy and then handed to me two blue books which
he had obtained after great difficulty. “Study
them,” he said,, “and you will agree with
me that it is wonderful.” I studied them.
It was wonderful. Also, it was pure bosh.
You had to spell each horse’s name phoneti-
cally and to each sound was given a numerical value.
After adding them together you added a value which
you called the “planetary period.” This latter value
was got by observing the planets. If, then, the total
agreed with the fixed value of the planet Mars, or any
ether planet whose birthday was that day, then you
had the winner. The author gave instance after in-
sance of his success. He guaranteed 75 p.c. winners.
I observed that the whole secret lay in the phonetic
spelling. If you didn’t get it one way, then you
spelt it another, and so you had the winner. When
I explained to Ike that it was punk he seemed quite
hurt. Anyhow, he went to the races the following
Saturday, and, using the planetary periods, only got
five winners. Now, how many would he have got if
he had spelt the horses’ names phonetically ?

Seth E’erry, still popularly known as “The
Master,” from having been leader of the Adelaide
Hunt Club many years ago, has been set reminiscing
by the death Ciust cabled from London) of Charles
Wentworth Dilke, son of his better-known politician
father. C.W.D. was a horn sportsman, and when
he came to Australia for “colonial experience,” just
after leaving school, he got busy on the turf at once.
Phil Glenister introduced him to the Adelaide Cup
meeting of 1896 and to Ferry, who then always had
a few jumpers in training. The visitor, being told
that Simulator and Banjo had a good chance for a
coming hurdle and steeple, launched out on the
double, won £12,000 or so on it, and (says Ferry)
presented £4BOO to the owner and stable.

No matter what other sideshows go to
the serapheap, the world’s sculling championship is
likely to survive. Last week Ernest Barry
(.England), who holds the title, sent a reminder to
burly Jim Paddon, of Evans Head (N.S.W.), that,
having got over his wound, he is prepared to defend
his right to the title sometime in 1919. Doubtless
Barry will ask for the event on the Thames; but if
he will shift to Australia for a change there’s a
syndicate prepared to supply the cash. As ages go,
Paddon (J 2) and Barry (37) have yet many years
ahead of them. Paddon holds the Australian cham-
pionship, won from Dick Arnst, in ’l3. Anyway,
there’s a ton of money for the match in Australia.

“Adelovl”: Consider the question, dear
brethren, from a player’s viewpoint. Cricketers can
pay their own expenses of preparation of wickets, after-
noon-teas, beer, etc., and play for the sake of the game
only if they want to. Cricket, as I understand it, is
an outdoor pastime devised for healthy physical
exercise. When a man goes to the wickets lie is
provided with a piece of willow fashioned for hitting
the ball about the landscape. When he deliberately
reverses the idea of the game and merely allows the
ball to hit the bat, the batsman himself and all
others on the field (excepting the bowler) might as
well be playing poker in a pub for all the exercise
they get. Even the scorer gets done out of his job.
Obviously the game does not serve its purpose unless
either runs are being made rapidly or the fieldsmen
kept busy. Writing as a player, give me the man who
keeps the proceedings lively. Then even the umpire
has to be on the qui vive for fear of stopping one.
Even after a hard day’s fielding a man is more
satisfied than after watching a bloke hold a bat for a
trundler to bowl at. “J.Q.” (B. 12/12/’18) praises
the blocker who annoys the bowler. If that is his
idea of sport, I wouldn’t let him play marbles with
my kid. The proper cricketing way to discourage a
bowler is to give cheek by smacking 4’s off him. If
mere annoyance is the object of cricket, why not
heave the bat at him ? Of course, that would be no
more like cricket than the stodgy wav of the blocker
happens to be like it.

“G.N.”: The castigation of “J.Q.” (B.
12/12/’18) for talking through his whiskers was well-
deserved. Eight balls to the over is a move in the
right direction, and a couple more useful moves could
be made by altering the l.b.w. ruling and framing
a precaution to make off-theory bowling an offence.
Nothing in the whole game causes the spectators to
become more sleepy seeing a batsman (taking advan-
tage of the former rule as it applies at present)
jumping in front of the wicket to a leg-break bowler
and playing the ball with his pads. Great somno-
lence also supervenes when watching trundlers wheel
up over after over without the batsmen making any
attempt to play one of them. Such proceedings are
merely tests of patience between bowler and batsman j
also between the two combined and the spectators!
Ihe latter s supply of the commodity necessary for
full enjoyment of these solemn happenings has nearly
run out.
“J.Q.” : Didn’t The Bulletin notice that
I described the love of “wild slogging,” not the love
of smart scoring,” as a display of bad taste? Also,
that 1 did not stigmatise Trumper as of less import-
ance than a stonewaller, but that I reckoned the
latter better than the flukey smiter? Now, public
interest in cricket has waned greatly, but it is wrong
to attribute this to a decline of batting. I
have watched big: cricket in Australia for upwards of
20 years, and have failed to detect any of the
alleged falling-off in batting displays. We' have had
during the past decade a crop of batsmen just as
brilliant as any representative crop of batsmen known
at any other period of the game in Australia. But
there has been a rise of other attractions appealing
more to the mob. Where once the girls decorated
their premises with photos of current cricketing stars,
they now get the decorative effects with pictures of
movie stars. Where Doreen finds diversion on Satur-
ciays there will the sentimental bloke find diversion,
too. It is unfortunate that Doreen’s fancy leads her
no longer to the cricket ground. It is obvious from
this, then, that if “Adelovl’s” scheme (B. 12/12, 18)
is to succeed, it must make sensational hitters of
the players. If it doesn’t do this it fails. 1
have no doubt that a team of C titters would
draw crowds of spectators, but the batting display
given by such a team would not be good cricket.
Trumper gave examples of smart scoring with grace-
ful play. Cotter gave examples of sensational hit-
ting. But was his cricket any more graceful and
scientific than would be the performance of a hippo-
potamus doing the highland fling? The crowd, as a
rule, is no more discriminating in cricket than it is
in literature.
And once more is this page moved to interrupt
“J.Q.” with disapproval of his logic. His growl
seems to be (1) that when the public rushed eagerly
to the cricket match and wrangled at great length
over averages and the “hill” got struck on Jones’s
rockin’ ’em in, it was a discriminating crowd, and
culture was in the ascendant. (2) That when the
crowd wearied about averages and became tired
watching Chunipkin dig himself in for 3i hours, it
was an asinine crowd, dead to the finer things of
life. It’s a queer rigmarole. What in Sheol lias
discrimination on literature got to do with smacking
’em to leg and hoisting Maryann Noble over the
boundary ? It would be as reasonable to accuse the
crowd which refused to flock in thousands to watch a
couple of elderly barristers strolling round the golf-
links with a load of ironmongery with having no
discrimination in music or steak or muffins. Anyway,
cricket is either a game to watch with interest or a
game to play with great intensity. The time has
passed when able-bodied Australians would get
wildly excited over bewildering arithmetic. Of
all the dreary twaddle prepared for human consump-
tion the miles of cricket arithmetic compiled in this
suffering continent takes first prize for futile and
pointless pomposity.

Wilfred Loveridge, drowned unaccount-
ably in the Onkaparinga (S.A.) at a motor-picnic,
was one of a family of brothers who have done good
service for the strong East Torrens crick*t team, of
Adelaide. They have mostly been useful bowlers,
not quite up to inter-State form, but Wilfred lately
made a century and was one of the batch of players
practising for the coming Victorian match.
N. S. Wales bowlers almost heaved their
dilapidated headgear, skywards lately. Four rinks
(captained by J. F. Cox, F. Gartrell, W. Alderson
and Dr. Burge) had tripped to Victoria and brought
back the rubber by winning two games to one.
Still feeling fit, the same captains lined up against
Bananaland last week, but the victims of an awful
climate outed them by two games to one, and kept
a grip on the title they won in days before the world
went to war. In rinks, honors were even ; but the
Bananaliraders had a 'majority in points—3o3 against
280.
Quick changes are going to take place in
the aeroplane records m a day or two. An American
military flier registered new height figures the other
day—2B,9ooft. above sea-level. That means over 51
miles. It must be mighty cold up there. The
furthest previous advance towards the moon stopped
at 20,258 ft. If the next man to fracture the record
goes 8000 ft. better, it may be nearer to drop in to
Mars for refreshments than to bother about coming
home.
A pigeon-fancier tit Katanning (West
Australia), 11. V. Mead, has a black chequer homer
lien which disappeared from its owner’s loft at
Katanning, where it was rearing young, and made a
trip to its old loft at Mount Lawley. The distance,
of this flight, a mere matter of 225 miles, is nothing
to boast about, but the pigeon is now three years
old and had not been out of a loft since it left its
first owner as a “squeaker” six months old, yet it
made a direct flight home although it had a rough
passage anil arrived in poor condition. A journey
like that with such handicaps is a remarkable exhi-
bition of a pigeon’s homing instinct and flying
capabilities, and shows what wonderful birds these
are. There are instances where they have saved
many lives during the war through carrying mes-
sages advising where prompt assistance was required.
P-S. —I’ve seen “sports” getting their amusement by
murdering homers in pigeon-matches, when the
ammunition could have been used to advantage on
rabbits or Germans.

“Pick’' : It is all very well for “Nimrod'’
(B. 27/11 /’ 18) to hee-haw at my-statement that the
6in. bull is sometimes used at 400 yards. To anyone
who cannot be made to believe that a pipe-bowl may
be hit at 100 yards this would naturally appear
improbable. But many clubs, whose members regard
the regulation 20in. bull at that distance as practice
only for kids, use the third-class targets instead.
Before the war, in the annual prize shoot at Central
1 ilba, a continuous match at 500 ya-rds was always
on the programme, in which the 6in. bull was used.
Incidentally, let me explain that spread of shots from
a fixed rifle is due, not to the mechanism—which, in
theory, should throw every bullet alike—but to barrel
‘Ouuhg and variations of atmosphere. The mug fires
all his shots without sight adjustments, and spreads
his hits all over the plate. The expert, after every
shot, makes allowances for fouling, expansion and
atmospheric pressure, and thus beats the rifle itself
ip grouping hits. The main thing is to become reallv
expert. Then you can do things which people who
are not experts consider impossible.

Y Adelaide University lately had two addi-
tions to its endowments. (It has always
been fortunate in this respect.) The sum of
£5OO has been presented by Mrs. A. M.
Sufipson to found a library of aeronautics
in memory of her late husband, and Peter
Waite has endowed the institution with
SSBO shares in Elder, Smith and Co., worth
a;- the present date over £60,000. which is
in addition to his magnificent gifts of his
Urrbrae estate and adjacent lands for the
study of agriculture and allied subjects.
These benefactions of Peter Waite’s will be
of immense benefit to S. Aus, in years to
come, and entitles him to share with his old
partner, the late Thomas Elder, the honor
of being one of the two greatest patrons of
education the State has had.

"THE CHILDRENS FOOD.”
Peanut”: The patriotic scheme was in
full blast, and the large and prominent citizen
on the platform was giving of his windy
best. He called upon every citizen in that
great crowd to make sacrifices as he had
done for our. brave boys. As I was admiring
the freshness of his style, I found a returned
man in “civvies” at my elbow. “Hear him,”
he said wearily. “It sounds well, doesn’t it?
Makes people glad that the poor dear sol-
diers have such nice, generous friends. Well.
I’ll tell you what he did for me. When I
got my discharge I put my little bit into a
car, and started in to run a passenger trip.
I used to charge 30 shillings. It was a 25-
shilling run before the big rise in petrol,
but 30 shillings was only a fair paying
thing, and the accepted figure when I
started in. I had hopes <j>f picking up a
living, for people will always patronise a
returned man while he gives them as much
or a bit more than the other fellow. Then
this big brother of Billjim started to run
me off. Ran 'his cars at £1 the trip. It
wasn’t paying him, of course. lie did it
at a dpad loss; but he hadn’t heen working
for five shillings a da„v for three years past,
as I had, and lie could stand it. ’ I couldn’t
get a fare at my old price, and I couldn’t
keep body and soul together at his. So I
had to sell out and look for a job. Then
when he d choked me oil lit* tmt his price up
to 30 shillings again. But lie’s a good
sprinker—l will say that for him.”

As Good as a Holiday.
The main object of a holiday is to gain
renewed energy for another year’s work, so
that one may return with sound nerves and
a clear brain, full of vigor and ready for
work again. Unfortunately that object is
not always attained.
It till depends on the state of your blood.
If your blood has become impoverished or
impure the holiday is probably too brief for
you to obtain proper benefit; and very few
people can claim to be free from the dregs
of some ailment which lias drained the blood
of its natural strength. That is why they
feel fugged out. brain-weary and incapable
Of much exertion.
If you cannot take a holiday, begin now
to build up your blood with a true tonic,
and so invigorate and refresh your system.
Nothing is more valuable for this purpose
than Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills. Over and ovc
again they have been proved to be as good
as a holiday to jaded men and women. Their
value to both sexes is made evident by the
fact that these pills make good the waste
caused by illness and the wear and tear of
life.
Do not put off finding relief a single day.
Get a box of Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills to-dio
and begin the treatment at once. Price 2/9
per box, six boxes 15/3.
Free.—A useful booklet, “Diseases of the
Blood,” will be sent on application to Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Co., Box 594 G.P.O.
Sydney.-— Advt.

New Hupmobile car.
THE COMFORT CAR.
IN building the Hupmobile the builders have ever in mind the driver’s point of view. Hup-
mobile owners are proud of the appearance, comfort, performance, sturdiness and economy
of operation of their cars. Ask the first Hupmobile owner you see about this.
Specifications.
BODY TYPES—Five-passenger, two- or three-passenger single seater.
WHEELBASE—II 2 inches.
CYLINDERS —Four, east, e.n bloc, removable head —82 mm. bore x 140 mm. stroke.
STARTING —Two unit system Automatic release.
IGNITION—Dixie high-tension magneto.
CARBURETTER —Special Strcmberg, with two adjustments.
PETROL SYSTEM —Stewart-Warner vacuum feed. 13 Imperial gallon tank —two gallon
reserve.
LUBRICATION —Pressure system. Gear pump driven from cam shaft.
COOLING —Thermo-syphon, extra radiator capacity.
CLUTCH—Dry-disc type. Seven steel plates faced with asbestos fabric. Fully housed.
REAR AXLE—Floating type. Spiral bevel gears.
TYRES—BIS x 105 mm. clincher type. Plain in front, all-weather tread on rear.
UPHOLSTERY —Genuine leather, stuffed with real curled hair.
TOP—Neverleek, khaki.
Rain-vision windshield; head, tail and instrument-board lights; petrol gauge; motor-
driven horn; ammeter; foot and robe rails; tyre carrier; extra rim; side curtains;
tonneau carpet; hood cover; pump, jack, full set of tools and tyre-repair outfit.

The Untamable.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HE was a beautiful horse, without fault
or blemish, as all that had got c ose
enough to see him admitted—a
thoroughbred that had got away with the
brumbies when a yearling—a big blacK
animal standing sixteen hands in height. \\ ith
a notable pedigree and the racing blood of his
sire and dam hot in his veins, lie had b.d 101
a free, unfettered life among the hills. He
was lord of the mob he had fought for and
won from the brumby stallion. A despeiate
fight, a struggle for victory before which the
mares grouped in fear and wonder. Sudden y
the black thoroughbred, with Ins velvet eoat
and tine silken mane bearing marks ot the
battle, reared back, leaving his opponent with
a great ugly tear in his throat, through which
his life-blood was rushing out. Wheeling
the mob, the thoroughbred drove them before
him, his ears laid back and head low on the
ground. He made a beautiful picture. Ihus
he lived for three years in the shelter ot
the hills with his harem. , ,
One day the mob, with the blood-horse
galloping in the lead, was seen by the station-
hands, and, on their excited report, they
were told to get the runaway. Many times
they “ran” him, starting out at dawn to
come on the mob feeding in the dew-wet grass
on the plains. But, fleet-footed as the deer,
the black would lead the mares to the scrub
and then to the hills. .
The black horse never seemed tired
after these flights. He would trot
round the mob, in a restless manner, with
quivering nostrils expanded—always on the
alert. The disappointed men would turn to
look at him far away scaling the rocky
heights or gazing out on a panorama of
purple hills and green, waving plains, that
seemed to stretch west and for ever west to
the dipping sun —standing as though all he
surveyed were his by the law of nature.
Finding that the mob could not be
taken by running them, the station-hands re-
solved to build a trap-yard around the
spring at which the brumbies watered. Soon
a tail strong yard was built, with wide-
swinging gates and late one evening the
men were to be seen sitting on limbs of high
trees waiting for the horses to come for
their drink. A great round moon shone from
a clear sky, illuming the thickly-wooded
bush. Kangaroos—blue, red, and grey—came
in mobs, followed by the timid rock-wallaby,
but these bounded off in fright at seeing the
water surrounded by logs. Dingoes emerged
from the scrub, trotting along the dusty
cattle-pads, to suddenly stand and sniff the
air tainted with the presence of man. And
they would disappear, and a weird, dismal
howl would be taken up by others and
answered far away in the distance.
At last, when all was still, the brumbies
came to water. Through the wide gates they
passed, led by their king. As they took their
fill, wading out into the cool liquid, splash- ,
ing it up with their fore-feet, they heard a j
noise behind them that caused them to rush j
up the bank terrified, with wildly-beating
hearts. What they heard was the gates
crashing to. And now they found a great
log fence surrounding them —a fence fully
seven feet in height. As they rushed about
madly to escape, the men came eagerly for-
ward to see the thoroughbred that had so
long baffled them. In that moonlit yard the
lustre of his fine black coat shone like satin.
He turned about and faced his enemies,
whistling defiantly as if daring them to
approach. The men were struck dumb with
amazement, wondering if his limbs worked
with electricity. As they gazed upon him he
raced at the fence and cleared it in his leap
as a bird might fly.
That night he wandered far away from
where •he had lost his mob. Two days
passed and no water did he find. It was on
the third day that the hunters ran him again,
and noticed a great difference in him. He
was gaunt and hollow ; his sides were drawn
up; but, with fire flying from the flint, he
beat them again to the ranges. When the
pangs of thirst drove him down in search of
water, he saw something far off shimmering
in the heat. It was a boundary-rider’s hut,
and there he smelt water. He discovered
it first in a well below his reach, but he
traced it a!6o to a tank, where nectar was
slowly dripping from its tap. With the mad-
ness of his great thirst he pawed and struck
at the tank, lashed it with his heels again
and again until a stream of water rushed
from its torn side.
He travelled back in a few days to where
he had left the mares—a lonely and desolate
object, as dark as the moonless night on
which he came forth in search of his mates.
He called for them, whinnying loudly and
listening with ears pricked to hear the
answer that would never come again to
him, for they were safe in the homestead
paddock.
All night he wandered about restlessly, and
all the following day he haunted the scrub
and scanned the plains. That night
big drops of rain fell, wetting the
parched earth. With the first twitter-
ing call of the birds at dawn a band
of horsemen came on him drinking from
a rock well. Again he fled, and they, with
the ironstone ringing under their horses’
hoofs, followed at break-neck pace. They
rushed him to the brink of a cliff that had a
sheer fall of one hundred feet, and here he
halted just in time. For him there was -no
escape now. The relentless men were be-
hind, and before him the cliff. He stood
trembling in every limb. A lather of sweat
broke out on him. A man approached, speak-
ing to him, trying to soothe his fear. In his
hand he held the coils of a long rope that
he was about to swing in the air to curl and
ring down over the beautiful animal’s head.
But like an outlaw that would not be taken
that untamable spirit seemed to lift the body
back from human touch. For a moment he
poised on the rim of the precipice, and then
' fell with a crash below. The stockmen,
climbing down, expected to see him smashed
to pieces, but he was not dead. He lay
there with his legs , nd back broken,' biting
the earth and stones in his agony.
They cut a vein in the thoroughbred’s
neck, and with light still flashing in his
eyes the wild thing’s life went out.
A. A. C.

Rexona for Surfers
Beautiful Miss Beryl Ferguson is one of
the brightest members of the J. C. Wil-
liamson Co., Ltd., and is a favorite with
all theatre-goers. Miss Ferguson loves
surfing, and writes us the following letter
in praise of Rexona:
I want you to know that I am a Rexona
Surfing Girl. I simply love surfing, and
am so fond of it that I often go in when
the sun is strongest, but I never have any
bad effects from surfing, for I always use
Rexona before going in. I rub some on my
face and hands, and I never get sunburnt
nor my skin affected in any way. After
my surf is over I always have a cake of
Rexona Soap, which I use in the shower,
on my hair and skin, and I find by
the use of Rexona and Rexona
Soap any girl can keep herself
auite as fresh and dainty when
surfing as she could by missing all
this fun and always thinking of
her complexion.
Amongst my girl friends I find
more and more of them using
Rexona every day, and I am glad
to write and tell you what excel-
lent things Rexona and Rexona
Soap are for surfers.
Delightedly yours,
(Sgd.) BERYL FERGUSON.

The Cockney Soul.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FROM Woolwich and Brentford and
Stamford Hill, from Richmond into
the Strand,
Oh, the Cockney soul is a silent soul as 1
is in every land ! ,
But out on the sand with a broken band it s
sarcasm spurs them through;
And, with never a laugh, in a gale and a
half, ’tis the Cockney cheers the crew.
Oh, send them a tune from the music-halls
with a chorus to shake the sky .
Oh, give them a deep-sea chanty now and a
star to steer them by !
Now this a song of the great untrained, a
song of the Unprepared,
Who had never the brains to plead unfit, or
think of the things they dared ;
Of the grocer-souled and the draper-souled,
and the clerks of the four o’clock,
Who stood for London and died for home in
the nineteen-fourteen shock.
Oh, this is a pork-shop warrior's chant
come back from it, maimed and blind,
To a little old counter in Grey’s Inn-road
and a tiny parlor behind ;
And the bedroom above, where the wife and
he go silently mourning yet
For a son-in-law who shall never come back
and a dead son's room “To Let.”
(But they have a boy “in the fried-fish line”
in a shop across the “wye,”
Who will take them “aht” and “abalit” to-
night and cheer their old eyes dry.)
And this is a song of the draper's clerk
(what have you all to say?)
He’d a tall top-hat and a walking-coat in the
city every day—•
He wears no flesh on his broken bones that
lie in the shell-churned loam ;
For he went over the top and struck with his
cheating yard-wand—home.
(Oh, touch your hat to the tailor-made
before you are aware,
And lilt us a lay of Bank-holiday and the
lights of Leicester-square!)
Hats off to the dowager lady at home in her
house in Itussell-square!
Like the pork-shop back and the Brixton flat,
they are silently mourning there ;
For one lay out ahead of the rest in the
slush ’neath a darkening sky,
With the blood of a hundred earls congealed
and his eye-glass to his eye.
(He gave me a cheque in an envelope on a
distant gloomy day;
He gave me his hand at the mansion door
and he said: “Good-luck! Good-bai!”}
N.s.w.
Henry Lawson.
The Commandant’s Lady.
[ FOR THE BULLETIN.]
«"1I dear Sophronia,” said the Com-
jWl mandant, in a voice that vibrated
between annoyance and apprehen-
sion. “I beg you to remember that Wallis’
Plains are not London.”
His gaze wandered through the open
French window of his office out across the
green levels that stretched beyond the river
to intensely blue ranges of mountains slum-
bering in the afternoon heat-haze of a sum-
mer day.
“You are not, my dear, in London,” he
repeated, and the tall lady, gloved and coal-
scuttle bonneted, who was seated on the plain
wooden chair beside his table, found the re-
mark so irritating as to cause her to bring
her clenched hand down upon the table with
a little emphatic bang, and to look at her
husband with an expression of stifled fury
that made her pretty pink and white face
hard and angry. Her dark eyes gleamed
malevolently out of the arching frame of the
ugly head-dress tied with a bow of pink rib-
bons under a shapely and determined little
chin. She was a tall young woman, well
figured and well dressed. Dark auburn ring-
lets strayed from under the “coal-scuttle,”
and the coolness of her muslin contrasted
with the heat of her expression.
“Damn!” she said, as though she spat at
him, “You are a fool, Anthony.”
Captain Plaskett’s frown deepened.
“No tantrums, I beg of you. No tantrums
now.”
For a moment she was silent. Then she
broke out into a quick torrent of speech.
“I want to go to Newcastle. I must go to
Newcastle. Why do you sit there talking
silly nonsense about this place not being Lon-
don? I wish to God it was London. You
wouldn’t dare flout me like this if I were
near my family. Not London, indeed. Why
do you say that?”
“.Simply, my dear,” he said mildly, smiling
a little, “because you appear to be under an
impression that you can have your own way
here, as you have been used to having it at
home. You wish to go to Newcastle because
Smail brings up word that the Government
schooner is in from Sydney and there’s a box
from England for you. The box will arrive
here in due course. You’ll see all your finery
in good time. I cannot go with you myself
and cannot spare an escort. The woods be-
tween here and the sea are infested with run-
away prisoners. I cannot send you in the
boat, because I am sending it up the Paterson
to-night—there has been a murder on the
river.”
“Wliat do I care for your disgusting mur-
ders? The murder will keep. I want to go
to Newcastle. Will you give me the boat?”
“No, my love. I”
“Damn!” she said again. She stood up
with a withering look at him, picked up her
shawl from the table and sailed out of the
room.
Captain Plaskett sighed. He had be-
come bitterly aware of the foolish situation
of a poor officer who has a wife who ought
to be rich. With her beside him there was
little chance of forgetting it. He was too
jealous of her to allow her a separate estab-
lishment in Sydney, even if he could afford
it, which w T as doubtful. She was a lady who
took every opportunity of making her hus-
band jealous. Fortunately, up here she had
few chances. But if she was even gracious
to a prisoner the Commandant could find
something suspicious in her graciousness.
While the Commandant was deep in these
distressing reflections a shadow darkened the
open doorway, and he was aroused from them
by a rasping voice which suddenly exclaimed :
' “Sir.”
Captain Plaskett looked up and beheld the
erect and soldierly form of “Drumstick”
Smith, his efficient, automatic and entirely
dependable head-constable.
“Yes; what is it, Smith?” he asked, a little
wearily.
“We've got him, y'r Honor. Got him in
th’ swamps.”
“Who? Who've you got?”
“Cartwright, y’r Honor. Me men came ou
him asleep be til’ big swamp early this morn-
ing. He’s here now.”
The Commandant immediately manifested
a renewed interest in the affairs of his office,
lie sat up in his chair and was almost enthu-
siastic in the welcome he accorded the head-
constable’s announcement.
“Good. Oh, very good. Oh, excellent.
Bring him in at once, Smith. We’ll deal
with the scoundrel immediately. Let me
see—how many times has he absconded,
Smith?”
“ ’Tis th’ fourth time, y’r Honor. A habit
he’s makin’ of it. This time, as y’r Honor
knows, he beat Clancy, th’ overseer of his
gang, black and blue.”
“Yes. by Gad. A violent ruffian. We’ll
steady him this time. Bring him in, Smith.
Bring him in.”
Handcuffed, and between two constables,
the absconder was ushered into the room,
lie was a splendid type of young man —tall,
athletic, not bad looking. His sunburnt face
had a straggling beard. His dilapidated
clothing, old and torn and ragged, could not
take off anything from his appearance. He
looked at the great man who was the arbiter
of his destiny with a hard and defiant blue
eye.
“Ah!” said that gentleman, slowly and
with an air of enjoyment; “so you’ve come
back to us, Cartwright, eh? Well, you’re very
welcome, I’m sure. What have you to say?”
The manacled captive said nothing. He
merely stared at the Commandant with a |
sort of stoical indifference. This roused the
ire of “Drumstick” Smith. He jolted the I
prisoner in the ribs with a “half-arm.”
“Find y’r tongue, me man. His Honor’s
speakin’ to ye.”
The prisoner made no acknowledgment of
this attention beyond a swift glance at the
head-constable. He continued to stare stonily
at the Commandant.
“Insolent, eh?” said that gentleman, tak-
ing up a pen and scribbling on a sheet of
paper in front of him. “Insolent. We’ll
charge him with insolence, Smith. A contu-
macious villain.”
“Yessir,” said Mr. Smith.
“You are charged with absconding, Cart-
wright. Tbe fourth time. It’s growing on
you, this habit of scuttling into the bush.
You are also charged with assaulting Over-
seer Clancy. And insolence. That’s three
charges. Did he take anything with him,
Smith ?”
“Took his rations, sir. Bread and meat.”
“Ah; stealing Government property. Four.
Ouite a little list, Cartwright, my man. It
all adds up, you know. Er—was he quite
sober when he assaulted the overseer,
Smith ?”
“No, sir,” the head-constable lied readily.
“Drunk, sir. Fighting drunk.”
“Oh, that’s five. Five separate charges,
my man. Drunkenness, assault, larceny, ab-
sconding and insolence. Well, well. Nothing
to say? Pleads guilty, evidently. Then it
only remains to apportion the punishment,
which, of course, must be deterrent. H’m.”
The Commandant paused to consider his
sentence. In the momentary silence the pris-
oner raised his manacled hands and wiped his
face with a rag he clenched in one of them.
One of the constables snatched the rag
from the prisoner and held it up to the Com-
mandant’s inquiring gaze. It was a little,
laced, woman’s handkerchief, very soiled.
The Commandant started when he saw what
it was. He dropped his pen with a sudden
oath.
“Damme!” he said. “Let me see it.”
It was handed to him. He spread it out
on the table. A black scowl came over his
face as he recognised a handkerchief of his
wife’s. There was a large embroidered “S”
in a corner.
“Where did you get this?”
No answer.
“By the Lord, I’ll make you speak, my
man. Further insolence, you dog.”
He took up his pen again and wrote for
half a minute or more. The man said no-
thing. Presently the Commandant addressed
the head-constable.
“Listen, Smith. For drunkenness he’ll have
twenty-five. For stealing, fifty. For assault,
one hundred. For absconding, one hundred.
For one charge of insolence, twenty-five,
ior the second one he’ll have another
twenty-five—and as many more as will make
him say where he got this property of my
wife s. In the morning. Lock him up.”
As the constables passed out on to the
verandah with their prisoner he called to
their chief.
‘ Smith a moment. Throw three or four
buckets of water over the floor of the lockup
before you put him in. Ha-ha. It may want
cleaning, you know. We’ll do him out of liis
night s rest, at any rate. And no rations,

Smith; not even water. I'll cure this clog.

T t 7 G ,?- vernm ®nt headquarters in the district of
Wallis’ Plains were situated, in 1821, on the
rising ground where now stands the town of
Past Maitland, not far from the position of
the present court-house and gaol. An exten-
sive and beautiful view spreads out to the
north and to the west. A century ago the
place was almost the limit of established
authority, and the outlook over which Cap-
tain Plaskett paused for a moment to feast
his eyes, as he walked down to the lockup,
bore little evidence of tbe occupation of the
white man. The bark roof of an infrequent
farm-house on the river-flats, and the rising
smoke from clearing operations in the distant
timbered country toward the hills, was all
that was to be seen of it, besides an occa-
sional log-fenced square of cultivation greener
than the plains.
It was nearly eight o’clock, on a glorious
blue morning, and the world was full of sun-
shine. That was more than could be said of
the Commandant’s mental processes. Mrs.
I laskett was in the sullen stage. She had
locked her bedroom door last night. She had
made no appearance at breakfast. He had
had no opportunity of questioning her about
her handkerchief. So he was more than sav-
agely determined to extract some information
on the subject from the convict Cartwright.
It was characteristic of Anthony Plaskett
that lie could even suspect his beautiful wife
m. intrigue with a prisoner. Why not? lie
had himself carried on such with female pris-
oners, both before and after his marriage.
They already had the prisoner spread out
on the triangle when the Commandant
entered the lockup yard.
Why the Hell don't you get on with it?”
lie heard the man say as he came into the
place.
Dr. Bailey was walking up and down, with
ins hands behind his back, smoking a cigar.
De nodded “Good morning.”
Cartwright was a magnificent man. As
lie stood with legs spread out and arms ex-
tended—crucified upon the framework of
flagellation—his broad shoulders white
below the line of sunburn about his neck,
his swelling forearms brown like old leather,
the great muscles of his back tense and
strong beneath the skin that was scored with
previous castigations—the very grandeur of
lus manhood might have appealed against
this defilement of it. But there was no
jl o li a thing in the minds of any
°- those who witnessed his martyrdom that
morning. A flogging was a commonplace in
New South Wales in 1821. Its most hideous
outrages were only “necessary corporal pun-
ishment.” Its results were neither under-
stood nor considered.
Is lrn all right, Bailey?” inquired the
Commandant perfunctorily of the medical
officer.
w u f e , s : foil Id stand a thousand.
Wish I had his constitution.”
“Very well. You may begin, Cassidy.”
One of the. flagella tors, a burly, brutal-
'ookuisr fellow, stooped forward, the cat in
his right hand. lie placed himself to the
It/-*' illK * ;l behind, the prisoner.
With his heel he ground a hole in the earth
of the yard. This was to be the pivotal
point of his exertions.
~ ’A 1 minits, y’r Honor?” he inquired of
Captain Plaskett.
Yes. Spin it out. I want to make the
brute talk. See here, Cartwright, my man
"A 11 sa y where you got that handker-
chief?”
to grunted the prisoner.
All right, Cassidy,” said Plaskett. “He’ll
speak up before we finish with him.”
Cassidy raised his right arm, and drew
back his right foot, swinging on his left heel,
the tails of the cat hanging behind his
shoulder. Suddenly, with a hiss of his
breath, he spun round rapidly and forcibly
bringing the lashes with a swishing blow
across liis victim’s back.
“One,” counted the chief-constable.
Mr. Cassidy dropped his hand and rested
while thirty seconds went by. Then he
struck again. The pause was a devilish
device by which the agony of the scourging
was prolonged. Cartwright’s 300 would be
spread over two and a half hours.
It was a cruel business, a hideous night-
mare of a business, but to no one in the
yard—not even the victim of it—was it any-
thing but ordinary. Nearly every day they
saw it, or did it, or endured it. Three times
had it happened to Cartwright before. When
Cassidy ivas relieved by bis understudy the
man’s sback was like a piece of raw beef
Throughout the terrible ordeal Cartwright
made no murmur, though sometimes his head
sank down between his shoulders and his
weight hung on his lashed wrists through a
few strokes of the cat. After one of these
lapses ,he would pull himself together and
turn his head to look at the Commandant,
lie grinned horribly always by the twitching
contraction of his facial muscles.
T^ e 1 / 1 ? inutes lengthened into half-hours,
the half-hours went on into the hours. Dr.
Bailey offered the Commandant a cigar
which he took and lit. The flies were be-
coming a nuisance, and smoking kept them
otf.
“Drumstick” Smith counted out:
Ihree liun’red.”
They went to cast the fainting man loose
and two constables stood by to support him,’
one holding his tattered shirt ready to throw
over the blood-dripping torso, which already
the flies swarmed about, unhindered bv the
swishing of the cat.
“Hold on a minute.” said Captain Plas-
kett. “He hasn’t spoken yet. What about
that handkerchief, Cartwright? Will you
say how you came by it? I’ll flog you till
you do. Come, speak up. We can’t stav
here a}l day.” J
. No answer; nothing but a feeble, defiant
jerk of the chin indicated that the terribly-
sufFering man had heard.
“Go on, Cassidy,” commanded the Com-
mandant.
But the doctor interposed. With his
fingers on the man’s pulse, he shook his
head.
. “No, no, sir. He’s had enough this morn-
ing. Another time. He’s all but played-
out. Wonderful endurance,” he muttered
“Never saw anything like it. Not a whim-
per. Heart of 'a lion.”
“Nonsenser Doctor,” angrily snorted Plas-
kett. ‘A few more won’t hurt him. He’ll
tel] in a few minutes —after this spell. Go
on. flogger.”
But the doctor was peremptory.
“No, sir. He’s nearly clone. Recollect
young Kermody. I’ll not be answerable if
you__ give him another lash.”
Kermody was an ill-conditioned young
gentleman who had died on the triangles
six months before, and the mere mention of
him was unpleasant to the Commandant.
He had prided himself that he knew exactly
when to stop flogging, and Kermody’s inso-
lence had made a fool of him.
“Oh, very well, then,” he agreed with an
ill grace. “Cast him off, Smith. But you’ll
have it later, you swine,” he growled to the
almost unconscious man.
They untied his ankles and wrists and
supported him under the shoulders. He took
an uncertain step forward —and fell down
dead,
“Oh, damn,” said the Commandant when
the doctor gave his verdict. “I’ll never
know now, blast his soul.”
* * * *
Captain Anthony Plaskett went out of
that comfortable hostelry in George-street,
the Australian Inn, after dark had fallen
upon Sydney, and bent his steps towards
the southern end of Castlereagh-street, to-
wards a house of considerable ill-fame on
the outskirts of the town. He came to the
garden gate of Piccadilly Lizzie’s well-known
establishment just as the nine o’clock gun
boomed out, faintly and far away, from Fort
Macquarie.
Officially and domestically the affairs of
Captain Plaskett were in dismal jeopardy.
He had been summoned' to Sydney by the
new Governor, Sir Thomas Brisbane, to
make explanation of the unpleasant fact
that, for tlie second time, a prisoner had
died under punishment during his term of
office as Commandant at Wallis’ Plains.
The matter of Cartwright might easily
have been hushed up, as young Kermody’s
death under the lash had been, were it not
lor the very disagreeable reason that Mrs.
Plaskett had run away to Sydney and her-
self seen the Governor about it. This act
ot. unwifely treachery had been followed by
a most terrible, amazing and harrowing per-
formance on Mrs. Plaskett’s part. Incred-
ible as it may seem, she, a lady by birth,
education and the position of his wife, had
east herself utterly away. She had not run
oil with some lover, but had actually become
an inmate of the bagnio conducted by that
abandoned but business-like creature, Picca-
dilly Lizzie.
Piccadilly Lizzie’s neat cottage was situ-
ated just opposite to the gallows which for
some time occupied, about those years, a
position where the Barley Mow hotel stands
to-day a three-legged structure with a
triangular set of beams.
That somewhat faded siren, Lizzie Baker,
w;is not unknown to Captain Plaskett. She
called him “dearie,” after the time-honored
fashion ot her sort, invited him to take
refreshment at his own exorbitant expense,
a h. lrn whether she’d call the girls in.
No,” said Plaskett. “I only want one
ot them. Have you one who calls herself
Sophroma, by any chance?”
“Oh, Sophy—yes, dearie. A real swell—
like I useter be. Ye’ll like her well, I pro-
mise ye. .Test a minit; I’ll go fetch her.”
Captain Plaskett shuddered.
When the door opened and she came in
clad in a loose and gaudily-flowered satin
wrapper, with her magnificent hair half un-
bound and loose over her shoulder, he rose
to his feet and took an angry step forward
as though he meant to strike her. Instantly
she produced a horse-pistol from behind her
back and covered him with it.
“Sit'down, Anthony. Sit over there,” she
commanded. 1 lie cold hatred in her magni-
ficent eyes frightened him.
“I’ll kill you if you move,” she said
quietly.
Captain Plaskett gripped the arms of the
chair, his face white and scared. “In God’s
name, what duos this moan? You ” he
gasped.
"Mean? What does it mean, ’Tony. Why
it means I’m what you just said. That’s
what it means. A pretty scandal, isn’t it?”
... Hard and terrible was the way she said
this, there was tragedy and hatred in her
every aspect. She leaned forward across
the table and pointed at him, laughing in a
half-mad way that was yet horridly sane and
earnest.
,“H?-. ha ; ha! The bold autocrat of Wal-
! 1S -The brave fellow who flogs men
to death I lour reputation up there will be
a fine one, won’t it? Listen, you brute.
I ve hated you ever since mamma made me
marry you. I’ve hated yon m o>-e ppd more
since I’ve been with you in New South Wales^
U ve longed for some way to hurt you, and
by God, Ive found it. But I’d not have
done this but for one thing. You killed my
bi other Charley. Oh, yes, the man you
logged to death —Cartwright—was my
brother Charley, who ran away to sea when
he was a boy. I found it out by chance,
and 1 tried to help him escape. There you
have it, you murderer. And you’re
going to do another murder.”
She rose to her feet, the pistol in her right
hand, and came slowly round the table. The
glitter of her eyes fascinated the cowering
man in the chair, paralysed him.
A moment she stood before him. Then
the wretched man screamed with pain as
she struck him across the face with the
heavy hexagonal barrel of the pistol. She
struck him again. He could not see. but the
sudden pain restored his energies. He sprang
to his feet as she fired the weapon into her
own breast.
. Half a minute afterwards Piccadilly Lizzie,
in the open doorway with one of the girls of
the house, saw Captain Plaskett standing
above the huddled body of the woman Sophy,
a pistol in his hand. It was his own
weapon with his initials inlaid on the butt.
Chat was they story at the trial.
The Court held that there was no possible
doubt about it. But there had been provo-
cation. A man might naturally kill his wife
rather than leave her to such a life as Mrs.
I laskett had adopted. So. for the next five
years, until they buried him in the little
cemetery at Norfolk Island, behind Emilv
Bay, Anthony Plaskett studied the “System”
he had administered at Wallis’ Plains, and
found it truly amazing.
J. H. M. ABBOTT.
